Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Ninian Martin Stephen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of
the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight Commander of the Most Excellent Order of the British Empire, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of
the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: FIFTH PERIOD)
Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 140 of 1982)An Act to amend the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 consequent upon the enactment of the Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982.
Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 70 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Act 1963.
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 77 of 1982)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Air Navigation Legislation (Validation and Interpretation) Act 1982 (Act No. 95 of 1982)An Act to make provision in relation to the exercise of certain powers and the performance of certain functions under regulations and instruments under the Air Navigation Act 1920 and for related purposes.
Appropriation Act (No. I) 1982-83 (Act No. 1160Â£]982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1982-83 (Act No. 117 of 1982)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 (Act No. 118 of 1982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service, in relation to the Parliamentary Departments, of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Asian Development Fund Act 1982 (Act No. lSI of 1982)An Act to authorize a further contribution by Australia to the Asian Development Bank for the purposes of the Asian Development Fund.
Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Act 1982 (Act No. 133 of 1982)An Act to establish a Trust Fund for the purpose of the grant of financial assistance to the States and to the Northern Territory for expenditure on upgrading Australian roads in celebration of the Bicentenary in 1988, and for related purposes.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 126 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 125 of 1982)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 124 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981.
Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 127 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Bank Account Debits Tax Act 1982 (Act No. 141 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks.
Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982 (Act No. 142 of 1982)An Act relating to the collection of a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Berry Fruits) Act 1982 (Act No. 132 of 1982)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain berry fruits.
Bounty (Ships) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 145 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Bounty (Ships) Act 1980.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 154 of 1982)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. ISS of 1982)- An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964.
Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 71 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Act 1959.
Conciliation and Arbitration (Management of Organizations) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 143 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.
Cotton Levy Act 1982 (Act No. 147 of 1982)An Act to impose a levy upon certain cotton produced in Australia.
Cotton Research Act 1982 (Act No. 146 of 1982)An Act to provide for the collection of levy imposed by the Cotton Levy Act 1982 and to establish a Cotton Research Trust Account.
Customs and Excise Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 81 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Excise Act 1901.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Customs Securities (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 137 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 in relation to certain Customs securities, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff Act 1982 (Act No. 113 of 1982)- An Act relating to duties of Customs.
Customs Tariff (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1982 (Act No. 115 of 1982)An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Customs Tariff Act 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 114 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 consequent upon the enactment of the Customs TariffAct 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 136 of 1982)An Act to repeal section 140fthe Customs Tariff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment (Countervailing Duties) Act 1982 (Act No. 68 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 in relation to countervailing duties.
Defence Force (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1982 (Act No. 153 of 1982)An Act to amend certain Acts, and to enact transitional provisions, consequent upon the enactment of the Defence Force Discipline Act 1982, and for other purposes.
Defence Force Discipline Act 1982 (Act No. 1520f 1982)An Act relating to the discipline of the Defence Force and for related purposes.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 109 of 1982)- An Actto amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.1) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. II00f 1982)- An Act to amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.2) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 108 of 1982)- An Act relating to taxes on diesel fuel.
Dried Fruits Export Charges Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. l440f 1982)- An Act to amend the Dried Fruits Export Charges Act 1924.
Drought Assistance (Primary Producers) Act 1982 (Act No. 99 of 1982)An Act to make provison for the grant of financial assistance to primary producers who are affected by drought.
Export Market Development Grants Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 157 of 1982)An Act to amend the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974, and for related purposes.
Fertilizers (Subsidy) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 69 of 1982)An Act to amend section 3 of the Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Act 1966 and to amend the Phosphate Fertilizers Bounty Act 1963.
Health Legislation Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 1120f 1982)An Act to amend the Health Insurance Act 1973, the National Health Act 1953 and the Health Insurance Commission Act 1973, and for related purposes.
Income Tax (Companies, Corporate Unit Trusts and Superannuation Funds) Act 1982 (Act No. 102 of 1982)- An Act to impose a tax upon incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Individuals) Act 1982 (Act No. 101 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax upon incomes, other than incomes of companies, of corporate unit trusts and of superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Rates) Act 1982 (Act No. 105 of 1982)An Act to declare the rates of income tax payable upon incomes other than incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 103 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Act 1979.
Income Tax (Rates) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 1040f 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Rates) AC11976.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.4) 1982 (Act No. 76 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.5) 1982 (Act No. 106 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.6) 1982 (Act No. 123 of 1982)- An Act relating to income tax.
Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 97 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Act 1980.
Live-stock Export Charge Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 83 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-slock Export Charge ACI 1977.

Northern Territory (Self-Government) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 130 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Northern Territory (Self-Government) Act 1978.

Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 78 of 1982)- An Act relating to certain remuneration and allowances.
Repatriation Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 100 of 1982)- An Act relating to repatriation and related matters.
Royal Commissions Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 139 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Royal Commissions Act 1902.
Salaries and Wages Pause Act 1982 (Act No. 134 of 1982)An Act to suspend for 12 months the powers of certain authorities in relation to the remuneration of certain persons, and for certain other purposes.

INCORPORATION OF MATERIAL IN HANSARD
Mr SPEAKER-This statement arises out of increasing numbers of requests by members for the incorporation of unspoken material in Hansard. On 10 May 1978 in a statement to the House I said:
I do not encourage the incorporation of anything into the Hansard record other than things such as tables which need to be seen in visual form for comprehension.

Underlying my attitude then, and of the House since Federation, is the aim of keeping Hansard as a true record of what is said in the House. Recently there has been a tendency for this commendable aim to be broken down. Other significant factors against excessive incorporation of unspoken material into Hansard are:
A member's speech is lengthened beyond his entitlement under the Standing Orders.
The incorporated material may contain irrelevant or defamatory matter or unparliamentary language.
Other honourable members will not be aware of the contents of the material until production of the daily Hansard next morning when a speech may be discovered to have matter not answered in debate and so appear more authoritative. Similarly, a succeeding member's speech may appear to be less relevant and informed than it would have been if he had known of the unspoken material before making his speech.
I should remind the House that the final responsibility for inclusion of unspoken material in Hansard rests with the Speaker. I do not propose a completely inflexible system. For example, the Treasurer must receive special consideration on Budget night. However, I think that the House has an obligation to itself to keep the Hansard record as near as possible a true record of what is said. I seek the co-operation of all members, not least Ministers and the Opposition front bench. I have directed the occupants of the chair to pay particular attention to this statement.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford Smith)-Mr Speaker, I seek your indulgence on this matter. I note with interest that you do not regard that decision, which I think has a lot of merit, as being inflexible. I should like to think that you would also regard it as being proper that transcripts of evidence given in court, which are a matter of record, and also judges' decisions, where those may effect a debate in the Parliament, could be incorporated. In other words, matters of record, particularly in judicial proceedings-that is, evidence given by a witness as to what he might think could affect members of Parliamentshould be able to be incorporated, as should judgments and determinations by judicial authorities. I regard that as essential because I have in mind something which I think might occur later today.
Mr SPEAKER-I indicate to the honourable gentleman that I disagree with the point he has made. As I emphasised in the statement, Hansard ought to be a record of what is said in this chamber. I have particularly in mind that very

2340 REPRESENTATIVES 2I October 1982

Questions without Notice

often members on either front bench, when asked for leave to incorporate, feel it necessary to grant leave otherwise it might appear that they are obstructing the course of the House. I think the process should not be the incorporation in Hansard but the granting of leave to table the document so that the document may be available to all. I emphasise that whilst I will not be inflexible, I am not willing to have Hansard become the repository of what a member chooses to judge as worth putting in Hansard, because that obstructs the whole purpose of Hansard as being an accurate record of what is said in the chamber.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition)-Mr Speaker, could I have your indulgence? May I, after I have had a look at what you have said in a more considered way, raise the matter with you if necessary? It is a very important proposition you are putting to us. I can accept some of the points you are taking. In fact, I have found it rather extraordinary on one or two occasions that people have sought to have incorporated in Hansard extracts of speeches they had made in this Parliament on earlier occasions. I can see that problem. On the other hand, there is a legitimate function in having certain documents from time to time incorporated in Hansard, because tabling, as I understand it, does not provide privilege which could sometimes be necessary. I wish, if it is desirable, to have the opportunity to raise the matter with you later. I presume that you accept that point.
Mr SPEAKER-Of course. The honourable gentleman may do so.
QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
INDUSTRY PROTECTION
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-My question is directed to the Prime Minister. I refer to recent statements by the Prime Minister, and earlier statements, of course, at the Commonwealth Heads of Government Regional Meeting in Fiji on the need for a reduction of the level of protection for Australian industry.
Honourable members---Hear, hear!
Mr LIONEL BOWEN--I notice the plaudits from the crowd opposite. I also refer to the serious problems now facing many industries in Australia because of the rapid growth of imports and dumping, for example in steel and metal products, where it has now been established that the Koreans are subsidising steel at the rate of $100 a tonne. The Queensland pineapple industry is under attack from imports from another place. The Tasmanian pulp and paper industry is also in

serious circumstances. Given those illustrations, could the Prime Minister indicate exactly what industries he had in mind in which protection could be reduced in accordance with his statements? What are the likely employment impacts of any such reduction?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The Deputy Leader of the Opposition will be well aware that when we announced the industry package on 19 July we said that because of current economic circumstances it would not be appropriate to have unilateral reductions in protection. The honourable gentleman will know that there had been an Industries Assistance Commission report relating to those matters. Of course, in that decision of 19 July we announced substantially improved depreciation provisions. We announced for the first time broad-based depreciation provisions for income-producing buildings. Those measures were broadly and widely accepted by all Australian industry because all Australian industry was to benefit. However, we made it quite plain that there would not be unilateral reductions in protection so far as Australia is concerned.
We would be very deficient in our responsibilities to Australia if we were not as a government and, I would hope if we were not as a parliament, concerned at the growing moves towards increased protectionism in many countries around the world. A meeting of trade Ministers at the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade ministerial meeting is being held in a very few weeks' time. One of the matters that will be under discussion at that meeting is some initiatives that Australia took which would be designed to achieve a general standstill or ceasefire in relation to protection and then a winding back of protective devices, whether that was protection in its traditional form or in the form of export subsidies. Those matters are up for consideration.
I think it is worth noting that Australian exports run into quantitative or non-tariff barriers in many fields. It may be that up to 50 per cent of Australian exports are affected by non-tariff barriers of one kind or another. But if our industries are to be excluded from more and more overseas markets and if that is to be the general trend of trade relationships between nations in the period ahead of us, then I suggest that the capacity of the major industrial countries to get out of the present recession will be very much reduced. In those circumstances we would be likely to see increasing levels of unemployment going way beyond the present 30 million to 32 million in member countries of the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development.

Questions without Notice

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2341

So whatever has been said needs to be put against the background of the decisions we took on 19 July and the multilateral discussions taking place at the GATT ministerial meeting. The nature of the Australian proposals was supported by all countries at the Commonwealth Heads of Government Regional Meeting, including New Zealand, Malaysia, Singapore and all the others as can be seen from the communique that was published at the end of the meeting. But there is no question of unilateral reductions in tariff. I hope that that provides the kind of assurance that the honourable gentleman wanted.
STOCK FEED GRAIN
Mr EWEN CAMERON-Can the Minister for Primary Industry inform the House what stock feed grain is being considered for importation? Will imported grain be available for direct use in drought affected areas or will it need to be processed?
Mr NIXON-The Government has agreed to an amendment to the Wheat Marketing Act to permit the Australian Wheat Board to import stock feed wheat if that proves to be necessary. It is one of the arms that we are introQucing simply to give enough flexibility to the various agents so that the very serious drought needs of farmers and graziers can be met. Consideration is being given to the prospect of importing some wheat. I point out to the House that if wheat does come into the country it will need to be milled at the point of entry. No grain as such can be transported into country areas in its natural form. It has to be milled to ensure that any weed seeds or diseasebearing grains are crushed and, therefore, are not able to be transported into the country areas. These are matters, of course, for my colleague the Minister for Health
As the honourable member for Indi will know every protection and precaution is taken to ensure that there is no prospect of disease being carried forward by grain importation. Those are the steps that will be undertaken. The only grain which we have been requested to import at the moment is sorghum. I understand that sorghum has very serious quarantine difficulties and importation is therefore unlikely to proceed. Consideration is being given to the importation of some wheat.
STEEL INDUSTRY
Mr LES JOHNSON-Is the Prime Minister aware that raw steel production went down by 25 per cent in the last year and that steel imports now consume up to 32 per cent of the domestic market? Is he aware that by December this year 8,200 jobs will have been lost at the steel works

and coal mines of Wollongong, Newcastle and Whyalla-some 1,800 by imminent retrenchment? Since today is Wollongong's day of mass protest against policies responsible for mass unemployment, I ask: Will the Prime Minister reverse his decision and substantially reduce import quotas? Further, will he inform Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd that this concession depends upon the withdrawal of all retrenchment notices by BHP in the steel industry and its coal mines?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I believe that everyone in this House would be concerned that people are being retrenched under current circumstances no matter what the industry might be. The decision that the Government had to take in relation to the steel industry was a complex one. It was not just a simple question of the level of protection for Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd because BHP is supplying what is a raw material for many other Australian industries. If those industries were going to be forced to pay much higher prices for the basic steel that they will use in fabrication and production, then they themselves would be in a position where they were not able to compete with imports and would need higher levels of protection. If protection in relation to BHP were too high, then one would have consequential claims for increased protection running through a large part of Australian industry. That would obviously create a difficult situation, one in which it would be even harder for Australian industry to compete in markets overseas. The main future of Australian manufacturing industry must surely not be just to supply the local Australian market but also to be able to compete internationally.
I think it is a great tragedy that the kinds of concerns that were inherent in the honourable member's question were not expressed through last year when the pressure for shorter hours and for higher wages contributed so much to the present problems and difficulties in the steel industry. That came at a time when world recession was obviously making it all the more important for Australia to maintain its competitive position. Putting all these particular factors together, the final Government decision was one which, in fact, gave considerably more protection to BHP than the Industries Assistance Commission or the Temporary Assistance Authority had recommended. The Government indicated at the time the decision was made that it believed it should not go further than it did, but it went very much further than the Temporary Assistance Authority in its recommendations had indicated it ought to go.

Questions without Notice

TELEPHONE DIRECTORY PAPER
Mr BURR-My question is directed to the Minister for Communications. Has the Government made any decision regarding the letting of contracts to produce telephone directory paper? In view of the difficult employment opportunities in Tasmania, will any contracts be let to Tasmanian paper manufacturers.
Mr N. A. BROWN-It is true that over the last few weeks I have been giving consideration to some recommendations from the Board of Telecom Australia with respect to the awarding of contracts for paper for telephone directories for next year. In the past there has been a particular problem in this regard to the extent that it has not been possible regularly to locate locally produced supplies of paper. On this occasion two Australian manufacturers and one New Zealand manufacturer submitted tenders. As I said, I have given consideration to the recommendations from the Telecom Board. I have very recently approved three quite substantial contracts for the supply of paper for telephone directories. One of those contracts is to Associated Pulp and Paper Mills Ltd at Wesley Vale in Tasmania. That is a contract for the supply of 12,001 tonnes of paper at a cost of just under $11 m. A contract of a similar size to Australian Newsprint Mills Ltd also, has been approved. That price also is over $10m. There is a smaller contract to a New Zealand manufacturer. I have no doubt that the awarding of the contracts, particularly that to the Wesley Vale mill, will make a substantial contribution to enabling those works to continue and will be a very substantial contribution to providing continued employment for people who live and work in that area.
RECRUITMENT OF TRADESMEN
Dr THEOPHANOUS-Is the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs aware that until about two months ago tradesmen recruited overseas by Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd arrived in Australia to work at that company's steel plants in Newcastle and Whyalla? Is the Minister aware also that some of those tradesmen have already been retrenched or are under threat of being retrenched by BHP? Does the Minister propose that his Department continue to assess immigrants according to the Occupational Demand Schedule which, by admission of his own departmental officers, is hopelessly out of date and which lists most categories of metal trades workers as being in short supply in all States at a time when metal tradesmen are being sacked all over the country?

Mr HODGES-The question raised by the honourable member for Burke is a very important one. It is given detailed attention by my Department in conjunction with the Department of Employment and Industrial Relations. The fact is that many industries in this country require labour often at short notice. Therefore, unless we recruit labour overseas at an early date we will frustrate the efforts of those industries to cater for the upsurge in activity. The Occupational Demand Schedule is provided twice yearly by the Department of Employment and Industrial Relations. But recently I have requested my Department to obtain more frequent reports from the Department and those reports are being provided. We have to appreciate, of course, that the more frequent reports may not be as accurate as the six-monthly reports are. Nevertheless, they are an indication of the demand for various skills throughout Australia.
I think it is worth saying that we do not force people in this country to travel from one area to another when their particular trade or profession is in demand in another area; in other words, we do not require people to travel to areas where they can obtain employment. Indeed, if they so desire they can go on the dole. Therefore, it is necessary either to bring people from other parts of Australia at their will or to provide skills from overseas. I assure the honourable member for Burke that every consideration is given to the very points that he is making.
COMMUNICATIONS SATELLITE
Mr JULL-Is the Minister for Communications aware of concern being expressed in the community that the Government is soon to award a 30-watt transponder on the HACOSS II communications satellite to the major television networks-namely, the 7, 9 and 10 networks? Has the Minister received indications of interest from other independent parties in these transponders? Will he guarantee that the interests of all parties will be considered before any decision on the allocation is made? Is a decision on the aliocation of the transponders soon, in fact, to be announced?
Mr N. A. BROWN-The answer to the part of the honourable member's question which asked, in effect, whether consideration would be given to the interest of ali parties before a decision is made on this matter is yes. I wish to dispel any suggestion that any decisions at all have been made on this matter. As the House wili be aware, the Government decided some time ago to make a very substantial advance in the whole communications field by establishing a national satellite

Questions without Notice

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2343

system which will consist initially of three satellites. Contracts have been considered and other important steps have already been taken. The decisions that have been made are with respect to a number of'the 30-watt transponders on the sattellite which, of course, will be used for distributing Australian Broadcasting Commission programs throughout Australia, particularly in those remote areas that do not at present receive television.
The question remains, of course, as to the use to which the remaining 30-watt transponders and, indeed, the other smaller transponders on the satellite will be put. I have received a number of representations from the interests that the honourable member refers to. It is true that consideration will be given to those. It is true also-I wish to emphasise this-that representations have been made by other parties with cases that are substantial. Those cases will be examined. Indeed, only a couple of days ago the honourable member for Maranoa introduced a delegation to me. I think the honourable member will correct me if I am wrong-we spent about two hours discussing the alternative proposal based on a completely novel use of the proposed satellite system to bring commercial television and other services to people, particularly in remote areas. I must emphasise that those representations are being given very careful consideration. No decision has been made. I will shortly be preparing material to put before the Cabinet. When that is done the Cabinet will make a decision. But I can assure the honourable member that whatever decision is made, it will achieve the original purpose of the satellite system, that is, to provide vastly enhanced communication, entertainment and information services to people in the whole of the country.
GOVERNMENT INFORMATION UNIT
Mr COHEN-My question is directed to the Minister for Administrative Services. I refer to Budget Estimates which indicate that this year the Government Infomation Unit will cost $1.4m, a rise of more than $1 mover 1981-82. Can the Minister say why such a large increase is occuring? Will he assure the House that no officers of the Government Information Unit have been or will be involved directly in work for the Liberal Party of Australia?
Mr NEWMAN-The functions of the Government Information Unit have been made clear on many occasions in this House and in the other place. It provides a ministerial Press relations service for Ministers who do not have Press secretaries. As I know the honourable member knows, unlike other governments this Government does

not have a proliferation of ministerial Press secretaries. Most of us depend on the GIU for that service. As to the Unit working for the Liberal Party, obviously with that definition of what the Government Information Unit does, it does not. It works for the Government and its Ministers.
As to the large increase in the appropriation, I take it that the honourable member is citing the correct figure. I do not have any dispute about that. I point out to the honourable member that the figure has basically risen because in previous appropriations certain expenses-telephone expenses and so on-were absorbed by Minister's offices, to which staff were attached. I felt that was not proper and I wanted a full and clear explanation of what was being spent on the GIU. The system was changed so that all those expenses would be revealed.
DROUGHT RELIEF MEASURES
Mr MacKENZIE-I direct my question to the Minister for Primary Industry. What measures are available to assist drought affected small business operators in Australia? Are these measures available in all States except New South Walesthe worst affected State? Has the New South Wales Government given any indication to the Minister of its position on assisting small business, its position in relation to Commonwealth proposals for a stock slaughter subsidy or its position on the application of the stock freight subsidy interstate? Why did the Commonwealth advance $lOm interest free to New South Wales over 2t months ago when that State has allocated only 10 per cent of that advance for drought relief measures and none of it has gone to small business? Will the Minister withdraw this advance from New South Wales forthwith unless the New South Wales Government gets on with the job of providing at least the same level of drought assistance as every other State provides?
Mr NIXON-I have to say that it is a very good question. I think it highlights the paucity of the New South Wales Government's approach to the whole drought problem. It is being totally miserable and miserly. For some reason the New South Wales Government fails to understand that in the countryside there are people who desperately need this aid. The core aid program provides, for up to $40,000 to be made available as carryon loans to small business. That announcement was made several weeks ago-indeed, months ago now. I regret to say that I do not think the New South Wales Government has yet picked that up as an aid to small business. It seems determined not to assist small business even though, across the

2344 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Questions without Notice

border in Queensland, the Queensland Government is providing aid to small business through this core aid program. I regret also that the New South Wales Government does not seem to be willing to pick up the slaughter subsidy of $3 a head which we announced as being available to the States. Again it does not seem to understand the difficulty farmers and graziers are faced with in dealing with the hundreds of thousands of starving sheep. The Victorian Government likewise is very slow to the barrier on that one, even though there have been strong representations by the farmers and graziers associations in both States seeking the introduction of the slaughter subsidy.
We made $IOm available. New South Wales has claimed for the last three years that in respect of the drought its Budget is unable to meet the needs of drought areas and therefore the New South Wales Government is unable to find the first $IOm required under the arrangement. The Commonwealth made an offer of $IOm interest free in this financial year so that that claim could not be made. I would not want my colleague the Treasurer to pull back that money; it is better left there. I hope, though, that the New South Wales Government, recognising the generosity we have shown towards this problem of drought, will start to be more generous itself. If the honourable member's figures are right and only some 10 per cent of that $IOm has been used, all I can say is that that speaks for itself about the approach taken by the New South Wales Government.

ROXBY DOWNS
Mr STEELE HALLI ask the Minister for Trade and Resources: Are the Minister and his Department fully aware of the enormous potential that Roxby Downs holds for future exports of minerals from this country? Has he any estimates of future production and can he give an outline of provisions which will be necessary for services such as roads, ports and infrastructure to support the large population which will live in the immediate vicinity and in other areas which will be involved in supply activities?

Mr ANTHONY-I am well aware of the interest of South Australian members, particularly the honourable member for Boothby, in the massive Roxby Downs project. I am pleased to inform the House that a draft environmental impact statement has been released for public viewing and is open for comment until 25 November. The Minister for Home Affairs and Environment will examine the final EIS and make recommendations to me. The draft EIS shows that Roxby Downs has at least 2,000 million tonnes of mineralised material. For planning purposes, production capacity has been set at 150,000 tonnes of copper a year, 3,000 tonnes of uranium oxide a year, 3,400 kilograms of gold a year, and up to 23,000 kilograms of silver a year.
The annual value of production could range from $442m to $658m per annum. The capital cost of the project in 1981 terms will be $1 ,400m. During the construction stage between 9,300 and 18,600 jobs will be created directly and indirectly in South Australia. The production work force will be 2,400, but the total employment generated in the long term will be between 5,700 and 8,300. A township of 8,000, rising to 9,000, will be established with planning envisaging a town population of approximately 30,000 people. It is quite obvious that the economic benefits for South Australia, and indeed the nation, will be enormous. What needs to be understood by the people of South Australia is that this whole project could be in jeopardy if there were a change of government in South Australia. The economics of it are quite clear. Unless uranium is mined the project will not go ahead. Uranium is a very important part of the overall production activity.
As we all know, the Labor Party is opposed to the development of uranium in this country and wants to close the uranium industry down. I think with such an enormous project and with the economy of South Australia being in such an uncertain situation as it is at the moment there is nothing offering more hope and more potential to that State than the Roxby Downs project. I hope that when people go to the polls in South Australia they will take full note of what that decision would mean if they unwisely opted to vote for the Australian Labor Party.

The decline in activity that had be.en evident in the past seems to have been arrested and unquestionably some recovery is forecast in the second half of this financial year. hazard the guess that, as with so many other issues, when the Australian Labor Party claims it is rethinking a matter that means it is merely rearranging its prejudices.
1982-83 BUDGET

The measures involve cuts in personal income tax; in the case of single income families an increase in the dependent spouse rebate; in the case of all families, with single or double income, increases in family allowances; and, in the case of that vast army of Australians paying off their homes, a rebate on home interest payments.
Although there has been proper caution about the consequences on home interest rates of the recent falls in official interest rates, there is no doubt that the anxiety of many Australians about possible further increases in home interest rates have at least been put to one side for the time being. I inform the House that it is now 21 October and they are nowhere near 18.5 percent.
HOUSING: RELEASE OF STATUTORY RESERVE DEPOSITS
Mr HAYDEN-My question is directed to the Minister for Industry and Commerce. It refers to his comments about additional money made available for housing in the Budget. Does he recall in the Treasurer's Budget Speech, more than two

2346 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Questions without Notice

months ago now, a firm undertaking to arrange for the release of funds equivalent to one percent of statutory reserve deposits, that is, a figure of about $400m, from the Reserve Bank for housing purposes? Has that amount of money been released yet? If not, why not, and when will it be released?
Mr PEACOCK-It is essentially a question for the Treasurer. As I understand it, under section 21 of the Banking Act it is the responsibility of the Reserve Bank to determine the statutory reserve deposit ratio applicable to the trading banks. As a consequence, the Government's request for a release of one percentage point from the statutory reserve deposits for the special purpose of lending for housing is a matter for consideration by the Reserve Bank Board. The terms and conditions on which the banks would make any such funds available would, of course, require subsequent discussion with the banks. At this stage, as I understand it, it is not possible to give the precise timing of any such discussions. However, it would be the Government's wish to see the matter brought to fruition as soon as possible.

DEPORTATION ORDER
Mr COLEMAN-Has the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs received a recommendation from the Administrative Appeals Tribunal that he revoke the order to deport both a Fijian Muslim man, convicted of the manslaughter of his four-year-old daughter, and the man's wife, convicted of assaulting the child, causing her grievous bodily harm, and criminally neglecting her? Did the Tribunal in reaching its recommendation state that it took into account evidence that the couple came from a culture in which child-beating and wife-beating are more common than in Australia? Has the Minister reached a decision on the Tribunal's recommendation?
Mr HODGES-The particular case to which the honourable member for Wentworth has referred relates to a decision which was taken by my predecessor, the present Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations. Toe case came before Mr Justice Morling in the Administrative Appeals Tribunal and this resulted in a 47 page judgment which is currently before officers of my Department. I point out to the House that the powers of the Administrative Appeals Tribunal in relation to deportation orders made under sections 12 and 13 of the Migration Act are such that it can affirm the Minister's decision or recommend that the Minister revoke the deportation order. I emphasise that the judge's decision is recommendatory and not mandatory.
I wish to say one or two things in relation to criminal deportations of persons. They are not taken lightly by me or indeed, I suggest, by my precedessors from either side of politics as Ministers responsible for immigration. Countless man hours are put into the investigation and preparation of detailed cases before they are placed before the Minister. All decisions concerning criminal deportations are taken by the Minister. I am conscious of the deportation powers vested in me and I am aware that the life of the individual is very much in the hands of the Minister.
In saying that I admit that I agonise over many of these cases. The decisions, as I said earlier, are

not taken lightly. I weigh up what is best for Australian society in terms of the protection of decent, honest Australians and I also take into account the previous record of the individual, whether that individual is likely to offend again and what the likely nature of it and its effects on Australian society will be. The courts mete out penalties, and it could be considered a further penalty if deportation is in fact ordered by the Minister. I can only say to the honourable member for Wentworth that I will give deep and careful consideration to the decision of the Administrative Appeals Tribunal, but I can give no guarantee that the recommendation of that Tribunal will be upheld.

PRIVILEGE ATTACHING TO TABLED DOCUMENTS
Mr SPEAKER-For the information of the House I wish to comment very briefly on the reference made earlier today by the Leader of the Opposition on the question of the privilege attaching to tabled documents. There is a long history attached to this question, largely deriving from issues raised in the United Kingdom case of Stockdale v Hansard (1837). I will not, however, canvass the history of this issue.
Briefly, it may be held that the actual tabled copy of a document would be so intimately a part of the proceedings of the Parliament that it would be absolutely privileged. The difficulties emerge, however, in respect of the distribution of copies. By virtue of the provisions of the Parliamentary Papers Act 1908, the actual parliamentary paper version-the familiar blue-covered 'editions'-of tabled documents is absolutely protected and no action may be entertained. This would clearly not, however, cover the distribution at the time of tabling of copies of a document which may contain actionable material.
This issue came under consideration in 1971 in respect of a report relating to the crown of thorns starfish. Because of doubts as to the protection of persons in distributing copies, the House resolved

to authorise publication of the document in terms of the Parliamentary Papers Act. By this action the House conferred immunity, and this has been repeated on many occasions since then.
The question of the need for privilege to attach to a document arises only when the document contains actionable material. Such material has never been permitted to be incorporated in Hansard. I do not propose that in future such material should be permitted to be incorporated, unspoken, in Hansard.

Mr SPEAKER-The honourable member for Wills has indicated that he wishes to make a personal explanation. Does he claim to have been misrepresented?

A very much smaller sub-heading above it reads: 'It must be a majority decision by workers'. There is a heading on the inside page 'Hawke; take pay cut'. Government supporters-Hear, hear!

After reading the headlines a number of people in the Labor movement and outside made some comments. It is appropriate that I read to the House the transcript which I have

Personal Explanation

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2349

from the Parliamentary Library of what was said on the Nationwide program. I refer to the transcript, where the host, one Richard Carleton, said tome:
Mr Hawke, the Union has turned to you and said, the option is a pay cut or getting the sack. Would you tell him to take the pay cut?
This question was asked after I had been shown clips of people in a factory being asked which alternative they would take-reduced hours with an associated pay cut, or the sack. My reply to Richard Carleton I think was fairly specific and is not capable of being misunderstood by an average intelligence, I said:
What I would say is this. That if in this sort of situation by agreement of the workers concerned and in consultation with the workers, the Union and the management, there is agreement that in difficult circumstances this is the best thing that can be done for the most people, then I would say that's what should happen.
The compere came back and did not seem capable of understanding a straightforward answer. So I said again:
Well that's what I am saying, there's got to be an agreement. If the majority of people say that that's what they want in the general good, then I think that's what should apply. But I've got to put this rider in, that we mustn't allow the emergence of this sort of agreed position to meet a difficult circumstance, we must allow that to set our minds away from what is the basic necessity, and that is that Government has got to reverse its economic policy so there is the creation of more job opportunities for more people.

Well, you're slow in comprehension, Richard, let me say for the third time .

I've said it, I can say it another six times but I won't say it any differently.

Yes well I'm saying not one person can answer it, it's got to be a collective and if there, as your program shows, some didn't like it, some did. In those circumstances I think it ought to be a majority decision and then if the majority decision is that this is the best way of handling it, then I think that that democratic expression of view should prevail.

DEPARTMENT OF THE PARLIAMENTARY LIBRARY
Annual Report
Mr SPEAKER-For the information of honourable senators and members, I present the fourth annual report of the Department of the Par!iamentary Library for the calendar year 1981. The report covers a year of record levels of activity for the client services, total measurable de-
mand being up by some In per cent over the pre-
vious year. Similarly, in the supporting services, some 9 per cent more books and periodicals were processed than the previous year while demand on the alert services was up by nearly 50 per cent.
A Client Assessment Survey carried out during the year sought the reactions of users to specific responses received for information and research requests. The overall result indicated a high degree of satisfaction with the work done but indicated that some attention is needed in ensuring the relevance of documents provided in response to questions.
Planning for automation is well under way with the expectation that electronic means of information retrieval and delivery will be routine in the New Parliament House. In the year under review the Statistics Group of the Legislative Research Service in particular greatly enhanced its computer capability and commenced the development of a number of major packages. These include a time-series and econometric analysis package, an election results data base and another consisting of the demographic profiles of Federal electorates.
The Parliament's Bicentenary Publications Project, an initiative of considerable national significance, was begun during the year and is administered and funded through the Department of the Parliamentary Library. It arose out of a lengthy consideration by the Library Committee

2350 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Human Rights Commission

concerning the appropriate way in which the Parliament might mark the Australian Bicentenary and the opening of the new Parliament House in 1988. The general framework of the project has been determined as follows: An analytical history of the Australian Parliament since Federation; a narrative history of the people and events connected with the Parliament; a popular book explaining how Parliament works, and a series of smaller and more specialised monographs prepared in conjunction with the major works.
An immense amount of preparatory work undertaken in connection with this project in 1981 enables me to allude to developments in 1982 and to tell honourable members that the author commissioned to write the analytical history is Professor Gordon Reid, Deputy ViceChancellor of the University of Western Australia. He may be remembered by some honourable members in the House as a former Serjeant-at-Arms before he went to academic work. Professor Reid was an officer of the House of Representatives from 1946 to 1958. He was Serjeant-at-Arms from 1955 to 1958. The author commissioned for the writing of the narrative history is Mr Gavin Souter, a senior journalist with the Sydney Morning Herald and the author of a number of successful historical works including Company of Heralds, a history of John Fairfax Ltd. Expressions of interest are currently being sought from authors interested in writing the popular account of how Parliament works. Proposals for the writing of the monographs will be assessed by the advisory board which Mr President and I have set up to advise us on all aspects of the project, and by the publisher to the project when he is appointed.
The year 1981 was a busy one for the Parliamentary Library and I am sure that all honourable members would wish me to thank the Library Committee for its services and to congratulate staff of the Parliamentary Library on their work and achievements.
HUMAN RIGHTS COMMISSION
Report and Ministerial Statement
Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond ValleyMinister for Communications and Acting Attorney-General)-Pursuant to section 30 of the Human Rights Commission Act 1981, I table the report of the Human Rights Commission on the Australian Citizenship Act 1948. I ask leave to make a short statement.
Leave granted.
Mr N. A. BROWN-I mention to honourable members that the report I have just tabled is the

first report of the Human Rights Commission and as such is particularly welcome. I point out also, as the Commission has done, that this is a report on the Australian Citizenship Act as it currently stands. The Government has anounced significant proposed changes to the Act which take into account important human rights aspects. The Government will, of course, be giving careful consideration to the Commission's recommendations. Due to difficulties with printing it has not been possible to table printed copies of the report. It is expected that these will be available next week.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford Smith)-by leave-My colleague the honourable member for Port Adelaide (Mr Young) will be handling this matter but he is temporarily incapacitated. I note with interest the recommendations of the Human Rights Commission. They relate to very important matters such as the definition of the word 'alien'. The Commission recommends that a mentally handicapped person should not be discriminated against and that those in mental hospitals should not have their qualifying period for citizenship suspended. The Commission recommends that the requirement of an adequate knowledge of the English language be consistent with the international covenant. Section 17 of the Australian Citizenship Act which deprives persons of Australian citizenship in certain circumstances should be modified to allow them to retain that citizenship. The Commission recommends that the oath of allegiance provided for should not include renunciation. There are discriminatory aspects of the Australian Citizenship Act in certain respects. The report notes also that there is an impending decision in the High Court of Australia on related issues. This report is of significance to a large number of people in Australia. It is important that the Commission's recommendations be carefully and quickly looked at. I note that the Government intends to do that. It will be advising further as to its actions.
AUSTRALIAN BIOLOGICAL RESOURCES STUDY ADVISORY
COMMITTEE
Report and Ministerial Statement
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment)-For the information of honourable members. I present the Australian Biological Resources Study Advisory Committee report for I July 1980 to 30 June 1981. I seek leave to make a short statement concerning this report.
Leave granted.

Biological Resources Advisory Committee

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2351

Mr McVEIGH-The Australian Biological Resources Study is a national program to study and document the naming, scientific classification and distribution of the Australian flora and fauna which form a crucial and very fragile part of our national resources. The study is planned and coordinated in my Department by the Bureau of Flora and Fauna. Over one hundred scientists and institutions from all States are taking part with the support of Australian Government grants made through the ABRS Participatory Program. Grants are awarded on the recommendation of an advisory committee of eight leading biologists under the chairmanship of Professor Ralph Slatyer. This committee also provides me with advice on priorities for the program. Some current priorities are the production of two important scientific publications, the Flora of Australia and the Zoological Catalogue of Australia. The program also includes the establishment of a network of data bases containing information of major biological collections held in Australian institutions.
The report contains a list of 90 grants awarded in 1980-81, totalling $606,000. This amount represented an increase of 216 per cent in funding over the previous year and an increase of 272 per cent in the number of grants awarded. The Advisory Committee recommended further increases in Government support for this very important program. Despite the need for continuing constraint of expenditure the Government has steadily increased the resources available for the program. In 1982 $800,000 was provided for grants and the Government will keep the level of support under close consideration. I commend the report to the House.
Mr KEATING (Blaxland) -by leave-The Australian Biological Resources Study is an important program co-ordinating taxonomic work on Australia's flora and fauna. It is working on the publication of Flora of Australia and an Australian faunal directory. Such taxonomic work is essential for ecological studies which require proper identification of the myriad of Australian plants and animals. Proper environmental assessment of resource projects can be hampered by the lack of adequate taxonomic data.
This Government finally recognised the importance of this program when it increased funding in 1980-81. This reversed its policy of cutbacks in funding to the program, which received $425,000 in 1975-76 but only $192,000 in 1979-80. The Government has not matched the funding increase to the participatory program with increases in staff levels in the Bureau of Flora and Fauna. The Advisory Committee noted that work in the

fauna and taxonomic sections was seriously impeded during 1980-81 financial year by lack of staff and recommended that junior scientific and technical staff were urgently required for expanding programs. The Government has increased staff levels by only one since 1980-81.
The Bureau has been forced to move between four premises over the past three years. One of these buildings, the Wales Centre, contained no laboratory space or storage facilities for specimens. I hope that the Bureau will be able to settle at its present premises in Acton House and that it will be provided with adequate facilities to carry out its work. I call on the Government to accept the Advisory Committee's recommendations.

Case (Australia) Pty Ltd, the tractor manufacturer at Murray Bridge, has laid off 83 employees in the last three months; Neilson electrical has laid off 28 in the last three months; Static Gereral Engineering Co. Pty Ltd has laid off 39 in the last

three months; Actil at Cheltenham has laid off 22 people in the last three months; D. Dunlite has laid off 17 in the last three months; Adelaide Steamship Co. Ltd has laid off 35 in the last two months; Lloyds timber has laid off 30 in the last two months; Adelaide and Wallaroo Fertilizers Ltd has laid off 30 in the last five months; Royal Assurance Australia Ltd has laid off 25 in the last six months; W. H. Whylie has laid off 27 and moves are being made for a shorter working week; Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd at Whyalla has laid off 125 this year, plus another 250 jobs will go. Some 4,700 jobs in the public sector have also been lost since the Tonkin Government took over in 1979. Scott Bonnar Ltd at Thebarton in my electorate has dismissed 96 people this month and the plant is closed. The figures I have cited relate mainly to unionised establishments. Some 7,377 jobs have been lost. In addition, several thousand jobs in non-union factories plus thousands of jobs in very small businesses, service stations and shops have been lost.
Thousands of jobs have been lost and the unemployment rate is the highest in mainland Australia. Over 50,000 people are unemployell with no hope of getting work. Over 50,000 peoplr are living on the dole in a State where the Tonkin Government was elected on its promise to stop the jobs rot. Job losses have been greatest among full time workers. In the past 12 months ended August 1982 a total of 12,000 full time jobs were lost in South Australia. This has happened under a government that said it would stop the job rot. In August 1982 a total of 50,400 persons were unemployed in South Australia. The unemployment rate was 8.4 per cent compared with a national rate of 6.7 per cent, under a government which said that it would stop the job rot. The same Government will have to answer to the people in the next couple of weeks and I am quite sure that it will get the answer that it deserves. It will be kicked out of office and we will have the Bannon Labor Government installed.
South Australia's good industrial relations suffered a very severe setback when the Tonkin Government's Minister of Industrial Affairs, Mr Dean Brown took office. Three years of Tonkin Government in South Australia has created more havoc in relations between the trade union movement and the Tonkin Liberal Government, particularly under the Ministership of Dean Brown who may have been too busy trying to get media coverage in order to set himself up to knock off the present Premier. The confrontationist attitude of Mr Dean Brown is paralleled only by that of the previous Federal Minister for Industrial Relations, the Honourable Ian Viner, and we know

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2353

what happened to him. Let us look at the record of Mr Dean Brown, Minister of Industrial Affairs in the Tonkin Liberal Government in South Australia. Recently he brought into the South Australian Parliament industrial legislation which, if agreed to, would bring about massive confrontation with the trade unions and, to a degree, with the employers of South Australia. One can only assume from the statements of this Minister that he wants confrontation. Let me explain. In the South Australian Parliament on 16 September 1982 the Minister of Industrial Affairs, Mr Dean Brown, stated:
Despite the good industrial environment in this State it would be foolish to believe that circumstances will remain static. Industrial relations is by its very nature an organic creature. Thus, in order to ensure that the framework operating in South Australia continues to be relevant to contemporary and future conditions, in November 1980 the Government appointed Mr F. K. Cawthorne, I.M., to conduct an independent examination of the Industrial Conciliation and Arbitration Act. The terms of reference of this exercise were 'to review the Industrial Conciliation and Arbitration Act, 1972-1979 (as it then was), and to report to the honourable the Minister of Industrial Affairs on any requirement for legislative change to meet current and likely future developments in industrial relations'. As I have indicated previously in this place, that report was made to me on a confidential basis.
There was an interim report which got wide coverage and was supported by unions and by employers, but the confidential part of the report Dean Brown refuses to make public. He refuses the call of the Opposition spokesman on industrial affairs in South Australia, Mr Jack Wright, to debate the issue publicly. Quite clearly the Government has something to hide. It is afraid of its industrial relations record. The Government has created havoc and unemployment in that State and will have to answer for that in the next two weeks.

Mr TAMBLING (Northern Territory) (11.23)-The issue I raise today was alluded to earlier in a question to the Minister for Communications (Mr N. A. Brown). The question related to the use of the domestic satellite that will be introduced in 1985 to provide great benefit across Australia for many years. The point I highlight is that the introduction of television to Aboriginal communities in my electorate through the domestic satellite will cause a social revolution in the Northern Territory. In the last few months I have had occasion to speak to a number of organisations about this. I have spoken to the Isolated Children's Parents Association, the Central Land Council in the southern part of my electorate, a

number of other Aboriginal organisations and, of course, many educationists.
I ask honourable members to ponder for a moment on the fact that Aboriginals will be presented with a much wider view of the European lifestyle through television. The values projected will be much different from those of Aboriginal society. Consequently, traditional ways will decrease in relevance, particularly for the young people reacting to what they will see on television. Aboriginals will see successful black Africans and American negros living in circumstances that are totally different from their own. Once this starts happening many young people will question their culture and accept other trends more easily. The changes will be most noticeable a generation from now, and that is why I am raising this issue and posing the question.
So often we have looked at Aboriginal issues based on the past and on the present problem. It appears that nobody is prepared to crystal ball, to look ahead and to ask what the sociological implications and changes will be. The first group of Aboriginals to experience this will be the generation who will grow up entirely with television and will start looking for opportunities outside the communities in which they live. Young Aboriginal children will have learning experiences of the Sesame Street variety, far removed from anything they will have seen in their surroundings. Unlike their parents, they will grow up with more pressures placed on them by our society through television. It remains to be seen how well Aboriginal society will be able to sustain the very important ceremonial events of its lifestyle, yet also move forward into the twenty-first century with the rest of the community.
I believe that the whole Australian community, in particular my electorate in the Northern Territory, should be preparing for these changes that will come in the future. For example, what will be the implications for employment? What will be the reaction of employers? Most businesses plan ahead well, on long term stategies and with a long term time scale. But if we look at the education of Aboriginals today, we see that dramatic changes are happening. The adolescents and the teenagers are being educated largely by our standards and our values in places such as Yirara College in Alice Springs and Kormilda College in Darwin. They are being brought into contact with the types of work experiences that we will be encouraging them to take. I state quite unequivocably that unless employers are preparing to give many young Aboriginals job opportunities by taking them into the work force, we will be leaving these young men and women in a void.

2354 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Grievance Debate

How must government departments react? How should they adapt their programs to be more in tune with the changing economic circumstances of Aboriginals? I hope that the hand-out mentality that so many government departments have perpetuated will disappear. But unless this is worked on constructively and positively and is planned for by government departments, we will see the dead hand of the bureaucracy perpetuating what it has done in the past without any form of vision. I am always critical that government departments seem to be the last to be able to pick up threads of the future direction and future administration in order to plan. It is unfortunate that the white people who have been attracted to positions of authority as community advisers and so on in so many Aboriginal communities tend to be people whom the Aboriginals like, not the people who are best qualified to serve Aboriginals through the agencies which employ them. Many of the whites who have been employed are on salaries that they could not attract if they applied for routine public service jobs in any other community. They would be appointed at much lower levels. But it appears that they are being paid salaries which are too high, to compensate for the disabilities caused by remoteness.
Those public servants, whether they work in Aboriginal communities or in departments in Darwin or in Canberra, must be able to anticipate all of the changes that are happening as Aboriginals receive mining royalties and get involved in business enterprises. Unless the Public Service reacts to this situation we will not be able to attract sufficient young people to take up appropriate positions of Aboriginal leadership. In particular, the Departments of Employment and Industrial Relations and Social Security must assess the changing welfare needs of Aboriginals and look carefully at the types of services that they provide and will be required to provide over the next 10 years. Future education for Aboriginals should capitalise on the satellite and take advantage of the benefits that will be offered. That wiIl mean some very radical and drastic changes for education in remote communities but let us not forget that there wiIl be a television set in every school, in every community haIl, in every home and in every humpy-if those humpies have not been improved by then. That will be one of the provisions.
I have strongly recommended to the Law Reform Commission that it study these changes in Aboriginal lifestyle, particularly in view of the reference that it is currently looking at with regard to Aboriginal customary law. We can no longer believe that Aboriginal customary law is

static and historical. It must adapt, and it wiIl adapt, to the future, particularly as a result of the effects that wiIl be brought about by television. I assert that one of the major things we should be looking at is the proposition that customary law should be viewed as a religion practiced by those who choose without infringement of the rights of others and without contravention of Australian or international law. It may weIl be that Aboriginal customary law will be considered as something like we have in our society, a masonic lodge available for those who elect to join it rather than joining it automatically. I am posing the major consideration at which we should be looking. What will be the impact of the domestic satellite for five, 10 or 20 years on Aboriginal communities throughout Australia? What are the changes that Government must make now, not, as it has with all other Aboriginal problems, after the problems have surfaced and got too serious? The major discrimination that arises in our community is generaIly brought about by behavioural problems and drunkenness. Let us not perpetuate it by adding to it a media mongul.
Mr WALLIS (Grey) (11.33)-In my contribution to this grievance debate I shall, like my colleague the honourable member for Hindmarsh (Mr Scott), refer to the South Australian election on 6 November. I understand that there was a big black headline in last night's Adelaide News stating: 'ALP tries to stop Pension Rises'. I suppose all those people who support the Australian Labor Party in South Australia wondered whether South Australia is going back to 1979 and whether they are to see a repeat of the filthy role that the Adelaide News played in the last State election. The action of the News in 1979 was to be deplored. The absolute fierceness of its political bias and prejudice came through quite strongly, to such an extent that I could name plenty of people with whom I associated who decided there and then that they would never buy the Adelaide News again and who have not done so. Let us look at what happened in 1979. I have before me an article dealing with the election and the role that was played by the News. It is an impartial article; it is not written with any political bias or from any particular side. The article states:
Much was made by the ALP of the publicity campaign launched by six major employers' groups against the state Labor government.
That is what happened. The six major employers' groups got together and financed fuIl page advertisements in the local newspapers. That, of course, helped to bring down the then Labor Government. The article goes on to state:

On Monday night the Premier of South Australia delivered his policy speech. I think that before he finished people were questioning some of the figures which he supplied. I refer to an article in yesterday's Australian which made reference to Mr Tonkin's figures for the amount of investment in South Australia. In his televised policy speech on Monday night he stated that the Liberal Government in South Australia was successful in attracting to South Australia 20 per cent of all investment capital in Australia. Now, everything in South Australia is closing down. My friend the honourable member for Hindmarsh supplied figures relating to the companies that are laying off employees everywhere. That 6.33 per cent is a fair way from the 20 per cent that was cited by the Government. Pernaps we could look at the Liberal's record in some areas. It is a fact that South Australia, which used to be a much larger State population-wise than Western Australia, has been not standing still but has been sliding backwards. It is now expected to be the smallest populated State in the Commonwealth. Information published by the Commonwealth Treasury as part of the 1982 Budget indicates that by the end of 1982 South Australia's total population will be well behind that of Western Australia. It is expected that its population will be exceeded by Western Australia's population by 8,300. The figure given for South Australia is 1,334,700 and the figure for Western Australia is 1,344,500. So we can see that South Australia's population will be down on that of Western Australia. During 1980-81 the largest loss in a financal year was recorded at over 7,000. The number of people who have left South Australia to go to other States since the Tonkin Government has been in office is over 15,600.

My friend the honourable member for Hindmarsh detailed some figures and gave a clear indication of the situation there. In my electorate quite recently Titan Manufacturing Co. reducing its staff by attrition. It announced last week that it would reduce its staff by a further 350. It is cutting out shifts. The fact is that the same situation applies as applies everywhere else; if employers cannot obtain lower staff levels by attrition, the workers will be given notice.

Mr BOURCHIER (Bendigo) (11.43)-1 wish to raise two matters in the grievance debate. I first deal, as did the honourable member for Grey (Mr Wallis), with the attitude of the media in reporting or misreporting facts. I refer to an article by one Kenneth Davidson of the Age which was printed on 24 June 1982 in which he attacked private schools for being funded by public money. He misquoted the facts to suggest that those schools received some sort of privilege and special treatment as against government schools. This article was replied to in depth in a letter by the Victorian Parents Council, but of course the Age elected not to run it. So I take this opportunity to answer some of the points made by Mr Davidson. Mr Davidson's article began with the usual theme that education has a fundamental role in obtaining top jobs and that top jobs are now becoming scarce in relation to demand. He continued:
It is not surprising in this situation that the more atlluent or more ambitious~or even more caring~parent is going to look for ways to beat the competitive pressure.
The obvious way is to buy a superior~or an apparently superior~education which can be done by increasing education spending for yourself and your group or by squeezing the resources available to other groups.

Thus, the Federal Government now spends more on nongovernment schools, which educate 22 per cent of the school population, than on Government schools which educate the other 78 per cent.
While expenditure per pupil in Government schools is still higher than for Catholic schools and about the same as for other non-government schools, non-government schools are expanding their spending while reducing their fees.
Thus Catholic secondary schools were able to reduce their fees by 10 per cent and the other non-government secondary schools by 14 per cent in real terms between 1974 and 1979.
To argue against that article and put the matter correct, I point out that in any discussion concerning funding from public funds-public funds including Commonwealth funds-the only figures that really matter are those of total government expenditure, State and Commonwealth. Public funds are collected from all citizens and the ownership of any moneys distributed by government resides in the people. Governments are simply the trustees of that money and expected to see to its equitable distribution on behalf of all Australians. Schools in Australia are funded by State and Commonwealth governments. State governments have the primary responsibility for education and spend almost five times as much on schooling as does the Commonwealth through the Commonwealth Schools Commission.
Some of the deficiencies in Mr Davidson's article can be summarised in this way: In total expenditure on schools from public funds of almost $5,000m and in a situation in which State governments have the primary responsibility for education, Mr Davidson commented only on Commonwealth funding, and ignored the fact that State governments spend, as I have already mentioned, five times that amount. There are good reasons why the Commonwealth, in its role of making good the deficiencies of State governments, has increased funding to non-government schools, and an impartial writer would at least have mentioned them. Mr Davidson is wrong in stating that fees in non-government schools were reduced between 1974 and 1979. The Schools Commission has in fact stated that the levels of both fees and other private income have increased in real terms.
In 1979 and 1980 the State governments spent $4,117m on schooling, of which $3,935m-95.6 per cent-was allocated to government schools. That is hardly what one would call a bias towards non-government schools. The allocation to nongovernment schools from the States was $182m out of $4,117m-that is, 4 per cent. The Commonwealth Government, through the Schools Commission, spent $853m. The sum of $492.5m, 57.7 per cent, went to government schools and $360.5m, 42 per cent, went to non-government

Grievance Debate

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2357

schools. Thus, of the total allocation of $4,970m, only $542m, or 10.9 per cent, actually went to the 22 per cent of Australian school children in nongovernment schools. The truth is quite obviously totally opposite to what our friend Mr Davidson elects to say. He referred to tables from the Commonwealth Schools Commission. I have asked the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett)

opposite whether he would allow me to incorporate a table in Hansard. I have mentioned this matter to the Acting Clerk of the House. The table is headed 'Recurrent Expenditure from Public Funds 1974 and 1979'. It clearly sets out the true figures in relation to this matter. I seek leave to incorporate that table.
Leave granted.

Mr BOURCHIER-Finally, only the Commonwealth Government gives assistance for capital works in non-government schools. In 1979-80 total capital expenditure by State and Commonwealth governments was $516m. Nongovernment schools received $37m or 7 per cent of the total. That small amount of $37m came from the Commonwealth Government. I suggest to Mr Davidson that when he elects to write articles-he is supposed to be an expert in economics-he should exercise his mental ability, study the facts and cover the whole school funding system and not be selective in a biased, political manner.
