The second matter I wish to bring to the attention of the House is a matter of concern about which I have spoken to a number of my fellow members. It arises out of the decisions of the Repatriation Commission in regard to war veterans who have applied for reassessment of their disabilities. I refer particularly to a person in my electorate who made an application and, even

though his medical history was confirmed as worsening, was told he was not to get any increase and that he could get a job.
I checked on this matter. I thought it was rather startling. We have to keep in mind that this man is 68 years of age. He was a motor mechanic in his working days. I checked with the Commonwealth Employment Service in Bendigo and I found that it did have a telephone call from the Department of Veterans' Affairs. The Department was advised that work opportnities for a 68-year-old mechanic would be virtually non-existent. I do not think it would take much intellingence to work that out, particularly if the person concerned was medically unfit. The Department of Veterans' Affairs officer said that his board interpreted the phrase 'virtually non-existent' to mean that there were some positions available. He also advised that the Department took only disability into account and did not consider age. The Repatriation Commission stated:
The report of an examination by a departmental medical officer who recorded the member's statement that he considers the pain in the low part of his back is worse and he now gets pain when straightening up from a bent position; he gets very irritable, his nerves are not good and is easily upset; he

2358 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Grievance Debate

also has pains in both wrists, elbows and left ankle and both knees ache a bit.

On clinical examination the medical officer found that, in respect of the member's lumbar spine, flexion when standing
was restricted but lateral movements were full; there was no swelling of the knees and movements were full; he had full movements of the elbows with pain at full extension; the right
wrist is thickned with some tenderness and restricted dorsiflexion; no significant changes were noted in the fingers.

The report goes on to state:

In summary the medical officer stated that the member had no apparent trouble dressing or climbing onto the couch but
has some difficulty sitting up from a prone position.

Honourable members should not forget that he is 68 years of age and was injured during the War. The Commission then referred to the case of Bowman v. the Repatriation Commission. The report
states that in broad terms:

. . . in assessing a member's entitlement to pension in

excess of the one hundred per cent

General Rate,

the determining authority is bound in law to take into account

the effect of the member's physical or mental disability on his

capacity to earn remuneration, by employment or otherwise,

in any market reasonably accessible to him. It is necessary, in

this respect, to establish whether, in fact, he is prevented, by

reason of his service-related disabilities, from performing his

normal occupation or some other occupation which, having

regard to his work history . . .

How broad does one have to be in order to try to work out how a 68-year-old man, crippled with
arthritis and suffering from other complaints, can possibly find a job? This is no problem for the
Commission, It has had no trouble at all in assess-
ing that the evidence, from which I have already quoted, shows that such work is accessible to the
member. The Commission's report continues:

The Commission notes factors affecting the member's capacity, namely his age-

At that stage he was 67-

and his rejected disabilities; however, these cannot be taken into account in this assessment.

That means that he is obviously getting better as
he gets older. According to the way in which the
Commission has assessed this man's ability, there is no doubt that he will be able to find a job prob-
ably anywhere at all. The report continues:

Having considered all of the evidence in this case, the Commission accepts departmental medical opinion in relation to the appropriate pension assessment and also the member's ca-
pacity for employment, being satisfied beyond reasonable doubt, that this opinion is fair and proper.

If honourable members were to go to any decent pub on a Saturday afternoon they would find out just what the expression 'fair and proper' means. It cannot be related to the Commission's decision.
An absolutely impossible situation is occurring when we have people who are supposed to know better making an assessment that a person who has a disability caused by the War is actually improving as he is getting older. Those people then

have the gall to state that this person aged 68 has to get work. He has to find a job because their could be a position available even though he has been told that jobs are practically non-existent.

Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (1\.53)-1 Jom with my colleagues the honourable member for Grey (Mr Wallis) and the honourable member for Hindmarsh (Mr Scott) in referring to the present South Australian Government. That Government was elected in 1979 on its supposed ability as an economic manager. The major argument advanced for its eJection in 1979 was that South Australia was in a decline as a result of the Labor Government's alleged anti-business activities. We have had three years of what is supposedly a pro-business government. What has been the result? In those three years there has been a steady and continuing erosion of South Australia's economic position. The only argument that the Premier can produce now, after three years of this government is that South Australia is going down the gurgler more slowly than the other States. The best argument that the South Australian Government can produce for not producing the economic miracle that it promised to bring to South Australia is that South Australia is going down the plughole more slowly than the other States. Even that argument has no substantial support in the evidence.
Somehow Mr Tonkin is trying to pretend that South Australia is resisting the economic malaise over which the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) presides. In fact, Mr Tonkin and the Prime Minister are birds of a feather. In both we have euphoric economic promises at election time and stagnation in the years between. At eJection time we have them both mouthing the same private enterprise litany. They promise miracles of economic achievement and then the following years are years of stagnation. The evidence is now overwhelming that the Prime Minister and Mr Tonkin are false economic prophets.
All honourable members are fully familiar with the 1975 policy promise of the Prime Minister that:
. . . a growth rate of 6-7 per cent is quite feasible . . .
In the seven years of Federal government ever since that promise was made that growth rate has never been approached. Mr Tonkin, in both his 1979 campaign and his present eJection campaign, has also stressed the importance of growth to South Australia. Let us look at growth in terms of

Grievance Debate

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2359

the criteria he set down for the Labor Government in 1979. In 1979, under Labor, South Australia's share of private fixed capital expenditure for manufacturing industry was 7.5 per cent-a disappointing figure. Under this Government, the latest Australian Bureau of Statistics figures puts South Australia's share at 5 per cent of private fixed capital expenditure. Another key indicator of growth used by Mr Tonkin-let us use his own criteria for judging Labor governments to have him judged-is new car registrations. With a 9 per cent share of the national population in 1979, South Australia had 8.2 per cent of new car registrations. This was lower than the population share of registrations and was an indicator used by the Liberal party in its 1979 election campaign to indicate economic stagnation. But under the Liberal Government's three years of management the share of new car registrations slumped to 7 per cent in 1981. This share has now risen marginally to 7.65 per cent. Thus, on Mr Tonkin's criteria, South Australia has now a lower share of these registrations than was the case under the stagnation about which it complained in 1979.
Again, all honourable members in this House would be familiar with the Prime Minister's policy speech promise of 1975:
Only under a Liberal-National Country Party Government will there be jobs for all who want to work.
We are all familiar with that promise which was made seven years ago. The result has been that, under this Prime Minister, we have had higher and more continuous rates of unemployment than under any Prime Minister since the Great Depression. Similarly we have Mr Tonkin in South Australia campaigning on a 'stop the job rot' call in 1979. If unemployment in South Australia is any indicator, jobs have simply rotted away under Mr Tonkin. When Mr Tonkin set out to stop the job rot in 1979 South Australia's level of unemployment was 7.6 per cent. At present 8.5 per cent of its labour force is unemployed. As for Mr Tonkin's argument that South Australia is doing better on the employment front than the rest of the nation, let us take the statistics for the 12 months ended August 1982. South Australia had a 1.3 per cent decline in employment, the second worst decline of any State in Australia. Only Tasmania had a bigger drop in employment in the same 12-month period.
There is a common thread to Liberal Government economic promises. They talk about sound, free enterprise management and they say that that will get the economy going. That is what Mr Tonkin promised in 1979.

Mr Hodges-Nobody believes you.

The result of those kinds of promises for this country, including South Australia, has been record unemployment and stagnant growth. Having failed there, what do these leaders fall back on? Is it job creation? Not on your life. Is it economic stimulation? No, that is too dangerous. What they produce is the cargo cult mentality that somehow there will be a mineral boom. He is the very same man who promised in December I980-just before the Federal .election-that there would be a mineral boom across this nation. That boom evaporated as soon as the election was over. We now have the same cargo cult mentality.

JOINT COMMITTEE ON THE AUSTRALIAN CAPITAL TERRITORY
Report
Mr FRY (Fraser)-On behalf of the Joint Committee on the Australian Capital Territory, I present the Committee's report on retail trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory, together with the transcript of evidence and the minutes of proceedings.
Ordered that the report be printed.
Mr FRY-by leave-The report I have just tabled deals with the subject of retail trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory. This matter was referred to the Committee for consideration after a proposal to repeal the present trading hours ordinance contained in section 90 of the Commonwealth Functions (Statute Review) Act 1981-otherwise known as the Lynch Committee of Review on Commonwealth Functionswas debated in the Senate. During this debate it was agreed that the Government would not proclaim the relevant section of this Act until the

Committee on Australian Capital Territory

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2363

Joint Committee on the Australian Capital Territory had considered comprehensively the question of its implementation and the possible effects of deregulation of trading hours. It was further stated in the debate that the Committee should provide every opportunity for all views to be heard.
The Committee was requested to examine the manner in which deregulation of trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory might be implemented having regard to the effect on retailers, employees and consumers. The Committee formally accepted its terms of reference on 10 September 1981 and concluded its deliberations on the inquiry less than 12 months after its referral. The initial response to the inquiry was quite significant. In the four weeks immediately after advertising the terms of reference, about 4,000 pro forma slips from individual shoppers, 200 form letters from small traders, 60 form letters from individuals, 40 letters from small traders, 6 letters from residents as well as 50 major submissions from retailers, trade unions, interested organisations and individuals were received. In order to provide an opportunity for interested parties to present their arguments the Committee held a series of public hearings in Canberra, Sydney and Melbourne. It was decided also in the concluding stages of the inquiry to conduct a series of inspections and informal discussions in the Northern Territory where trading hours, apart from liquor trading, are not subject to regulation. In all, 68 witnesses, representing 38 organisations, and seven persons appearing in a private capacity, gave evidence to the Committee.
As I do not agree with the recommendations of the Committee I do not wish to speak any further about the report. I want to make a few remarks about the dissenting report which I have signed. The normal practice, of course, is for the Government to make a decision following a report but in this case a decision was made by the Lynch Committee. The Joint Committee on the Australian Capital Territory was then asked to legitimise that decision. I objected strongly to this procedure. By handing this type of reference to the Committee I believe that the Committee's legitimate role has been prostituted to legitimise a hasty and illconceived decision of the Lynch Committee. Many of the decisions of the Lynch Committee in relation to the Australian Capital Teritory can likewise be described. Some of them have been abandoned. I hope the Government will give further consideration to this decision.
As a result of this procedure we found that the Committee was placed in the position of having to make recommendations which in my view and in

the view of my colleagues were in direct conflict with the evidence presented to the Committee. Hence, my colleagues and I really had no option other than to present a dissenting report. I must say that I had some sympathy for the Government member, the chairperson, who was placed in the position of trying to justify government policy in the face of contrary evidence. The Government is obviously bowing to pressure from its big business friends. Even that pressure was not unanimous. I must say that the big business organisations of Myer Canberra, G.J. Coles and Co. Ltd and Woolworths Ltd pressured very heavily for deregulation. But the views of big business organisations were by no means unanimous in that Waltons Bond Limited and David Jones (Aust.) Pty Limited gave contrary evidence and did not press for deregulation at all.
I suggest to the House that the evidence, if looked at impartially and objectively, indicates that there was overwhelming objection to deregulation because 4,000 shoppers, 200 small traders, the Australian Association of Independent Businesses and the Shop Distributive and Allied Employees Association wrote to the Committee and registered their total opposition to deregulation. In contrast with the strong opposition to deregulation there has been virtually no public demand for a change in trading hours in Canberra. In this respect I must say that I have found that people have taken a fairly unselfish attitude in this matter. Some people have said that it would be nice to have longer trading hours but that we must consider the position of the people who work in the industry. Some people have said that it is not fair to ask people to work extra hours and on the weekends because that would deny them the opportunities for recreation and relaxation that we have. An influencing factor in the lack of demand may be that Canberra residents already enjoy the convenience of shopping seven days a week and extended shopping hours at their small neighbourhood shopping centres where most things can be purchased.
We consider that the evidence indicates that the cost of goods to consumers will rise if trading hours are extended. David Jones provided such evidence. It said that Saturday afternoon trading would result in an increase in the price of goods of around 1.5 per cent. Waltons calculated a 13.3 per cent increase in payroll costs associated with Saturday afternoon trading. Such an increase must put pressure on prices. In addition, small traders argued strongly that increased overheads brought about as a result of increased trading hours would inevitably result in higher prices for consumers.

2364 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Committee on Australian Capital Territory

The dissenting report basically supports the major arguments raised by the traders and the Shop Distributive and Allied Employees Association as well as many religious and community groups which appeared before the Committee. They generally stated that deregulation would result in reduced opportunities for full employment, increased prices of goods to the consumer, would seriously disadvantage and threaten the viability of small traders, would cause disruption to the family life of employees and would reduce the opportunities for sporting and religious pastimes at weekends. In addition, I think it should be noted that any change in the shopping hours in the ACT would seriously disadvantage traders in the surrounding areas of New South Wales, particularly in Queanbeyan. I hope the Government takes note of this situation. It would cause a considerable amount of disharmony in the harmony which presently exists between the ACT and Queanbeyan.
The dissenting report appeals to the Government to reconsider the situation and suggests that if the Government looks at the evidence impartially it will reject the proposition for deregulation of trading hours. We suggest that the Ordinance should be amended, if necessary, to remove any anomalies but that for the interests of the people and the shopkeepers-particularly the small business people and the people who work in the industry-deregulation would be a backward step. We suggest that it is in conflict with the evidence and the findings of inquiries into deregulation in many other parts of Australia.
Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth)-by leave-In conducting this inquiry the Joint Committee on the Australian Capital Territory was aware that its first obligation was to the traders, retail employees and consumers of the Australian Capital Territory. But it was also aware that the inquiry was being watched carefully by those same classes of people in the States. If anything I believe that added to the conscientiousness of the inquiry. It is an area in which there are strong disagreements. In recommending a limited deregulation the Committee believes that it has gone as far as is possible to reach some sort of consensus among the conflicting positions.
The Committee began by examining the anomalies in the actual operation of the Trading Hours Ordinance in the Australian Capital Territory. This Ordinance provides that the sale of goods in shops is prohibited on a Sunday, a gazetted public holiday, a weekday before 6 a.m. and after 6 p.m. other than a late shopping day when trading hours are extended to 9 p.m. and on a Saturday that is not a public holiday before 6 a.m.

and after 12 noon. The Schedule to the Ordinance
specifies goods and classes of goods which are
exempt from the provisions of the Ordinance. The Ordinance has been subjected to a number of pub-
lic inquiries and reviews. The difficulties associated with the enforcement of the Ordinance have been highlighted repeatedly at public inquir-
ies. Due to problems associated with the lack of enforcement of the present legislation the situ-
ation in relation to trading hours in the Australian
Capital Territory is, at present, not satisfactory and some direct action is required to correct the
anomalies in the present system.

In giving evidence to the Committee the De-
partment of the Capital Territory admitted that it
is not at present enforcing the Ordinance and that it has no regular inspection program. It also gave evidence that when action was once taken to en-
force the Ordinance it resulted in strong public reaction and antipathy. I should also mention the
problems associated with the definition of exempt
goods. The Schedule contains some obvious anomalies. An example is mentioned in the re-
port. It is that a light fitting could be considered as an electrical good, non-exempt, or as a good for use in the decoration or repair of domestic prem-
ises, exempt. I think the Committee regards this sort of interference in the market-place as
ridiculous.

In accordance with the terms of reference of

the Committee it has examined 'the manner in

which deregulation of trading hours in the Aus-

tralian Capital Territory

might be

implemented having regard to the effect on re-

tailers, employees and consumers', after having

examined how trading hours operate in other

States and after having taken into account the

special conditions applying in the Australian

Capital Territory. In looking at the effect of

deregulation on retailers it is recognised that cer-

tain constraints apply which make it difficult for

total deregulation to operate. Retailers have

entered the market on the basis that the hours of

trading, as set out in the 1962 Ordinance, will con-

tinue to apply.

Small traders are more vulnerable to sudden
changes in legislation affecting their hours of op-
eration than are larger retailers. Therefore, small traders would be greatly disadvantaged in an environment which changes overnight from one
of regulated trading hours to one of deregulation. The Committee has taken this into account in its recommendations. Another point is that the
nature of leases in large shopping complexes prohibits individual retailers from opening or closing at times which they are free to determine. The
question of whether any increased costs

Committee on Australian Capital Territory

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2365

associated with opening longer hours would be reflected in increased prices to consumers was not
able to be determined by the Committee.
In relation to the effect of deregulation on employees, a lot of controversy has been generated by claims that deregulation will contribute to a futher decline in the number of full time permanent positions in the retail industry. The Committee considers that deregulation would most probably continue, if not accelerate, the trend towards the structural readjustment of employment in the industry away from full time to part time employment. However, the Committee considered that this is an inevitable trend. The Committee was also concerned about the argument that the proposed deregulation would lead to a disruption of family life. This matter certainly concerns me because I have had discussions with members of the Shop Distributive and Allied Employees Association-the SDA-over the years and, indeed, more recently in my electorate in
Sydney as well as, of course, during this inquiry.
Major retailers have stated in evidence that only voluntary staff would be used on weekends and that this would reduce the impact on the family and social life of employees on Saturdays and Sundays. I know that members of the SDA whilst accepting that that statement had been made to the Committee and that it was influencing the Committee, nevertheless expressed scepticism about it. They stated that they are in the service business and are psychologically conditioned to service. They thought that if a retailer really required a certain member of the staff to work on a Saturday afternoon it would be difficult for that member of the staff, psychologically conditioned to service as he or she is, to refuse. They said the effect over a time would be a moving away from the reliance on voluntary staff. We have made this recommendation on the basis that only voluntary staff would be used at weekends, and this is something that we will obviously have to watch the operation of very carefully.
Reference was made during the inquiry to results of surveys to determine public support for or opposition to deregulation or extended hours of trading. The most recent survey results indicate increasing support for an extension of trading hours and the polls on this matter are published in appendix IV to the report. For example, a poll conducted by the Bulletin and published on 29 June 1982 indicated that extended shopping hours had gained favour since the previous poll. It showed that 56 per cent of people aged 14 and over throughout Australia favoured all day Saturday trading while 36 per cent were opposed and II per cent were undecided. In New South

Wales, perhaps the most relevant State, 68 per cent of those polled favoured Saturday afternoon trading and only 20 per cent opposed it. The Committee acknowledges the shortcomings of these polls. A lot depends on the wording of the question. They give conflicting results. Nevertheless statistics are set out at appendix IV to the report on retail trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory.
The Committee, while favouring the general principle of deregulation, considers that the nature of the market place in the Australian Capital Territory, combined with the oversupply of retail space in areas of Canberra and a shortage in others, makes it difficult to recommend the immediate repeal of existing legislative provisions governing trading hours. It is impossible because of the number of variables involved to forecast accurately the effect of deregulation. It is also not possible to draw on the experience of comparable areas in Australia because of the special nature of the market place in the Australian Capital Territory and the fact that there is a general lack of experience of deregulation in other States.
Therefore, in reaching its final conclusions, the Committee wanted to arrive at a formula which would enable a move to greater flexibility in trading hours while not implementing full deregulation. It was considered that too many exclusionary categories of shops or goods would create problems of definition and legal interpretation and that therefore a single provision was preferable to a number of constraints. For this reason it was decided to base the determination on the number of staff employed by anyone retailer which may involve one or more shops in one week.
The Committee supports unlimited trading Monday to Saturday. This means that in addition to the hours presently allowed under the Ordinance all shops should be free to determine their own hours of operation between midnight Sunday and midnight Saturday. On these recommendatons, the major traders will be excluded from opening on Sundays. This will enable small shop owners to be free to trade in an environment not dominated by the major stores. In conclusion, the Committee considers that any future reviews of trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory should be referred to the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly.
Mrs KELLY (Canberra)-by leave-I support the dissenting report of the Joint Committee on the Australian Capital Territory on retail trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory. It strongly rejects the findings of the main report.

2366 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Committee on Australian Capital Territory

I reject the majority findings because of the implications of those findings on business and the community in this Territory. The majority report recommends deregulated trading hours all day Saturday. That recommendation will undoubtedly bring in Saturday afternoon trading throughout Canberra. Honourable members may say that that is a matter of choice for the retailers, but I point out that the majority recommendation will do away with the element of choice. Everyone knows that it is the major retailers, those who in the Committee definition employ over 100 people, who are the pacesetters in the industry. If they open, small retailers will be forced into following their example. Added to that is the fact that most of Canberra's shopping is done in shopping complexes which have a major retailer. Do honourable members think for one minute that the Lend Leasers of this world are going to tell the small retailers in these complexes that they will have the freedom to choose whether or not they open on Saturday afternoon? That is clearly not the case, and the Committee has evidence that that would not be the case.
The minority report, however, still allows that flexibility. Our recommendation would ensure that there would be freedom for small traders to open seven days a week but they would not have the pressure of the large retailers forcing them to open on Saturday afternoon. Honourable members will recall that the decision to deregulate the Australian Capital Territory's trading hours was made last year by the Lynch razor gang. It is worth noting that the role of the Review of Commonwealth Functions was supposedly to prune government expenditure. In the light of this it was very difficult to understand the purpose of inflicting a totally deregulated trading environment on Australian Capital Territory business as that would hardly save the Govern-
ment any money.
The reality was that the only reason that decision was brought down by that committee was to use the Australian Capital Territory in an attempt to get a flow-on for deregulation of shopping hours throughout Australia. Members of this Parliament should be aware of the significance of this report. It is not just a report about trading hours in Canberra. What it will end up doing is bringing about a flow-on effect of increasing trading hours throughout Australia. It will begin in New South Wales. It will flow from the Australian Capital Territory borders to Queanbeyan and Yass and then throughout New South Wales. This report is probably the most significant report to be brought down on trading hours in Australia. Honourable members have to understand what

we are debating today and the effects that it will have on people in the retail trade throughout Australia.
The razor gang, or Lynch Committee, in making its deregulation decision, did not consult with local House of Assembly members, local business organisations or Federal representatives as to the likely effect of such a decision on the local economy. The decision was made in the face of strong opposition from many quarters throughout Canberra and was only very reluctantly referred to the Joint Parliamentary Committee on the Australian Capital Territory for consideration. At
least three of the 10 members of that Committee represent the Australian Capital Territory and are able to look after the interests of Australian Capital Territory residents, business people and employees. Unfortunately in the final report of the Committee it is apparent that one of these local representatives~the Chairman of the Committee~was able to ignore the fact that 4,334 submissions which were received opposed deregulation while only 36 favoured it. I know that local residents and business people will have noted that with very great disappointment.
It will be recalled that the Australian Capital Territory has no State or local level of government. Its only form of representation in a legislative forum is through its Federal members of parliament~its two House of Representatives members and two senators~and through the Joint Parliamentary Committee on the Australian Capital Territory, which unfortunately has become a surrogate administrative and appeals body for local Australian Capital Territory matters. It is not only inappropriate but also undemocratic for a small number of Cabinet Ministers to hand down far reaching decisions such as this one when they do not live in Canberra and are not accountable to the people of the Australian Capital Territory. I know that the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) will support me on this matter as he spoke very strongly in similar terms on Tuesday evening last about the impropriety of outsiders making decisions for his constituents.
It is readily apparent from the number of submissions and the evidence received by the Committee that the overwhelming majority ofretailers and employees are opposed to deregulation of trading hours in Canberra. It is irresponsible of the Committee to ignore the wishes of the people who live in Canberra, those who have to work in the deregulated trading hours environment and the consumers and retailers who would have to pay for the increased costs associated with unregulated shopping hours. It is inevitable that the cost of goods to consumers will rise if trading

Committee on Australian Capital Territory

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2367

hours are deregulated. David Jones provided evidence that Saturday afternoon trading would result in an increase in the price of goods of about 1.5 per cent. Waltons have calculated a 13.3 per cent increase in payroll costs associated with Saturday afternoon trading which would put pressure on prices if it could not be absorbed by the firm. Many small traders argued strongly that increased overheads as a result of increased trading hours would inevitably result in higher prices to consumers. That is just common sense. If a store opens longer the trader will have to pay more.
Appendix III of the report indicates the strength of local feelings on this matter. A total of 4,334 submissions opposed deregulation and only 36 submissions supported it. We are very concerned that the Committee's report has not accepted the evidence provided by local businesses and shoppers and appears to favour the submissions of a very small number of large interstate retail chains-Myer, Woolworths and Coles. These chains appear to have had considerable funds at their disposal to launch major television advertising campaigns in other States as well as in the Australian Capital Territory to get support for their views. It is worth reiterating that after all this effort only 36 submissions out of 4,370 submissions received by the Committee favoured deregulation.
It is readily apparent that the push for deregulated trading hours did not develop locally but rather was a push originating from other States in the hope that extension of trading hours in Canberra would flow through to New South Wales and Victoria. I believe that will be a direct consequence of this report. For example, how can Queanbeyan and other nearby towns not follow Australian Capital Territory traders without disadvantaging the traders in those towns? There is very little doubt that this recommendation must flow on into New South Wales and be incredibly significant there. Local residents have in fact been quite content with existing trading hours. Here in Canberra we already enjoy incredible flexibility in our trading hours. We have the convenience of seven day shopping and extended hours at all small neighbourhood shopping centres. Further, it has been my experience that most visitors and new arrivals to Canberra from other States are pleasantly surprised by the generous and convenient shopping hours that already exist. Many honourable members will be aware of the situation from their own experiences. In areas such as food and groceries, for example, Canberra is well served seven days a week by its unique system of

urban planning with small neighbourhood shopping centres. In the area of department store retailing only one firm, Myer, supports deregulation, while David Jones and Waltons oppose it.
I am very disappointed that the Committee chose to disregard the enormous number of major inquiries that have already been conducted on this subject in the States and even in the Australian Capital Territory. Extensive reviews of trading hours in other States were conducted-in New South Wales and South Australia in 1977, Queensland in 1978 and 1982 and in Victoria in 1978. These all resulted in the recommendation that trading hours not be extended other than to include one week night. Accordingly, we reject the Committee's finding in chapter 5, on page 121 of the report, that 'there is very little factual data to support many of the contentions made'. On the contrary the subject of trading hours has been exhaustively studied in other States. More importantly, the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly Standing Committee on Development conducted a major inquiry in 1979 into trading hours. It recommended that trading hours not be extended because this would seriously disadvantage many of the small traders. I ask honourable members to remember that this is a recommendation of the group elected by the people of Canberra. It recommends that we do not extend trading hours.
However, one of the recommendations of the majority Committee report brought down today is that in future all matters relating to trading hours be referred to a committee of members of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly. In recognition of the importance of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly in matters affecting the Australian Capital Territory it is suggested that any future review of trading hours in the Australian Capital Territory be referred to a committee of members of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly. However, when the House of Assembly Committee brought down a report on trading hours its views were ignored. Basically the views that it expressed were that we should not extend trading hours because the economic climate in Canberra was not appropriate. That was a decision made in 1979. I think all honourable members of the House would agree that the prevailing difficult economic climate in Canberra has not improved since 1979. In fact, if anything, it has got worse. Accordingly, I believe that this is the worst possible time to be considering measures that would cause serious disruption to Canberra's major private sector industry and theaten commercial viability and the competence of investors.

2368 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Committee on Australian Capital Territory

I have grave doubts about the argument that increased sales would result if people had more flexible shopping hours. It must also be questioned whether estimates of savings bear very much relation to the spending power of people when it is remembered that consumers now have to save more to preserve their real assets which are gradually being eroded by inflation. In times of very high interest rates and high housing prices-Canberra is second only to Sydneyconsumers need to save significantly greater amounts to bridge the deposit gap. People simply do not have the cash to spend. All of these factors cast doubt on the notion that there would be more disposable income available for spending if shopping hours were extended. My colleague the honourable member for Fraser (Mr Fry) has pointed out the adverse effect that deregulation would have on retailers and their employees. The honourable member for Wentworth (Mr Coleman) who was speaking to the report also referred to the fact that we were attempting to get a commitment from the traders that they would not force anyone to trade on Saturday afternoon. I have grave doubts as to whether we would be able to enforce that.
However, the area that I am particularly concerned about is in relation to those small family retailers. They are the people who will suffer as a result of this. They are the ones who will not have that element of choice and who will have to open on Saturday afternoon. This decision will place enormous strains on these people and their families. For employees it is likely that this will mean less security of employment and acceleration of the trend to part time employment in this industry. I also reject the Committee's suggestion that the implications of deregulation and more flexible trading hours have not been fully considered by those giving evidence. On the contrary, most small business owners are well aware that it is not commercially feasible to open and shut a business at various times throughout a week in line with the most profitable periods of trade. Most traders are well aware that consumers would not be attracted if opening hours were varied on an individual basis and if there were uncertainty about particular shops being open on particular days of the week or times of the day.
In conclusion I believe that consumers would not be served by such a deregulated shopping environment. I think the minority report that states that small retailers should be able to open 24 hours a day, 7 days a week, but that large stores be closed by I p.m. Saturday and until Monday morning is a very good compromise. This recommendation would be easy to implement, cause

little disruption to business and would provide sufficient opportunities for consumers.
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory)-by leave-I publicly thank the honourable member for Fraser (Mr Fry), the honourable member for Wentworth (Mr Coleman) and the honourable member for Canberra (Mrs Kelly) for their remarks. I compliment the Chairman of the Joint Committee on the Australian Capital Territory, Senator Reid, and all the members of that Committee for the work they have done, The Committee inquiry that the Deputy Chairman, the honourable member for Fraser, referred to commenced on 2 November last year. It has been a mammoth inquiry involving some 4,000 plus submissions and over 70 witnesses. I will read with great care the Committee's recommendations. I will also read with great care the dissenting reports of the honourable member for Fraser, the honourable member for Canberra and Senator Colston, and the separate dissenting report of Senator Georges. I will advise the Government in due course.

PECUNIARY INTEREST
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:

Pecuniary Interest

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2369

The Government's failure to provide for public disclosure and accountability in respect of members of parliament, senior public servants, statutory offl'ce-holders and the judiciary, thereby causing a lack of public confidence in the administration of national affairs.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number o/members required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr LIONEL BOWEN ,(Kingsford-Smith) (12.51)-This is not a new matter for the Parliament. At this stage we will be interrupted by the luncheon adjournment before the debate is finished. This matter is very serious. In recent weeks and months it has become very clear that the people of this nation regard the Parliament as ineffective, unable to cope with the problems of normal administration, and certainly weighed down by allegations of inefficiency, negligence and, indeed, corruption. If it goes on in this fashion we will have no democracy left and certainly no respect for the Parliament. It comes back to what we in this Parliament have been doing for a period of years. As far back as 1974 we suggested that there should be public disclosure of income and assets by parliamentarians, public servants, statutory office-holders, or anybody in any position which could influence a decision the general public might feel was unfair to them or gave an advantage or a benefit to those able to influence that decision. There has been a fair amount of agitation since that time as to what should be done.
Parliaments in other countries-for example, in the United States of America, Canada, the United Kingdom and Sweden-are able to lay down a course of conduct not only for members of parliament but also for people holding public office. In 1974 we set up what was known as the Riordan Committee-the Joint Committee on Pecuniary Interests of Members of Parliament-to report on what should be done. The Riordan Committee said that it felt there should be a code of conduct to remind members that their ethical obligations to the community did not cease merely by declaring their interests. It felt that there should be public disclosure to ensure that there was no misunderstanding about people's obligations when holding public office. That recommendation was agreed to and I will now advert to its principles. We asked when the Committee's proposals were likely to be implemented-bearing in mind there had been a change of government in 1975-and the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) declared that at

that stage his Government had not finally determined its attitude to the matter.
This approach continued until the inevitable disaster happened within the Government's ranks in what is known as the Lynch indiscretion, the result being, of course, that the then Treasurer lost his job on the eve of an election and the Prime Minister stated in December 1977 that he wanted a new approach to the matter. Therefore, in 1978 the Bowen Committee of Inquiry Concerning Public duty and Private Interest was established. Because of public opinion of the former Treasurer's indiscretion-there appears to have been a large amount of capital gain which was dissipated amongst members of a family, thereby minimising tax; it was as simple as that-he lost his job and there was introduced a rule of conduct which guaranteed that nobody would minimise tax and that there would be retribution should somebody do so. The Bowen Committee presented a report, which we have debated. The Opposition has said in debates that it is a pretty weak report, which it is. Honourable members cannot deny that. Whilst the Bowen Committee was asked to grapple with the problems, since its report the problems have grown more acute and more difficult for this Parliament to gloss over.
I turn to more recent reports, such as a report on the meat industry by the Kelly Committee of Inquiry to Examine Commonwealth and State Meat Inspection Systems. That 'report showed that an industry worth $3,000m was held to ransom by 1,800 inspectors. That report talks about many other matters that were wrong in the meat industry. For example, some evidence in the Landros notes suggests blackmail, inspectors not doing their job, free meat being made available, non-compliance with standards, drunkennessall sorts of allegations. If a Minister has the responsibility for the running of a department, he is obliged to set out a code of conduct for that department. He is obliged to set out in the code of conduct that the department will report to him any matter that comes under its notice. The permanent head of the Department of Primary Industry should have reported to the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) as soon as it was clear that there was mismanagement and difficulty. Something should have been done about a major industry being held to ransom by 1,800 inspectors. Nothing was done until substituted meat appeared in San Diego. If this was a matter of knowledge within the Department and was made public in evidence to an inquiry, why was the Parliament not told?
I come to the Treasurer (Mr Howard). He tabled a report which clearly indicated that in

2370 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Pecuniary Interest

1977 there was concern about tax measures which would mean a substantial loss of revenue, including sales of companies with current profits and sales of dormant companies. As far back as 1978 there was a question of what was to be done about revenue problems. With regard to straw companies, on 24 May 1978 the Commissioner of Taxation~who, I might add, was doing his job-in a report to the Treasurer, stated:
I have previously mentioned the worrying problems of a trend to tax evasion through the stripping of funds from companies so that they are not able to meet their tax liability.
In many cases there is an arrangement for a trustee to be appointed who is made liable to tax but who is a man of straw. Why was the Parliament not told about those problems at the time? Why is there no disclosure of information of that nature? It does not do any damage to the Government. The Government has the information. But it does enormous damage to peblic concern about what is happening to revenue when the public knows that this information was made available to the Government four years ago and nothing was done about it. The evidence discloses that in November 1978 a draft Cabinet submission indicated that there was a very serious fraud on revenue. Why was the Parliament not told about this matter, as an example of what we mean by public disclosure?
I turn to the classic case of the Asia Dairy Industries scandal. An amount of $3m was placed in a bank account in Singapore. The Auditor General stated that Mr Uytengsu confirmed to his officers that he decided to move the funds to Singapore because of somebody's decision and that he entered into arrangements. These funds could properly be said to belong to dairymen in Australia. I add that $3m is not an insignificant amount of money. In the final analysis of the situation the Auditor-General stated that the funds were subsequently removed to a Swiss bank account. My office was informed that the account cannot be located. Nothing will be done about that. I am mindful of the fact that the Auditor General, when writing to an officer of the Prime Minister's Department after the report was tabled, said:
I only want to make this comment: That the tabling of the report has generated a remarkable resurgence of memory on the part of officers of the corporation who, during the audit investigation, found themselves unable to offer more than the barest modicum of information to the audit base.
When we talk about public disclosure we have to talk about codes of conduct that Ministers must lay down for their departments, as well as what we have said in previous debates which, I might add, the Government has tended to ignore. The Bowen Committee made other recommendations.

A study of Hansard clearly indicates that in 1980 the Government had in mind the setting up of an ethics committee~a public integrity commission~which would generally talk about a register of parliamentarians' assets and liabilities. None of that was done.
Sitting suspended from 1 to 2 p.m.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-Prior to the luncheon break we were debating the Opposition's proposition that something should be done to give some more prestige to this Parliament in the light of relatively recent events. Reference was made to the Bowen report which recommended that where there can be a conflict between a private interest and a public duty the public of Australia is entitled to know all the facts at all the relevant times. Prior to lunch I was indicating how recent events have downgraded the prestige of this Parliament and have perhaps made it look ineffectual. I referred to all the crime, corruption, negligence and incompetence that is now being revealed in reports that the Parliament eventually had to commission. The public is asking: 'Why did things go so tragically wrong, costing millions or billions of dollars? Why are those who seem to be guilty of crime not being prosecuted?' Generally it appears that this place is just a talking shop and that we are not really implementing anything at all worthwhile.
I wish to advert to the responsibilities of those in public life-not only members of parliament but also public servants, office holders and others. We ought to operate on the basis that office holders are trustees~that applies to all of us~and that we have a duty to account. We also have a duty of care. We have a responsibility for competence and we have a liability for negligence. Failure to perform should mean the sack. Of course, it does in our political terms mean the sack because our constitutents evaluate us and can accept or reject us. Before the luncheon break I enumerated some of the reports that have been made public and the matters that they deal with. There was the meat scandal. A multi-million dollar industry was in jeopardy because of blackmail and all sorts of criminal activity which were known to the Department and allegedly not drawn to the attention of the Minister. What is the solution? Surely it is that a Minister should set down a code of conduct whereby anything that is deemed to be wrong in his Department should be brought to his notice. In relation to Asia Dairy Industries we saw $3m apparently just frittered away, disappearing into Swiss bank accounts. The Auditor-General visited the company and could not find out what had happened to the money. It is certainly related to matters which meant that

Pecuniary Interest

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2371

the dairymen of Australia had some equity in that amount. Surely that matter should have been dealt with on the basis that there should not have been that negligence or incompetence.
We come to tax evasion or avoidance, the straw companies. It has now been disclosed that the extent of tax avoidance and evasion was put on record in front of the Treasurer in 1978. I make the point that surely if a matter relating to tax revenue was on record to the Treasurer it should have been on record to the Parliament. The Government would have at least been prodded into action. Instead, it has lost billions of dollars and has had to wait four years for the report of the Costigan Committee of Inquiry into the Activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union to indicate clearly where it was wrong. The Bowen report clearly indicated that a number of matters ought to be done. Some of them have been done but the Government has not really grappled with the issues. The Bowen report made a very significant point which I think is worthy of note. It stated:
The impression should be avoided that any person can improperly influence an office holder or unduly enjoy his favour.
An enormous number of people seem to have had the benefit of enjoying the favour of or, apparently, of influencing an office holder. We have to do something about this and it has to be done in the following terms: A Minister, when he is appointed, should set out a code of conduct not only as to his own assets and liabilities-that ought to be a matter of public register, and the Government will not accept that fact; it was the Bowen committee, I think, which said that something should be done along those lines-but also as to minisferial responsibility. There should be a code of conduct for public servants. When matters of criminal activity, incompetence or negligence come to the attention of public servants they should certainly advise the Minister and, if nothing is done promptly, advise the Parliament. The code of conduct for the public servant should include an obligation to bring to the Minister's attention any problems that may arise. This should be done whether or not the Minister asks for the information. There cannot be this plea that we often hear now: 'I didn't know; I wasn't told'. The inevitable plea of the Australian Taxation Office is: 'Under section 16 of the Income Tax Assessment Act, we cannot disclose the information you seek.' Nobody wants to know people's names, but the files show that there is certainly a record of massive tax loss. A similar code of conduct should apply to the judiciary. We cannot have members of the judiciary perhaps interpreting the law under which they themselves

might have some benefit. There has to be a register of their interests. The Costigan report reveals things that we have never been told. Mr Todman, for example, wrote an 18-page report on what he thought was wrong with the Attorney-General's Department. There is plenty wrong with that Department.
In summary, apart from the obligation to comply with the missing matters-that has not been dealt with in the Bowen report-new matters and new material now before us clearly indicate that there has to be a more detailed code of conduct, a more frank public disclosure. Those appointed to public office now have an obligation to disclose their interests and their assets. Otherwise they should not take the job. In other words, the public has to be brought fully into the parliamentary process. I submit my proposition on that basis.
Mr DEPUTY) SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Aviation) (2.6)-The honourable member for Kingsford Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen) in introducing this matter of public importance today has given the impression that this Government is insensitive to the kinds of propositions that he has advanced. Also, of those who have listened to him and those who will later read his remarks will be under the impression that the Government has not taken any action in this area. The fact of the matter is, of course, that it was this Government that took the initiative of establishing the Bowen Committee of Inquiry into Public Duty and Private Interest. I need to remind the House and particularly the honourable member for Kingsford-Smith that the Government supports the main thrust of the Bowen recommendations and, indeed, has implemented most of those recommendations. This has been made clear on a number of occasions in this House by Ministers of the Government in statements and also in answers to questions that have been asked from time to time. It also needs to be remembered that it has been only in the time of this Government that these procedures have in fact been laid down. Indeed, in all but one area the Government has responded positively to the Bowen recommendations. I shall refer to these later in my remarks. The only recommendations yet to be fully dealt with by the Government are those concerning members of parliament. In my capacity as Minister Assisting the Prime Minister I have recently been giving consideration to this matter. I hope to be in a position to discuss the question, and further details of it, with my colleagues as soon as possible.

2372 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Pecuniary Interest

As I mentioned earlier, the Government has already responded to the recommendations of the Bowen report and, indeed, has put into place a comprehensive system to ensure that the public duty of office holders is not prejudiced by personal interest. In relation to Ministers, I submit that it is a matter of public record and standard procedure that the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) regularly seeks declarations from Ministers in respect of their private interests. Indeed, under the guidelines laid down by this Government, Ministers are required to confirm to the Prime Minister that they accept no retainers and derive no income from personal exertion other than that arising from their duties as Ministers of the Crown and as members of parliament. These declarations are provided to the Prime Minister and kept on a confidential basis as was recommended by Bowen. In addition, it is a well established practice that in the course of a Cabinet meeting or a Cabinet commitee meeting or, indeed, discussions, Ministers declare any interest that they may have relating to matters under discussion.
Moreover, procedures relating to gifts and acceptance of sponsored overseas travel by Ministers have been laid down. These are well established principles which have been in operation for some considerable time. As a Cabinet Minister I can assure the House that these procedures are in fact followed to ensure the absolute propriety of Government operations. With respect to senior public servants, and indeed all public servants, this Government has directed the Public Service Board to require all Government employees to comply with the standards laid down by the Government. I remind honourable members that as early as 1979 the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) said this in this House:
The Government has accepted the code of conduct (as proposed by Bowen) in principle as the basis for particular action to be taken in regard to particular categories of office holders. The code is a statement of the broad principles to which effect will be given, in the first place, in relation to Ministers, public servants and other officials. The Government accepts, as the report proposes, that every effort should be made to secure the widest possible familiarity with and observance of the code.
I seek leave to table an official circular from the Public Service Board concerning the implementation of the Bowen Committee report in so far as it affects public servants.
Leave granted.
Mr FIFE-This circular draws attention to the code of conduct proposed by the Bowen Committee. It consists of 10 principles to promote the avoidance of conflicts of interest and to provide a basis for their resolution where necessary. It also requires public servants to disclose any interests

that they may have in order that they should not conflict or appear to conflict withtheir public duty. The circular also outlines rules relating to post-separation employment by public servants. The Government believes strongly that the service it receives from its employees deserves commendation. We in this country are fortunate to have public servants of such high calibre and integrity who have served not only this Government but also previous governments to the best of their abilities.
Let me now turn to the position as it affects statutory office-holders. I remind the House that the Prime Minister regularly writes to Ministers requiring them to ensure that these standards, which I mentioned before, are implemented. For ease of reference I now seek leave to table provisions relevant to statutory office-holders.
Leave granted.
Mr FIFE-These provisions require Ministers to draw to the attention of statutory office-holders the principles I outlined. Insofar as members of the judiciary are concerned, let me remind the House that Bowen regarded the provisions of the Crimes Act as sufficient and did not recommend any further measures. The provisions referred tb provide that any person, being a judge or magistrate, who wilfully and perversely exercises Federal jurisdiction in any matter in which he has a personal interest shall be guilty of an offence. The Opposition has accused the Government of failing to provide an adequate system for public disclosure and accountability. It alleged that this is causing a lack of public confidence in the administration of national affairs.
During the course of my remarks I have outlined the existing measures already implemented by the Government in respect of Ministers, senior public servants and statutory office-holders. This system has been rigorously implemented to ensure that the highest standards of government operation are maintained. It is also pertinent to remind the House at this point that, as indeed Bowen said, there is a need to strike a proper balance between the public accountability of office-holders and their right to privacy in respect of their private interests, including their pecuniary interests. It was for this reason that Bowen recommended against a public register of interest, preferring instead disclosure to the appropriate authorities-the Prime Minister in the case of Ministers, and heads of departments in the case of public servants.
I submit that what is necessary to ensure public confidence is a system such as the Government has implemented, not an unwarranted intrustion

into the private affairs of individuals. The Opposition has been singularly unsuccessful in this House in its use of smear tactics and innuendo to accuse innocent people. The Opposition's endeavours today, I believe, similarly are an abject failure.

PETITIONS
The Acting Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Retirement Benefits: Private Enterprise
The Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble Petition of certain citizens of Australia respectfully showeth our concern that:
I. The Income Tax Assessment Act (the Act) and its administration is inadequate in providing retirement benefits for employees in private enterprise;
2. The Commissioner of Taxation (Commissioner) denies deductions necessary to index retirement pensions;
3. Deductions for the self-employed and employed are inadequate;
4. The Commissioner has exercised his discretion improperly by negating a choice the Act allows on investment in Government Securities;
5. The Commissioner's constant alterations to guidelines and lack of communication has created great uncertainty.
6. The Commissioner is acting arbitrarily, and without proper notice in restricting trustee's investments with employers;
7. The Commissioner seeks to apply new actuarial guidelines retrospectively to July 1981.
Your Petitioners humbly pray that the House of Representatives, in Parliament assembled, take action to remedy these inadequacies and in particular we pray that action be taken:
(i) To ensure that the Commissioner exercises his discretion only with proper regard to legal principles;
(ii) To ensure that the Commissioner does not delegate to auditors part of his function of administering the Act;
(iii) To enact legislation allowing proper levels of retirement benefits to be provided;
(iv) To ensure that retirement benefits are regulated by a Department with a primarily social welfare function.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Hawke, Mr Jarman and Mr Scholes.
Petitions received.

Sinai Peacekeeping Force
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members ~f the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The petition of the undersigned respectfully showeth:
We believe that Australia's participation in the US-- sponsored 'peace-keeping' force is not in the best interests of the Australian people.
We fear that Australia's involvement may draw it into a Vietnam-type conflict.
Further, we cannot see that it will contribute to a solution of the problems of the Middle East region. In particular, we are concerned that the so-called 'Peace-keeping' force is being used as a cover by the US for introducing its rapid deployment force into the region.
We demand the Australian Government reverse its decision to send Australian troops. to the Sinai Desert region.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Milton, Mr Scott and Mr West.
Petitions received.
Defence Force Pay Rates
To the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The petition of certain citizens of Australia shows:
That in respect of the Defence Force Tribunals reassessment and subsequent downgradings of Operator Specialist Vehicles downgraded from pay level 6 to pay level 4 has had detrimental effects on the morale of our husbands and severely disadvantaged them, since the Governments' implementation of the Tribunals findings for the following reasons:
I. Primarily, a number of the operators gave up the promotional standing to do the complicated course, of which no consideration was given in the findings of the tribunals' report, and no element of compensation was ever suggested.
2. The added strain and responsibilities incorporated by being an O.S. V. also has not been considered.
3. The scammel and trailer are in the order of some 12 years old and require extra-ordinary dedication in operational maintenance to keep them on the road.
4. The fact that the O.S.V. must have the knowledge of special routes, road and bridge classifications and knowing the axle loadings to both the prime mover and trailer.
5. The servicing alone is a long, complicated process far more involved than that of the smaller vehicle ego the 109 truck, for which the O.S.V. is now being paid at the same rate.
The ramifications of the re-assessments and subsequent downgradings are:
I. The members cannot be expected to maintain the faith, trust and loyalty they had experienced prior' to the downgrading.
2. As the Government can re-assess a member at will they feel the 'security' which once promoted the service is now non-existent for them.
3. Again, due to the re-assessment members are given no inspiration to succeed in terms of qualifications.
4. Some members, believing a substantial pay increase had been granted have financially committed themselves to the increase they did not get.
5. The career minded soldier that has been downgraded no longer feels the desire to fulfill his ambition of completing a 20 year term.

Petition received.
Proposed Student Loans Scheme
To the Honourable the'Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that the introduction of a comprehensive student loan scheme will:
I. Restrict Access to Education
People from poorer backgrounds will be deterred from studying by the prospect of incurring large debts. These people, if they enrol, will be forced to amass loan debts. Richer students do not face this handicap.
2. Undermine the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme
TEAS should cover the basic living costs of students. Currently the scheme only assists 38% of full-time students and the maximum level of TEAS is a mere $49.67. Government statements indicate TEAS will be further downgraded if loans are introduced.
3. Disadvantage Women
Women students would be disadvantaged by a loans scheme as they have a lesser ability to repay loan debts, because on average women earn less and have a higher unemployment rate.
4. Disadvantage Mature-Age Students
Usually mature age students forego income to study and already have large financial commitments. They could not return to study if it meant incurring substantial debts.
5. Distort Educational Choices
Mounting loan debts would force students into studying shorter courses or not continuing with post-graduate study.
6. Result in Massive Attrition Rates
Students will attempt to undertake extensive part-time work or overload courses to minimise their loans. Increased failure rates will result forcing students to "drop-out" rather than continuing study and increase their debts.
7. Cause Administrative & Financial Difficulties
Overseas experience shows high default rates will occur on student loans. Government money will be channelled away afrom TEAS to paying off banks. The government will also bear the administrative burden of pursuing defaulters.
Therefore, the proposed student loans scheme will have disastrous effects on tertiary education, which we believe benefits the whole community, not just the individual student.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that:
The House of Representatives will reject the introduction of a student loans scheme
And your petitioners as in duty bound shall ever humbly pray.
by Mr Scholes.
Petition received.
