CONCILIATION AND ARBITRATION (COMPLEMENTARY INDUSTRIAL
RELATIONS SYSTEM) AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Macphee, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr MACPHEE (Balaclava-Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations) (2.20)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The amendments to the Conciliation and Arbitration Act proposed by the Conciliation and Arbitration (Complementary Industrial Relations System) Amendment Bill seek to address aspects of one of the fundamental structural problems of the Australian industrial relations system, namely, the multiplicity of industrial jurisdictions. The broad purpose of the Bill is to foster greater co-ordination in the work of Federal and State industrial tribunals. This is a significant goal, but it is one which of course cannot be accomplished by means of Commonwealth legislation alone. The wider significance of the legislation is to be found in the agreement of the Commonwealth and the States to enact complementary legislation on the matter. The measures proposed by this Bill provide the response to the implementation of that agreement which is required of the Commonwealth.
The concepts which underlie the provision made in this Bill emanate from consultative arrangements entered into by the Commonwealth and the States with a view to finding means whereby a more complementary system of industrial relations might be brought about. Honourable members may know something of these arrangements and the background to the legislation. The Premiers Conference in June 1979 agreed that Commonwealth and State Ministers for Labour should conduct a review on the question of fundamental changes to the Australian industrial relations system, and the Commonwealth State Review of Industrial Relations Systems was established.
The working party set up to carry out this review put forward a wide range of proposals for consideration by Ministers during 1979-81. These proposals were considered by the National Labour Consultative Council. While consider-

ation was being given by the Commonwealth and the States to a proposed national inquiry into wage determination and industrial relations, the review established by the Premiers was suspended. The Premiers Conference in February 1982 decided, however, not to proceed with the proposed inquiry, but agreed that a working party of Commonwealth and State officials be established with the following terms of reference:
To examine and report on steps which could be taken to achieve:
(i) a complementary industrial relations system within Australia;
(ii) more effective liaison between State and Commonwealth industrial jurisdictions.
The Premiers Conference requested that the report of the working party be presented to the June Premiers Conference. The working party reported to the Conference of Labour Ministers in May 1982 and the report was unanimously adopted by the Ministers, who agreed upon its submission to the Premiers. The June 1982 Premiers Conference agreed to implementation of the recommendations contained in the report. I should say that the spirit of co-operation which was evident at all stages of the development of the proposals now reflected in the Bill, and at the June 1982 Premiers Conference, gives promise of further co-operative proposals to bring about a more effective industrial relations system for Australia. The officers working party is continuing to meet and I am confident that further proposals of significance will emerge.
I might now turn to consider briefly the character of the industrial problems with which this Bill is concerned. While well recognised in the industrial relations community, they are not generally clearly perceived by the community at large. The power of the Commonwealth to legislate with respect to industrial relations matters is limited to the enactment of laws for the prevention and settlement of interstate industrial disputes by conciliation and arbitration. Disputes which are wholly within a single State cannot be dealt with by the Federal Conciliation and Arbitration Commission and not all matters which may be in dispute between employers and employees are 'industrial' within the meaning of the Constitution. Outside the permissible scope of Commonwealth law the State legislatures have wide powers. All States have established tribunals to deal with industrial disputes and in each State there are organisations-which mayor may not be registered-of employers and employees operating wholly within the State.
Particular consequences of these arrangements are, first, that no one tribunal, Commonwealth or State, may have jurisdiction to deal with what is in

Conciliaation and Arbitration Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2379

effect, but may not be in law, a single dispute or with closely related disputes, and secondly, that it is difficult to co-ordinate the decision-making activities of the tribunals and to bring about uniformity in the principles and policies applied. Some efforts have been made in recent years to ameliorate the problems arising from the divided industrial jurisdictions and to bring about a measure of co-ordination in the work of industrial tribunals. The President of the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission, Sir John Moore, has since 1976 convened meetings of the heads of Commonwealth and State industrial tribunals. Initially held annually, the meetings are now convened each six months and have been widely supported as effective in promoting cooperation and co-ordination between tribunals. Furthermore, to meet particular industrial situations, such as where part of an industry is covered by a Stat'! and part by a Federal award, or where one of two unions competing for work is covered by State and not Federal registration, the Australian Commission has conducted joint conferences and joint hearings on an informal basis with an industrial tribunal in a State. It is desirable that such a practice be put on a statutory footing.
For the benefit of honourable members, I will prodeed now to outline the particular provisions made by this Bill. In short, the Bill provides for joint sittings of the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission and a State Tribunal; expansion of the powers of local industrial boards-when constituted by a State Industrial Authority-to permit them to exercise Federal jurisdiction; and the exercise, by agreement, of State jurisdiction by the Australian Commission.
Joint Proceedings of Commonwealth and State Tribunals
The provisions made by the Bill are intended to provide a proper statutory footing for the conduct of a joint sitting of a State tribunal and the Commonwealth Commission. In terms of the proposal developed by the working party, it is envisaged that each tribunal, whilst it would sit with the other and hear the matter simultaneously, would exercise only the powers conferred by its own constitutive legislation and would make a separate decision-though ideally in identical terms-in its own jurisdiction. The appeal, reference and review provisions applicable by reasons of the constitutive legislation of each tribunal would apply to a decision made in the jurisdiction.
The provision made in the Bill, while guarnteeing the validity of the joint sitting arrangement, does not fetter the discretion of the President of

the Commission in deciding to initiate a joint sitting or to respond to a request from a State tribunal for such a sitting. Similarly, the President is not limited in his capacity to determine the timing of a joint sitting, such as whether the sitting is to be held at the conciliation or arbitration stage of proceedings, or as to termination of the sitting.
The Government considers that such a provision will be of considerable utility in the circumstances referred to above, such as where State and Federal unions are involved in a demarcation dispute, or where neither the Federal nor the State jurisdiction has complete coverage of an industry. In the latter case, a joint sitting could be convened in such circumstances as where a Federally registered union makes an application to the Commission for variation of an award and proceedings have commenced in a State industrial jurisdiction in respect of a State award or determination dealing with employees in occupations the same or similar to those in which members of the Federal union are engaged, or where a log of claims is served by a Federally registered union, a dispute is notified in the Federal Commission and proceedings have commenced in a State tribunal in respect of an award or determination dealing with employees in categories of work the same as or similar to those covered in the Federal log.
Powers of Local Industrial Boards
Under the Conciliation and Arbitration Act as it stands a member of the Commission may refer a matter to a local industrial board for investigation and report. A local industrial board may be constituted by representatives of employers and employees with a chairman, or by a State industrial authority. New arrangements proposed by the Bill will permit the President of the Commission to refer a matter to a local industrial board constituted by a State industrial authority and that authority will be empowered to settle the dispute by conciliation or arbitration or both as appropriate. In performing these functions the local industrial board will be exercising Federal jurisdiction and will be able to exercise powers under the Conciliation and Arbitration Act.
The principal benefits of such an arrangement are twofold. First, circumstances may arise in which a member of the Australian Commission is not immediately available to deal with a dispute but a member of the State industrial authority is able to proceed to deal with the matter at short notice. Disputes occurring in remote locations are particularly amenable to such proceedings. Second, situations arise in which a dispute at a local level may affect both State and Federal jurisdictions-such as where employees in a single

2380 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Royal Commission into Meat Industry

enterprise are covered by both State and Federal awards-and it is appropriate for a member of a State tribunal to proceed to settle the matter in both jurisdications.
Empowering of Federal Commission to Deal with Disputes in a State Jurisdication
As part of the agreement reached by the Commonwealth and the States it is envisaged that, in appropriate circumstances, the President of a State industrial authority would be able to request the President of the Commission to exercise the jurisdiction of the State tribunal. In such circumstances the powers of the member of the Commission exercising the State jurisdiction would be given by the legislation of the State concerned. Mr Deputy Speaker, it is intended that the powers of the Australian Commission in relation to the joint proceedings and local industrial board provision made by this Bill will be exercised only in relation to such State industrial authorities as are prescribed. As State parliaments enact legislation to give effect to the agreement as to complementary industrial relations systems, the Commonwealth will, for its part, make regulations to give force to the amendments proposed by this Bill in respect of particular State industrial tribunals. As I suggested at the outset, Mr Deputy Speaker, the significance of the Bill lies perhaps as much in the fact that it is a manifestation of a co-operative approach by the Commonwealth and the States to the resolution of industrial relations problems as in the specific provision made. The Government is optimistic that this process will continue and bear further fruit. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
REPORT OF THE ROYAL COMMISSION INTO THE AUSTRALIAN MEAT INDUSTRY
Ministerial Statement
Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry)-by leave-The Government has now had the opportunity to make a preliminary study of the recommendations of the Royal Commission into the Australian Meat Industry. In view of the significance of this report to our export meat trade, I believe it is important that I detail an initial response to those recommendations without delay. I propose to discuss the recommendations in the report under three broad categories. Firstly, those recommendations on which action has already been taken, secondly, those which are readily acceptable, and thirdly, those which require further detailed consideration. Many of the measures recommended by the

Commissioner have already been implemented. In particular, the report endorsed the basic reorganisation in March this year of meat inspection within my Department and the further modifications proposed by my Department in its final submission to the Commission. This endorsement is set out in paragraph 9.1 of the report. It states:
'The Department of Primary Industry should proceed to reorganise the Commonwealth meat inspection service along the lines indicated in its final submission to the Commission. In particular, the removal of the Service from the Bureau of Animal Health and placing of it in a newly formed export inspection service is commended. Unless otherwise stated, the Department's proposals for its new inspection system-so far as they fall within the Commission's terms of reference-are endorsed. This applies to both legislative changes and administrative arrangements.'
The Commissioner also recommended that an investigations unit and compliance and evaluation unit be established within the Department as a matter of some urgency. Public Service Board approval for the establishment of these units was obtained in early September this year. They are being set up in the new investigations branch of the export inspection service and staff are currently being recruited for recently approved positions. The report concluded that the relationship between the branch and the Australian Federal Police will require close attention and recommended that some former police officers be employed. The Government agrees with this and a formal memorandum of understanding is now being finalised with the Australian Federal Police. Moreover, a former commissioner of the ACT Police, Mr Roy Wilson, has been appointed to head the investigations unit within the Department. A separate filing system for incidents of possible malpractices has also been instituted as recommended in the report. The Royal Commissioner's recommendations on an improved documentation system are to be considered by an independent computer consultant soon to be engaged for the export inspection service.
One of the malpractices which the Commissioner found to be widely prevalent in the industry was the provision of free meat or other ]Jenefits to inspectors. As a result of evidence placed before the Royal Commission, I took vigorous legislative action earlier this year, through the Export Control Act, to stamp out such practices. Section 24( 1) of the Act specifically prohibits owners of registered establishments supplying free meat to Commonwealth meat inspectors. Circulars and standing instructions were also issued, in May this year, to all staff and to all registered export establishments, stressing the new law and the already existing provisions governing the

Royal Commission into Meat Industry

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2381

acceptance of gratuities. This action was recognised in paragraph 7.127 of the Commission's report. It stated:
However, the Department has now acted resolutely by applying a blanket ban on the receipt of benefits, and requiring written approval for any exceptions. This is clearly the proper course.
Excessive and fictitious overtime, also disclosed before the Commission, has been countered by the tightening up of attendance recording and the introduction of unannounced checks on overtime performance. A complete review of overtime arrangements is underway which will see more significant management control by the export inspection service over the extent and nature of overtime worked by inspection staff. This raises the question of action which has been taken against those departmental officers implicated in malpractices by the Commission's evidence. Where criminal matters have been involved, the Australian Federal Police are conducting investigations. At the request of the Police, my Department has not taken action which the Police believe might prejudice the successful conclusion of their investigations into criminal charges. Nevertheless, three officers have already been dealt with under the disciplinary provisions of the Public Service Act. In addition, Police charges have been laid in the past two weeks against 22 officers, in relation to alleged bribery at Tennant Creek in the Northern Territory. As a result, immediate action was taken to suspend those officers without pay.
The Commissioner made several recommendations regarding the registration of meat premises. The Export Control Act 1982 contains new provisions to regulate the registration of establishments. These provisions will be put into effect as soon as possible. The Commissioner endorsed the requirement that applicants be 'fit and proper persons' and that this should apply to both managers and owners. However, he recommended that the power to refuse registration be used sparingly with appropriate appeal provisions. He also considered that the Minister for Primary Industry should be able to suspend registration where there is cogent evidence of serious malpractice. These points will be taken into account when introducing the new provisions. Moreover, on the matter of registrations, on 27 August I announced new requirements for the list of plants certified to prepare meat for entry into the United States of America. The new criteria included factors such as the possible lack of integrity and general reputation of the establishment and/or its operators and whether criminal charges had been laid against people directly involved in the operation

of an establishment. The new listing requirements came into effect on I October 1982, and as a result a small number of establishments have lost the right 'to process meat for the United States market. The Royal Commissioner found that the most serious malpractice in the industry, the one which prompted the establishment of the Royal Commission, was meat substitution. I introduced strict countermeasures immediately the substitution was discovered. The Commissioner has fully endorsed those actions. They include sampling and species testing and checks on refrigerated containers. Penalties for substitution and related offences have been drastically increased, to a maximum $100,000 fine or five years imprisonment or both. Other measures involve greater security arrangements for carton, load and premises sealing; the segregation of export meat in cold stores; and the development of a new 'Australia approved' stamp, with stricter controls over its production and distribution.
I will also take into account recommendations on the measures made by the Commissioner. The Commissioner considers that these measures should be kept under review and relaxed as soon as appropriate. In particular, he considers that random testing be concentrated in areas of greatest risk, that premises sealing be relaxed at an early stage and that discretion be used in the application of the new penalties. These recommendations will be taken into account. I should add also that a computerised system of product flow monitoring is currently under examination. The Commissioner recommended that all pet meat for export be dyed with brilliant blue and come under the control of the export inspection service. All pet meat for export is now under the control of the service. Furthermore, I agreed in July this year, prior to the Commission report, to the staining of export pet meat, and introduction of this measure is underway. It is therefore clear that the actions already taken at my direction have coincided to a very substantial degree with the recommendations of the Royal Commission report. Indeed, in paragraph 1.22 Mr Justice Woodward commented:
I believe that, in consequence of the strong reaction of the Minister and the Department, and the establishment of this Royal Commission, the current level of malpractice in the meat industry is lower than it has ever been.
I now turn to those recommendations which are readily acceptable. These proposals have been largely developed as a result of evidence placed before the Commission. I am sure that their implementation will represent additional genuine reforms, and thereby further significantly enhance the integrity of the industry. However, I must

2382 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Royal Commission into Meat Industry

stress that their full implementation will require the agreement of unions, State governments and the meat industry. I therefore take this opportunity to appeal to all those parties to regard the Commissioner's endorsement of the proposals as a persuasive argument for full co-operation with the Commonwealth Government.
The report recommended that managements be encouraged to take over responsibility for certain work performed by meat inspectors and that greater emphasis should be placed on management accepting responsibility for the operation of their plants. My Department has proposed, as a key feature of the inspection reorganisation, the progressive transfer to companies of a proportion of responsibility for hygiene, quality control and product description. The aim is to provide services to each establishment on a fee-for-service basis. This will ensure that the services conform more closely with the actual needs of industry. It will also make industry more conscious of the costs of providing these services.
Improvements in the recruitment and training of inspectors were recommended also by the Commissioner. Proposals to accomplish this have already been put forward by the Department. A consultant is currently being engaged to investigate this aspect of the new service. Furthermore, a staff integrity program along United States lines, which has been proposed also, will be the subject of detailed discussions this month with a visiting United States expert. The export inspection service is being developed with a strong decentralised management system, which conforms with the recommendations of the Commissioner. Already, a revised organisation in each State has been approved, including the upgrading of a senior position. A corporate planning consultant is being engaged to develop a detailed plan to ensure that these and other management aspects of the new scheme are successfully implemented.
The report stresses the role of the unions-the Meat Inspectors Association and the Professional Officers Association-in successfully reforming the industry. It recommends that the Department consult closely with them before committing itself to proposals which could affect their industrial interests. This approach is in fact being adopted by the Department in the development of changes aimed at better aligning the demands of the job to industry requirements.
A very significant recommendation of the Commissioner was for common standards of construction, hygiene and meat inspection for all Australian meat works. He considered that, once developed, a common domestic standard should

then be applied to foreign markets which are prepared to accept it. Special standards would apply only to works which export to countries insisting on those standards. This is a major aim of the export inspection service. However, considerable consultation and development will be necessary before the ideal can become a reality. The Department will also maintain a close liaison with the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation on its licensing and inspection functions, as recommended by the Commissioner. The Department's plan to use automatic data processing to develop a system of product flow monitoring and for documentation has been endorsed by the Commissioner. The Department will certainly take into account his comments on the necessity for broad consultation on the proposal.
On the issue of halal slaughter the Commissioner recommended that there be a single system of certification in Australia under the direct and continuous supervision of the export inspection service. This proposal has a great deal of merit. However, there are many interests which need to be consulted and satisfied before it can be implemented, and I will be pursuing the recommendations with my colleagues, the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) and the Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) as a matter of urgency. The inspection service will give also the fullest possible coverage to the game meat industry, as the report recommends.
I now turn to a recommedation on which further detailed consideration and consultation with the States is required. This recommendation is in paragraph 9.2 of the report. It states:
So far as Victoria is concerned, it is recommended that a joint authority be established along the lines already negotiated and reported to the Australian Agricultural Council.
As honourable members know, the Commonwealth has been striving for a long time to find a solution to the problem of dual meat inspection services. The matter has been discussed time and time again at meetings of the Australian Agricultural Council from August 1980 onwards, and some progress has been made. The proposal to examine the feasibility of a joint authority with Victoria was put by me to the August 1981 meeting of Council. My view was that if such an authority could be introduced successfully in one State it could serve as an impetus and as a model for other States. Subsequently a form of joint authority was drawn up in outline by officials of my Department and the Victorian Department of Agriculture. This form was then reported back to the Council.
In the meantime, however, there have been significant changes with regard to meat inspection.

Royal Commission into Meat Industry

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2383

The two most important developments have been the creation of the export inspection service-a completely new approach to export inspectionand the offer of the New South Wales Government to hand over its inspection services to the Commonwealth. In addition to these, we now have this Royal Commission report, and clearly the States will have views on the findings and recommendations. Whilst Mr Justice Woodward has recommended that the establishment of a joint Victorian-Commonwealth service be pursued, he has also indicated his belief that a single inspection service under single control should be the ultimate aim. As he says in paragraph 5.74:
I believe the best that can be presently achieved is a commitment by the States to the need for a single service and the establishment of interim arrangements which represent a move towards, and which leaves the way open for, a single service under single control.
The Government believes it is essential that the Commonwealth control the inspection of all export products. Our overseas customers demand this, and it is therefore essential for the protection of our export markets. I believe the new Commonwealth arrangements, particularly the creation of the export inspection service, provide a strong basis for a national Commonwealth service. I believe also that the New South Wales offer will go a long way towards creating a climate for a change in the traditional attitude the States have adopted on what they regard as their sovereign responsibilities. As a first step, discussions will be initiated with Victoria and the other States to discuss the views and recommendations of the Royal Commission with regard to meat inspection. Once these have progressed at official level the matter will be discussed at the Australian Agricultural Council.
That covers those recommendations which relate to the Commonwealth's responsibility. I believe it has been clearly demonstrated that the Royal Commission's recommendations either have been, or are being, implemented by the Government. We will, of course be giving further consideration to those aspects requiring consultation with industry and State governments. I remain determined to ensure that Australia's reputation as a reliable and reputable world meat supplier is maintained and enhanced. The recommendations of this report will be of considerable benefit in meeting that end.

AUSTRALIAN SHIPPING COMMISSION AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Hunt, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport and Construction) (3.6)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to provide the Australian Shipping Commission with more autonomy in its day-to-day control over the operations of the Australian National Line. The opportunity is also being taken to make a number of minor amendments which are of a machinery nature and to repeal some sections which are redundant. Honourable members will be aware that it is the Government's intention that the Australian National Line should be operated on a commercial basis in competition with other shipowners and transport operators. Accordingly, the Government has taken a number of decisions with the objective of making the Line more responsive on a day-to-day basis in the commercial world, without in any way reducing the Commission's accountability to the Parliament for its stewardship of the Australian National Line.
The significant provisions of the Bill which give effect to these decisions relate to purchasing limits, directions to the Commission and subsequent reimbursement, charges for services, corporate planning, terms and conditions of employment for all Australian National Line staff, and its financial policy and borrowing powers. Section 16 of the principal Act is to be amended to permit the Commission to enter into contracts for the acquisition or disposal of any property, right or privilege up to the value of $2m without ministerial approval. The previous limit was $500,000. While the Commission will require ministerial approval to enter into financial lease arrangements, provision is made for the Minister to approve a specified class of these contracts or arrangements.
In the past, the Commission has been required to comply with certain government policies and practices which have not applied to its competitors. In some cases the Commission has been placed at a commercial disadvantage and has incurred additional costs. It is proposed therefore that unless specifically directed by the Minister, the Commission shall not have to comply with such policies and practices. Such directions will have to be tabled in the Parliament and the Commission will be entitled to reimbursement of the costs it incurs from its compliance. One of the

Australian Shipping Commission Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2387

major amendments proposed is to allow the Commission freedom to set its rates of charges for the services it provides. The Commission will be required to submit the basis on which it will establish its charges to the Minister, who will have the power to amend that basis; but once that basis is fixed, the Commission can set its charges in accordance with normal commercial practices and against the background of the financial targets established each year. This should allow the line to compete more effectively with other transport operators.
Honourable members will also note that the Commission will be obliged to undertake corporate planning. It is intended that, very broadly, the plan should outline the Commission's objectives and how it proposes to achieve them. The Commission will be required to keep the Minister informed of the plan. The terms and conditions of employment for all staff of ANL other than the General Manager's salary are to be determined by the Commission. This is appropriate for a commercially oriented authority. The General Manager's salary and allowances will continue to be set by the Remuneration Tribunal. As honourable members will be aware, it is essential for a commercially conducted organisation to have the flexibility and responsibility for its financial affairs. With the introduction of the new section 29, the Commission will determine a financial target for each year's operations. However, should it be considered necessary to do so the Minister may vary the target. In addition, the Bill makes provision for a review of the financial results of the Commission during the year and it is expected that, like any commercial organisation, the Commision will respond positively if it appears the profit targets are not going to be achieved. The Commission shall also propose to the Minister the dividend payment which would be appropriate in view of commercial factors including the level of profits and strategies for future development. Moreover, the Commission will be given power to determine the disposition of profits after payment of any divideod to the Commonwealth.
New borrowing provisions are included in the Bill. These are updated provisions to overcome problems previously encountered with trade credit borrowing. The machinery amendments to the Australian Shipping Commission Act, which I mentioned earlier, relate to the appointment of commissiOflers, their remuneration and allowances, the disclosure of interests, meetings of the Commission and the ability of the Commission to delegate its powers and a classification of the Commission's functions and by-laws and regulations. By and large, the redrafting in these areas

bring this Act into line with similar Acts. However, there are two items in particular which I should mention. Firstly, the Act will now allow the appointment of a part time Chairman, thus giving the Government an option should it wish to exercise it at some time in the future. Secondly; an acting commissioner can now be appointed in the event of a commissioner not being available at any time. Presently, an acting commissioner can be appointed only for the unexpired term of a retired commissioner. This has caused some problems for the Commission.
Sub-sections 12 (2) and (3) of the Act, which prevented anyone but a British subject from being appointed as an officer of the Commission; section 47, which permitted the purchase and sale of vessels by the Commonwealth to allow the payment of shipbuilding subsidy; and section 48, which ensured the adequacy of tonnage on the coast by requiring ministerial approval to transfer or mortgage vessels off-shore have been repealed. It is not appropriate that the last two sections mentioned should be continued under the Commission's Act. Repeal of the two sub-sections on employment restrictions will allow the Commission to employ suitably qualified non-British subjects. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Morris) adjourned.
AUSTRALIAN NATIONAL RAILWAYS COMMISSION BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Hunt, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport and Construction) (3.15)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill and the accompanying Australian National Railways Commission (Transitional Provisions and Consequential Amendments) Bill is to repeal the Australian National Railways Act 1917 and replace it with legislation more appropriate to the Commission's role as a commercial statutory authority operating in an intensely competitive market-place. This is the first full scale revision of this legislation since the establishment of the Commonwealth Railways in 1917. Successive amendments, including the 1975 amendment which established the Australian National Railways Commission, have changed various elements of the original legislation to deal with changing circumstances. Much of the legislation has remained unchanged and is now totally inappropriate to a modern government business undertaking.

2388 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

National Railways Commission Bill

The Government's policy regarding ANRC is that the Commission operate as far as possible in a commercial manner. To achieve this it is necessary that the Commission be in a position to respond quickly and positively to the demands of the market-place. Honourable members will recall that in the light of our policy the Review of Commonwealth Functions committee recommended that ANRC be deregulated. This legislation will give effect to the deregulatory measures approved by the Government and will considerably enhance the Commission's ability to operate effectively in what is a very competitive market. This legislation, as it must, provides for ministerial control in certain specific areas and by way of reserve powers so as to ensure that the Commission remains properly responsible and accountable for its actions. The new legislation therefore sets out to define precisely the Commission's powers and responsibilities by giving it a clear and distinct charter; remove many archaic constraints on the Commission's ability to operate in a commercial manner; and allow the Commission freedom to react flexibly to the demands of the market-place while retaining the control necessary to ensure proper responsibility and accountability.
The changes proposed are in accordance with the Government's policy that transport business undertakings should essentially operate in a commercial manner. If the Government requires the Commission to act not in a commercial manner then such requirements should be by way of an explicit direction and any costs incurred should be fully reimbursed. The legislation is consistent with the recommendations of the House of Representatives Expenditure Committee report on the Australian National Railways Commission. The Committee's theme was:
AN(RC)'s commercial judgment must predominate and therefore the scale and range of operations of AN (RC) should be determined by customer preferences for its services subject only to explicit Government directives . . .
I have circulated explanatory memoranda to assist honourable members in their consideration of both Bills. Let me now speak briefly and in general terms on the main provisions of the new legislation. Firstly, the Commission, as currently established under the Australian National Railways Act 1917, will continue. A significant feature of the new Australian National Railways Commission Bill is that, for the first time, the functions and powers of the Commission have been set out in a comprehensive statement. It was very difficult to ascertain what the functions and responsibilities of the Commission really were under the previous legislation. A significant change from

existing arrangements in this area relates to the Commission's powers to provide 'consultancy services'. Previously the Commission was restricted to providing services out of its surplus resources for, or on behalf of, the Commonwealth. This power has been widened to allow the Commission to provide consultancy services to the Commonwealth and its authorities, to other persons within Australia from surplus resources which it may have available from time to time, and to overseas clients with the approval of the Minister or at the request of the Commonwealth.
While the Bill removes a number of inappropriate constraints, Ministerial control in certain important areas will remain. Specific matters where Ministerial approval will be required are, firstly, entering into contracts valued at over $2m-the present limit is $500,000; secondly, entering into lease financing arrangements; and thirdly, participating in the formation of companies et cetera. The Commission will also have power to construct small extensions to its existing railway network when it decides these are commercially viable. An Act of Parliament which was previously necessary for all railway construction will now only be required when railways over 25 kilometres long are constructed. The Commission's powers relating to the construction, maintenance and operation of railways, including its powers to enter and occupy land, have been brought up to date to ensure that the Commission has the power necessary to perform its functions, and that it does so openly and responsibly.
The general duties of the Commission have been clarified to highlight its commercial role. The Commission will be required to operate its services safely and efficiently, in a manner that accords with proper commercial practice and attempt to achieve its financial target, subject to any directions by the Minister. A broad direction power is provided in this legislation. In order to ensure that this power is not used to constrain the Commission unduly in the proper exercise of its powers, directions will be tabled and noted in the Commission's annual report. Furthermore, if a direction results in financial disadvantage to the Commission, the Commission will be reimbursed. The direction power is an essential element of the measures which ensure proper responsibility and accountability of the Commission.
The Bill provides new arrangements for the determination of fares and freight rates charged by the Commission. The Commission will be required to prepare and submit to the Minister a statement setting out the basis under which it sets its fares and freight rates-essentially its pricing policy. The Minister may within 60 days either

National Railways Commission Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2389

approve this basis or substitute an alternative, giving reasons for any change. The Commission may then charge in accordance with the approved basis without further reference to the Minister. Should the Commission wish to alter the basis, the Commission is required to notify the Minister of the proposed variation to the basis. If the Minister considers a change to the basis to be unacceptable and not 'in the public interest' he may within 60 days direct the Commission either not to implement the change or to substitute an alternative. Such a direction will be made under the 'general direction' power-that is, it will be tabled in Parliament and noted in the Commission's annual report. This new arrangement will allow the Commission to respond positively to changes in the marketplace.
The Government is concerned to see that its trading authorities should take a long term view. The Commission has been undertaking corporate planning since 1978. Corporate planning has proved of great assistance in welding the three disparate rail systems which previously existed into a single entity and enabled the Commission to determine and address its long term goals. The new legislation will specifically require such planning. There are a number of changes to the Commission itself. The most significant are: To provide an option for appointing future Chairmen on either a full time or part time basis; to enable a deputy Chairman to be appointed; and to remove the statutory retiring age of 65 for part time Commissioners.
The Bill continues in existence the railway service which existed under the previous Act. I assure honourable members that the rights of the employees of the Commission, including those persons transferred from the former South Australian and Tasmanian systems, have not been altered in any way. There have nonetheless been some changes in the area of personnel practices. The power of the Public Service Board to approve the terms and conditions of employment of the Commission's staff and to represent the Commission at arbitration proceedings has been removed. The requirement for ministerial approval of salaries of senior positions has been removed. Such matters are properly for the judgment of the Commission. In addition, the ability of the Commission to offer alternative superannuation schemes, subject to the concurrence of the Minister for Finance under the terms of the Commonwealth Superannuation Act, has been clarified in the new legislation.
The provisions relating to the Commission's financial affairs have been amended to give the Commission greater responsibility in determining

its financial policy. The Bill provides for the Commission to advise the Minister prior to the beginning of a financial year of its financial target for that year. The Minister may vary this target but this action and the reasons for the change must be reported in the Commission's annual report. The Commission is required to plan its operations so as to achieve the target so determined and continually review progress in achieving the target. The Commission will be required to report regularly to the Minister on its progress. The dividend provision has been altered to reflect this greater responsibility. The Commission will, subject to ministerial intervention if this is considered necessary, be able to determine the. level of the dividend it proposes to pay to the Commonwealth.
Some alterations have been made to the Commission's borrowing powers to clarify its power to undertake trade credit borrowings and similar transactions. While consideration was given to removing the Commission's tax free status, it was decided that this should not be done at this time in the light of its present financial position and the comparable position of railways operated by the States. This matter will be kept under review as the Commission moves towards profitable operations.
The Part of the Bill dealing with miscellaneous matters contains several provisions which deserve mention. The first of these relates to accident reporting and investigation. The present accident reporting provisions have been clarified to ensure proper oversight in this important area. The power of the Minister to order independent investigations of accidents has also been strengthened. The approval of the Minister rather than the Governor-General will be required before the Commission can close railways. As in all such matters, the provisions of this Bill will, as with the previous Act, be subject to any overriding requirements of the transfer agreements. This is specified in clause 74 of the Bill. The Commission is also no longer to be a common carrier and this is specifically stated in the Bill. This change, which is in line with moves recently taken both overseas and in Victoria and Western Australia, will allow the Commission greater freedom, which has been available to its competitors, to provide those services which it is best suited to provide.
The numerous offences provisions which existed in the previous Act have not been repeated in the new legislation. They will be replaced with a single serious offence of endangering the safety of the railways with a substantial penalty. This offence is modelled on provisions of the Crimes (Aircraft) Act. Other offences will be handled through the Commission's power to

2390 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Protection of the Sea Bill

make by-laws to protect railway property and railway users and to prescribe offences including penalties up to $500. These by-laws, which are a normal requirement for operating authorities will, of course, be subject to the same sort of parliamentary scrutiny as regulations. I believe the new legislation will be a significant step in giving the Australian National Railways Commission the powers and responsibilities to achieve its corporate goal of profitability. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr Morris) adjourned.
AUSTRALIAN NATIONAL RAILWAYS COMMISSION (TRANSITIONAL
PROVISIONS AND CONSEQUENTIAL AMENDMENTS) BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Hunt, and read first time.
Second Reading
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport and Construction) (3.27) - I move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill is complementary to the Australian National Railways Commission Bill 1982. Its purpose is threefold. It repeals the Australian National Railways Act 1917 with effect from the date of proclamation of the new ANRC Act. It makes necessary. savings to ensure continuity in the Commission's operations. In particular, it continues the appointment and terms and conditions of service of the Chairman, Commissioners, General Manager and officers and employees of the Commission as if the Act had not been repealed. Lastly, it changes references in other legislation where this is necessary. I commend the Bill to honourable members.

PROTECTION OF THE SEA (PREVENTION OF POLLUTION FROM
SHIPS) BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Hunt, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport and Construction) (3.31 )-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Pollution of the marine environment has been a matter of concern to the Commonwealth Government for many years. During this period, legislation has been introduced from time to time to take advantage of increasingly effective international treaties. In 1981 the Parliament passed the protection of the sea legislation package. The

1981 package included measures designed to provide improved protection to the marine and coastal environments, to provide powers of intervention in cases of actual or threatened marine pollution incidents and to provide a regime which will ensure that considerable compensation will be available to pay to combat and clean-up tanker-sourced oil pollution. All of those measures had their origins in treaties made by the International Maritime Organisation.
The two Bills in the 1982 protection of the sea legislation package will take the Government's fight against marine pollution a significant step further. The Bills will, together, give effect to the Commonwealth's obligations under the 1973 International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships as amended by the 1978 Protocol to the Convention. These two instruments are widely referred to as MARPOL 1973-78. MARPOL 1973-78 is a complex and wide-ranging antipollution convention. It was designed to provide all nations implementing its provisions with uniform controls over marine pollution caused by oil, noxious liquid substances carried in bulk, harmful substances carried in packaged form, sewage and garbage.
This Convention was developed to take advantage of technological advances that have occurred since the original oil pollution convention was made, namely, the 1954 International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution of the Sea by Oil1954 OILPOL-. Australia is a party to the 1954 Convention. MARPOL 1973-78, however, is such an advanced convention that it has taken nearly ten years for the operational requirements of the Convention to be translated into forms suitable to maratime nations. It is only now that the Convention is nearing the membership required to bring it into force internationally. MARPOL 1973-78 includes two mandatory annexes dealing with prevention of pollution by oil and noxious liquid substances carried in bulk form. The provisions of the compulsory annexes will come into force, in the case of oil, on the day that the Convention comes into force internationally, and three years following that day for noxious liquid substances carried in bulk.
The Marine and Ports Council of Australia recommended to the Commonwealth, State and Northern Territory governments in 1981 that Australia should, by complementary legislation, give effect to the compulsory provisions of MARPOL 1973-78. Consultation with the States and Northern Territory is taking place via the Marine and Ports Council to facilitate the development of

Protection o/the Sea Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2391

model complementary legislation for early introduction into State and Northern Territory parliaments. Australia will not be able to ratify MARPOL 1973-78 until all necessary Commonwealth, State and Northern Territory legislation is in place. It was decided by the Marine and Ports Council that while Australia is in a position to ratify the compulsory provisions of MARPOL 1973-78, a further study concerning the noncompulsory provisions needs to be undertaken in order to determine whether there is a need for a~gmentation of port reception facilities. As I mentioned earlier, MARPOL 1973-78 is not yet in force internationally. The passage of this legislation and that of the States and Northern Territory will place Australia in the position of being among the early participants in this very much improved means of controlling marine pollution.
The Protection of the Sea (Prevention of Pollution from Ships) Bill 1982 will repeal and replace the Protection of the Sea (Discharge of Oil from Ships) Act 1981, which applies the provisions of the 1954 OILPOL Convention to which I referred earlier. This Bill introduces new and improved pollution control provisions for oil pollution, including special arrangements for protection of the Great Barrier Reef. This particular matter was the subject of legislation in 1981 which provided Australia with the enabling legislation to implement provisions of the 1954 OILPOL Convention, which are not yet in force internationally. It is now anticipated that the MARPOL 1973-78 Great Barrier Reef protection measures will enter into force earlier than will the OILPOL provisions.
The new Bill introduces new controls on the operation of ships carrying noxious liquid substances in bulk. Specifically, provision is made concerning prohibition of discharges of noxious liquid substances into the sea, cargo record book requirements and cleaning of cargo tanks following discharge of cargoes. The protection of the Great Barrier Reef has been continued in relation to noxious liquid substances by imposing outright prohibition or special discharge rules when ships are within 50 miles of the outer edge of the reef. Differing rules are applied depending on the categorisation of the substance and its strength in the mixture being discharged. In many cases discharge into the sea is prohibited entirely. This Bill extends the effective control of ships from the pollution point of view into an entirely new area, and the Government believes that these new controls will, with the co-operation of the States and the

Northern Territory, further assist in the preservation of the beauty and uniqueness of the Australian marine and coastal environment. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr JohnBrown) adjourned.
NAVIGATION (PROTECTION OF THE SEA) AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Hunt, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport and Construction) (3.38)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill is the second of two important steps in applying the provisions of the MARPOL 1973-78 Convention. In this Bill, Division 12, inserted by the Navigation (Protection of the Sea) Amendment Act 1981 is repealed. The existing Division 12 has not yet come into force because it was inserted in the legislation to enable Australia to implement the 1971 ship construction provisions of the 1954 International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships and those provisions have not yet attracted sufficient signatures for them to have come into force internationally.
The MARPOL 1973-78 Convention supercedes the 1954 Convention and therefore new requirements will be inserted in the Navigation Act 1912. This Bill inserts a new Division 12, relating to ships carrying or using oil, and a new Division 12A, making provision concerning ships carrying noxious liquid substances in bulk. As all matters relating to construction, safety and survey of ships are incorporated in the Navigation Act, MARPOL Convention requirements have also been included in that Act for simplicity of administration and understanding by the industry. I commend this Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
WEIGHTS AND MEASURES (NATIONAL STANDARDS) AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Thomson, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr THOMSON (Leichhardt-Minister for Science and Technology) (3.40)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to update the nomenclature of reference standards of physical quantities and to clarify the functions and powers

2392 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Weights and Measures Amendment Bill

of the National Standards Commission. The establishment and use of a common and uniform system of weights and measures has a long history which may be traced back in written records to the earliest civilisations. Today's high technology society requires a far more complex and extensive system and cannot be satisfied by standards for the measurement of length and weight only. A uniformly applied system of measurement is as essential to modern society as a common language or monetary system.
Under section 51 (xv) of the Constitution, the Commonwealth has power to make laws for the good government of Australia in respect of weights and measures and as a result it established the National Standards Commission. In practice, and by mutual agreement, the Commonwealth has limited the application of its power to legislation covering uniform measurement standards, while the States and Territories have legislated to regulate weighing and measuring practices in the market-place. The activities are complementary and combine to give Australia a uniform measuring system applied honestly and consistently everywhere. In general, the principles expressed in the Bill have been discussed with those Ministers of the States and Territories who are responsible for weights and measures and have their support.
The National Standards Commission was originally established in 1948 and presently operates under the Weights and Measures (National Standards) Act, which was extensively revised in 1960 and has been subject only to minor administrative amendments since then. It is responsible for establishing and maintaining legal units and standards of measurements for uniform application throughout Australia, and for approving the pattern of measuring instruments for use in trade to ensure accuracy and reliability in service.
In this era of rapid technological change, the States have looked to the Commission to play an increasing role in matters such as the promotion of uniform principles in State legislation relating to trade measurements and packaged goods and giving effect to internationally agreed principles of legal metrology. Furthermore, the range of responsibilities of the Commission in regard to trade instruments has increased markedly due to the technical complexity of the measuring devices submitted and the integration of measuring equipment with other devices. The Bill proposes to give legal effect to the increasingly important role undertaken by the Commission in legal metrology in the new technological age.
In summary the Bill provides for:

(a) an updating of the hierarchy of standards of physical measurement in accordance with modern practice;
(b) use of other than Australian legal units in certain circumstances;
(c) a clearer definition of the functions and powers of the Commission to reflect present procedures;
(d) clarification of the Commission's powers and responsibilities in respect of approval of patterns of complex measuring instruments in use for trade including component parts of such instruments and complex measuring systems incorporating measuring instruments;
(e) updating the requirements concerning conduct of the Commission; and
(f) an increase in membership of the Commission from a Chairman and four members to a Chairman and six members.
This last provision will allow two members to be appointed representing industry and commerce. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
NATIONAL CRIMES COMMISSION BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr N. A. Brown, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond ValleyMinister for Communications and Acting Attorney-General) (3.44)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
A succession of reports from royal commissions and other inquiries on criminal activities in Australia convinced the Government that a fresh look must be had at existing arrangements and institutions for the investigation and prosecution of criminal offences of a serious kind, particularly offences in the nature of organised crime.
In the course of this fresh look, a close examination was made of existing arrangements, consultations were entered into with the States and discussions had by the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) in the United States and the United Kingdom in July of this year with law enforcement authorities in those countries. As a result, the Government has concluded that existing Australian law enforcement efforts need to be supplemented by the establishment of a new standing body with the necessary expertise and powers to make an effective attack on organised

National Crimes Commission Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2393

crime. The purpose of this Bill is therefore to establish such a body, to be called the National Crimes Commission, which will be given the functions and powers necessary to deal with forms of crime with which existing law enforcement bodies now have difficulty in coping. However, it must be emphasised that the Commission is intended to supplement the efforts of regular law enforcement bodies, not supplant them. It will be directed indeed by the Bill to work in cooperation with these bodies.
Several recent royal commissions have demonstrated that they are able to uncover and bring to public notice instances of wrong-doing not detected by ordinary law enforcement agencies. These include the Costigan Royal Commission into the Activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union, which attributes its success in this regard to first, its power as a royal commission to require by subpoena the production of documents and the attendance of persons for examination-this power is not of course possessed by the police; secondly, its access to equipment and expertise to enable sophisticated intelligence gathering and analysis; and, thirdly, the use of personnel with high intellectual capacity and special training.
From the Government's general experience and the information obtained by the Attorney General during his overseas consultations, the Government came to the view that the solution to the need to improve law enforcement does not lie merely in the use of royal commissions in their traditional form. The function of a royal commission is essentially to inquire into and report on the matter referred to it. It may recommend the institution of prosecutions; however, most Australian governments, Commonwealth and State, have had the experience of a royal commission recommending the prosecution of named persons but it being subsequently found that it is not possible to bring the prosecutions because of lack of evidence.
A further consideration in relation to royal commissions is that it usually takes a royal commission quite some time to establish itself and develop expertise in its particular task. That expertise and its trained staff are dispersed when the commission's task is finished. Having regard to these considerations, the Government concluded that, while the new form of commission should have certain points of similarity with a royal commission, for example, the subpoena powers of such a body and the power to examine witnesses, it should differ from that body in several significant respects.

Perhaps the most important difference will be that the primary function of the Commission will not be to make reports to government but rather to inquire into areas of criminal activity with a view to prosecution action, if appropriate. It will be enabled to pass information, as obtained, to the regular police forces but, in serious or more complex cases, it is intended that it will seek the appointment of a special counsel who, if pros-
ecutions eventuate, would be the ultimate prosecutor. This is seen by the Government as the means by which the Commission will be enabled to make the most effective imput into the prosecution process. A Special Prosecutor appointed under the recently enacted Special Prosecutors Act could, in appropriate cases, handle prosecutions resulting from the Commission's operations.
Other important differences between the Crimes Commission and the traditional form of royal commission are as follows: Firstly, it will be a standing body able to accumulate experience and expertise. Within its wide statutory area of inquiry it will not require a reference from government but it can be given a particular reference by government. Secondly, the Crimes Commission will be given a wide discretion to conduct its hearings in private. This will be appropriate if there is possibility of prejudice to the ultimate fair trial of a person. Thirdly, self-incrimination will be expressly made a lawful excuse for failure to answer questions put by the Commission. The Commission can seek immunity for a witness in an appropriate case as to use of his evidence against him in subsequent proceedings; but, except where such immunity was granted, the answers given by a witness would not be made by the statute inadmissible in other proceedings. This is contrary to the rule applying in relation to royal commissions. Fourthly, the Commission will not be permitted to name persons in public reports as suspected of committing criminal offences. Finally, the Commission will not be expected to examine under subpoena the principal target of the investigation unless this person wished to give evidence.
The Commission will be empowered in its annual reports to draw attention to patterns or trends and the nature and scope of any criminal activity coming to its attention. It will also be empowered to recommend, on the basis of its investigatory experience, changes in the law or administrative action-for example, that the attention of the police should be directed to particular areas. In addition, the Commission can be empowered by a reference from the Government to conduct a general inquiry, which could include public hearings, into a particular area of concern

2394 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

National Crimes Commission Bill

with a view to making recommendations to government. In exercising this function, the Commission would be prohibited from naming individuals as suspected of committing criminal offences.
Protection of Individual Rights
The Commonwealth Government recognises the need to ensure that proposed changes in law enforcement procedures and institutions do not prejudice individual rights. Therefore: Firstly, self-incrimination will be provided as a lawful excuse to answering questions by the Commission. Secondly, when the Commission is investigating suspected criminal activities, it will be given appropriate discretion to ensure that a hearing will not be in public if the ultimate trial of a person would thereby be prejudiced. Thirdly, a witness will be entitled to be represented by counsel if he so wishes. Fourthly, the Commission will not be empowered to name persons in a report as suspected of committing criminal offences or as recommended for prosecution. Fifthly, the Commission will not be expected in ordinary circumstances to examine under subpoena the principal target of an investigation. Sixthly, the legislation will expire after five years unless extended by parliament after review of the Commission's activities.
By clause 7 the Commission will be given a wide authority to investigate on its own initiative or at the request of the Attorney-General a wide range of matters within the Commonwealth's constitutional jurisdiction. While the Attorney-General will be able to issue directions or guidelines of a general nature, he will not be able to issue a direction or guideline as to the conduct of a particular investigation. The community thus has the assurance that the Commission will be able to discharge the functions entrusted to it by parliament free from any extraneous direction as to particular matters.
I now deal with the very important question of participation in this national effort by the States and the Northern Territory. From the outset the Commonwealth Government has emphasised the great desirability of the States and the Northern Territory participating in this matter. A series of meetings have been had with State Ministers and the Government is hopeful that the States will recognise the desirability, and indeed the essentiality, of involvement in this national effort.
To allow for State participation, the Bill as introduced provides for involvement of the States and the Northern Territory in either of two ways. Firstly, provision is made in the Bill to permit a State or the Northern Territory to vest functions

in the Commission by, or under, a State or Territory law. Secondly, the legislation will permit members of the Commission, with the consent of the Attorney-General, to accept functions or powers from a State or the Northern Territoryfor example a commission under State legislation providing for establishment of royal commissions-and to perform those functions and exercise those powers in conjunction with the performance or exercise by the Commission of its functions or powers under the Bill. In this connection, the Commission members would be able to use the resources and facilities of the Commission. Thus, under these provisions, it will be open to a State or the Territory to authorise the Commission either on a standing basis in relation to a general area or on an ad hoc basis in relation to a specific matter to carry out investigations drawing on State or Territory powers.
The Government sees the first alternative as the preferable and more effective one but the second course, if preferred by a particular State, is workable provided the relevant State legislation is in a form compatible with the Bill. The Australian Bureau of Criminal Intelligence is seen by the Government as an important adjunct to the Crimes Commission and the Government is prepared to support a review of the resources available to the Bureau with a view to the upgrading of its performance.
Another important feature of the Government's legislative plan of action in relation to the criminal law is the Criminal Investigation Bill. This Bill, which was introduced in the Senate in November last year, has attracted considerable comment including comments from police forces. The Attorney-General, (Senator Durack), shortly before his illness, considered these comments in detail and approved a number of proposed amendments to the Bill to take into account the public response. Unfortunately, before it was possible for the Bill to be brought on for debate, Senator Durack's illness intervened, preventing the matter from being brought to finality.
All too clearly in recent months we have seen evidence of the dangers presented to the Australian community by organised crime and crimes involving sophisticated techniques and methods. While in no way belittling the efforts of the ordinary law enforcement agencies of Australia, it is clear that the problems presented by these new developments require new solutions. The Government is confident that the Crimes Commission to be established by this Bill with the new functions and powers given by the legislation and the expertise that it can be expected to develop will be a

Road Development Trust Fund Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2395

very significant supplement to the efforts of law enforcement agencies. Together with those agencies it will provide an effective answer to the problems presented by these new developments in the field of crime. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
AUSTRALIAN BICENTENNIAL ROAD DEVELOPMENT TRUST FUND BILL
1982
Second Reading Debate resumed from 20 October, on motion by MrHunt:

upon which Mr Morris had moved by way of amendment:

Mr LES JOHNSON (Hughes) (3.59)-The Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Bill is a very important Bill involving the collection of an extremely large amount of revenue from Australian motorists. Australian motorists have long been among the most exploited of all the identifiable groups in Australia. Now they are to be subjected to an additional $2.5 billion surcharge on petrol and diesel fuel to finance what has been described as this new public relations gimmick of the Government-The Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program.
This measure can be regarded as a death-bed repentance by a government which has been guilty of flagrant neglect of Australia's road needs. For years motorists have been bled dry by petrol taxes, crude oil levies and sales taxes in connection with motor vehicles. I intend to provide some statistical substantiation of the contentions that I am making. Now we are to have a new tax on motorists which will have enormous inflationary consequences, particlarly for country people, who are also hit by changes to the Petroleum Products Freight Subsidy Scheme. All road taxes on vehciles have an inflationary effect since they are involved with the transportation of goods and primary materials right through to the finished product stage.

I know that statistics are hard to assimilate but I want to put some figures in the record. Substantial revenue is gained under two major headingsthat is, the crude oil levy and excise on petroleum products. For 1980-81 the amount collected from those two levies was $3,944m and the amount allocated to the States for roads was $606.9m. For 1981-82 the amount collected from those two areas was $4,074m and the grants to the States for roads amounted to $662m. In the current year, 1982-83, the estimate is that $4,568m will be collected and the grants to the States for roads will amount to $709.3m. The total amount derived in those three years from the crude oil levy and the excise on petroleum products was $12,586m and the amount spent on roads was only $1,978m. If those collections had been delivered to the road construction program, the Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program and tax imposition would not be needed at all.
Last night the honourable member for Herbert (Mr Dean) said that if the members of the Opposition want more money spent on roads they have to nominate the area of expenditure that they want curtailed. He said: 'Do you want less money spent on education, health and social security? You would have to reduce expenditure in those areas'. The Opposition is saying that that enormous amount of $12,586m collected from the motorist in three years should have been directed to roads, instead of the miserable $1,978.2m which in fact went in that direction. For the sake of the record, as at 30 August 1982 the Commonwealth was extracting excise of 6.15c per litre on petrol and 6.l5c per litre on diesel fuel. There is some Federal-State complicity in the whole deal. For example, the New South Wales Government is collecting 3.02c on super petrol, 2.95c on standard and 5c on diesel fuel.
The Roads Grants Act 1980 covers a five-year period. Expenditure for the current financial year, 1982-83, under this Act is $734m. That represents part of the undertaking to provide a total of $3,650m for roads over the five years to 1984-85. The Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program will expire in 1988. An amount of lc per litre has been imposed, increasing to 2c per litre on 1 July 1983. This will go right through to 31 December 1988 to raise over that six-year period $2.5 billion. What will all this cost? The Minister for Transport and Construction (Mr Hunt) has acknowledged that at a 2c per litre level an average motorist travelling 15,000 kilometres per year-which seems to me a fairly reasonable rate to attribute to an ordinary motorist-will pay something like 67c per week. The driver of a 38-tonne truck travelling Sydney-Melbourne on a

2396 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Road Development Trust Fund Bill

round trip will spend an extra $18 in fuel as a result of this imposition. As we all know, that also will get passed on in retail prices and have an inflationary effect.
Remember the estimate that I gave for 1982-83. The revenue from the crude oil levy will be $3,237m and the excise from petrol will be $1,331 m, making a total revenue in that financial year of $4,568m, but road grants to the States will be limited to $709m. If the Government started to give the motorist back a substantial proportion of what it is taking from him it would have no problems in respect of the development of Australian roads.
Roads have been neglected for many years. Government expenditure has never kept pace with the great need that is apparent. In 1979-80 road construction costs rose by 15.9 per cent and Federal road funds rose by only 7.5 per cent. In 1980-81 the costs of road maintenance and construction rose by 15.3 per cent, but funds increased by only II per cent.
Mr ScottIt has gone backwards.
Mr LES JOHNSON-It has gone backwards. The Minister acknowledged that in his second reading speech. He said:
The Commonwealth acknowledges that the level of funding of roads in recent years has not kept pace with increases in road construction costs.
That is quoted from page 2086 of Hansard of 14 October. So road users are the victims of neglect. They have been hit with rising fuel taxes for years and many other taxes as well. As the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris) said the other day, every petrol pump is now a tax collector and there is a 100 per cent tax imposition on fuel. The fact of the matter is that Federal revenue from fuel related taxes has more than quadrupled since 1975-76; that is to say, they have increased since the Labor Government went out of office by 400 per cent. Despite this massive increase in revenue, Federal road expenditure has been cut by $51 7m in real terms when 1975-76 is compared with 1980-81. That is the situation in the general sense.
We have heard some parochialism espoused in respect of these matters. I want to mention the situation in my State of New South Wales, which has suffered a cut in Federal road grants of $92m in real terms since 1975-76. I note that for Victoria the allocation has been slashed in real terms by $112m over the same period. Last financial year I understand the Commonwealth collected in New South Wales alone $1,300m from motorists. Only 16 per cent of that was returned to the States for road construction. In fact, New South Wales receives only about 16c for every dollar paid out

in fuel taxes. The honourable member for Boothby (Mr Steele Hall), a former State Premier of South Australia, complained during the course of this debate about the raw deal that has been meted out to his State in respect of this new Bicentennial Road Development Program.
