Road Development Trust Fund Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2397

engineer from the New South Wales Department of Main Roads, in a recent seminar said that in the I970s, 36,000 people were killed, nearly one million people were injured and that the cost of road accidents was $1 ,800m a year or $260 a vehicle. They are astronomical figures. Some people contend that the overall social and economic costs and other expenses associated with road crashes are currently running at the rate of $4 billion per annum. Whether those figures are accurate is very hard to know. But we all know that they reach an astronomical level and something has to be done about the position. In that respect, some very interesting points are being made. Mr Dobinson, in the same seminar, is reported as saying:
. . . we must grapple with the basic problem-the inadequacy of the Australian road network. A comparison of the accident rates in any other countries shows that those with the lower fatality rates all have well developed road systems. The fatality rate in the USA. which is 60 per cent of that in Australia could be regarded as a target to be achieved.
I think that puts the situation in very stark form. In quoting from that contribution to the seminar which was called The Community Benefits of Roads' and which was held in the Camberwell Civic Centre in March this year, I make reference also to another interesting speech that was delivered by Mr E. Drinkwater of the Royal Automobile Club of Victoria Ltd. The summary of the seminar states:
Mr Drinkwater maintained that motoring taxes are being used by the Government for increasing revenue. Road users, through the operation of their vehicles contribute nearly 10 per cent of total Commonwealth revenue but less than 2 per cent is returned in the form of road grants, and this situation exists because there are 'no votes in roads'.
This is the interesting point that he made:
Australia now has over 7 million registered vehicles or one vehicle f9r every two persons, and there are only three countries in the world with a higher vehicle ownership (USA., Canada and New Zealand). Over the last decade there has been a phenomenal growth in motor vehicle ownership and usage, and also high increases in road construction and maintenance costs (currently 16 per cent p.a. in Victoria).
This situation has not been recognized by the Commonwealth Govenrment's road grants which, over the last 10 years, have increased by only 188 per cent, equivalent to an annual increase of less than 7 per cent. By comparison other Commonwealth expenditure over the same period has increased as follows:
Education 862 per cent, Social Security and Welfare 617 per cent, Health 552 per cent, and Defence 225 per cent.
These are very high figures indeed when compared with the increase in road expenditure of only 188 per cent over that lO-year period. Although, as I said, we are glad to acknowledge that more money will be spent on roads, we do not altogether approve of the way that the funds are being raised. I notice one feature of this Bill which does not enthuse me. It is the fact that in regard to

the national roads program and I think also in respect of the other programs-the local and urban road programs-it becomes a requirement for the State main roads departments to subject themselves to a tendering process in competition with private enterprise. There is a very adverse reaction to this from the States and certainly from their employees.
It is very likely that what we will see happen is that overseas construction companies will start tendering for our national roads program. They will destabilise the day labour system and the main roads departments in the States. After they have virtually put these out of business, these overseas construction companies will follow the pattern in this country that they have demonstrated in other parts of the world. Having taken a dominant role over the Australian road construction market they will just be able to do precisely what they like. The sky will be the limit so far as the tendering prices are concerned. I warn this Minister and this Government about that. It is very bad to destabilise the main roads departments. It will result in retrenchments in country towns that cannot afford it. I ask the Minister to have another look at this matter. I simply say in conclusion that when a Labor Government takes office we will be using the funds that are derived from motorists for the purpose of road construction.

Mr COWAN (Lyne) (4.l9)-For many years now the Australian people have been looking for a fund that is identified directly with road construction and road maintenance work in Australia. Most families possess a motor vehicle and some possess two. Road Development Trust Fund Bill

Whether we like it or not, we have a great responsibility to upgrade the roads in Australia. We have three categories of roads~national highways, arterial roads and local roads~and I believe the legislation before the Parliament today is a step forward in the improvement of roads.

As has been said in the House, over a six-year period a fund amounting to $2.5 billion will be directly available for that purpose.
Three Commonwealth funds are available for roads~the petrol tax fund, the personal taxation
fund, and the fund that is being provided for in the legislation we are considering today. The petrol tax fund and the bicentenary road fund are two funds that will be used directly for road building in Australia. This year $914m will be available for direct spending on the roads and, in 1983-84, $1,208m will be made available, or an increase of 33 per cent over last year. Today we heard Opposition speakers say that New South Wales is being deprived of its share of these funds. We know that New South Wales contributes a lot of money to the petrol tax fund.

A comparison of 1983-84 funds with those for 1981-82 shows that New South Wales will receive an extra 78 per cent in road funds. I do not think members of the Australian public mind paying an identified 1c per litre if they know that it will go into a trust fund and that it will be spent on the construction of maintenance of our roads.

Admittedly that increase will not all go to roads, but a fair percentage will go to the shires throughout the States for their roads. The funds are available for local government to spend, no matter whether they are offset against an increase in rates or for whatever purpose they are used. The money will go into the coffers of the councils in Australia. The amount available is $350m this year, and I do not believe that anybody will be able to criticise the Commonwealth for the contribution it is making
to roads.

for introducing this Bill.

Mr WALLIS (Grey) (4.39)-Although the Opposition has moved an amendment to the motion for the second reading of the Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Bill it does not oppose the Bill. It has moved the amendment in order to try to improve the Bill somewhat. I am sure that the people of Australia would agree that better roads are something that everybody wants and looks forward to. I suppose that in a sense people may not mind paying an extra Ic a litre if they are going to get them. I just wonder why, when motorists are paying $3,200m in fuel excise, they are being asked to pay more. As I said the other night, it is like being asked to pay for one's own birthday present. I know it often happens to parents when their kids buy them a nice birthday present. Kids come back and
say: 'Dad, will you buy this?' It looks as though a similar situation is going to happen here; we will have to pay for the roads ourselves.
It would not be so bad either if people were not paying such ridiculously-high prices for fuel. I do not know what it is like in the other States but in South Australia the variation in fuel prices is too silly to be believed. I will give some examples of prices around the electorate of Grey. These are all areas within 120 miles to 140 miles of Port Augusta where I live. The petrol that is distributed in that area is offioaded from tankers at Port Pirie. From Port Pirie the fuel is taken 50 miles to Port Augusta. There is a difference of about 2c or 3c a litre within that 50 miles. The fuel is taken another 50 miles down to Whyalla, on the western side of the Gulf, and the price drops about 4c a litre. In Whyalla you can get fuel for about 39c-plus a litre but in Port Augusta you pay 44c a litre.
North-west at Woomera, the Defence support centre, it is possible to get fuel for about 40c a litre. Five miles away at a place called Pimba fuel is about 46c or 47c a litre. I recently travelled up the Stuart Highway to a place being constructed to service people travelling through. What did I see on the pumps there? Petrol was more than 48c a litre. We can see the variation in those prices. At Coober Pedy, which is 395 miles by road from Port Augusta, petrol is sold at 42c a litre. It can be seen from various distances and directions I have mentioned and the various prices motorists are asked to pay for petrol just how stupid the whole thing is.
The Federal Government introduced legislation into this Parliament to set up the freight equalisation scheme which was supposed to ensure that people in the more remote areas would not have to pay more than 0.44c a litre more for their petrol. When we take into account

the figures that I have just given, showing that it is possible to get fuel for 39c a litre in one area and ISO miles away you have to pay 48c a litre, I ask: Where does the freight subsidy come in? It is about time that something was done about this. As I said, people probably would not mind paying an additional cent a litre if the price was about the same all over the place. In regard to what has been said up to date in the debate on this Bill, I agree with what the honourable member for Boothby (Mr Steele Hall) said last night. From 1972-73 to 1980-81 the number of motor vehicles in South Australia had risen by almost 40 per cent, but Commonwealth funding to South Australia had been cut by 40.2 per cent.

. . . our share should be 9.4 per cent, in terms of State area 15.5 per cent, length of road 12.2 per cent, population 9 per cent, vehicle kilometres of travel 9.21 per cent, fuel consumption 9.25 per cent and yet we have to settle for 8.22 per cent . . .

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2403

I make no apology whatsoever for giving such an important status to the east-west road in north-western New South Wales. It is a very important road link between far western New South Wales and the northern part of the State. Bourke has no direct transport link with northern New South Wales other than the unsurfaced and, in places, black soil road between Bourke and Narrabri. It is a road that is essential to the maintenance of power lines by the Namoi Valley County Council, which serves the western part of New South Wales. It is essential for the movement of agricultural and pastoral produce from western New South Wales to the seaboard. I add that there has been tremendous development in irrigation in the area. It is probably one of the most highly productive agricultural areas in Australia.
The fact that the road happens to be in the electorate of Gwydir should not be a reason why the importance of this road should be ignored. To apply that idiotic approach to development projects in the Gwydir electorate would be to deny public expenditure in an area occupying one-third of New South Wales. In no way should the area be discriminated against by such a stupid approach by the honourable member for Shortland. In any event, the road has a very strong State-wide support. The New South Wales Shires Association strongly supports it. A letter which I received on 8 January 1982, signed by Mr N. T. G. Miles, the Secretary of the Association, reads:
Recently, I reported your press release of I October 1981 dealing with the funding of the Bourke-Narrabri road to members of this Executive.
Members were very pleased indeed to receive this information, and resolved to congratulate the Government on making this important decision. Construction of the road will bring benefits not only to the residents of the Shires involved, but also to the whole population of New South Wales through the increased access to markets and safer travel1ing conditions.
The Association looks forward to hearing of further developmental roads declared, in line with Government policy.
So it had the support of all the councils concerned along the road. It also had the support of the Orana Tourist Association which covers well in excess of one-third of New South Wales. This puts into perspective the importance of this road. The road forms part of the National Association of Australian State Road Authorities inter-regional road system which was developed in 1960. The system was investigated in 1972-74 by a Commonwealth-State highway study team comprising Commonwealth and State officers during the time when the Honourable Charles Jones, the honourable member for Newcastle, was Minister for Transport.
Mrs Kelly-A good Minister, too.

Mr HUNT-He was a very good Minister. The team found that the Bourke to Walgett section warranted sealing to 6.2-metre pavement width during the period 1974 to 1979. I table that report because it also puts into perspective the importance of this road. In answer to the allegations by honourable member for Shortland about the disproportionate amount of funds being allocated to this important road in inland New South Wales out of the Commonwealth road development allocations to the State, I remind him that $43m has been allocated to development roads in New South Wales since 1974. The majority of this funding was spent on the Botany Bay foreshores and coal roads in .the Wollongong district. Nobody would object to that. It is true that $1.5m was appropriated to the east-west road in the last financial year, which amount represented about 3.5 per cent of the total expenditure for this purpose since the inception of this category. Honourable gentlemen may be interested to know that only $14,000 was spent on the east-west road in 1981-82. There will therefore be a much higher allocation spent on the east-west road in this financial year. Indeed, the Minister for Roads in New South Wales, the Honourable Paul Whelan, has recommended quite a substantial sum to be spent on the east-west road in the current year. I am currently giving consideration to his recommendations. Indeed, I will be meeting with him in the very near future to discuss the roads program in regard to both national highways and the arterial roads area for the next five to six years.
The honourable member for Shortland has moved an amendment to the motion for the second reading of the Bill. The Government rejects the amendment. The first section of the amendment deplores the Government's failure to make provision in the Bill for an equitable allocation of national roads funds between the States and the Northern Territory. The very reason that the Bill provides flexibility in the allocation of national roads funding is to meet the requirements of the differing lengths of roads that exist in the various States and the Northern Territory. It is not possible to distribute the actual funds for national roads on a pro rata basis between the States and the Northern Territory and at the same time meet our national objectives of having the national highway system completed by the bicentennial year. Some States necessarily have longer lengths of roads to be sealed and also made flood free than do others. The Commonwealth's major priority in the national roads area is to bring the declared national highway system in all States and the Northern Territory to a dust free and virtually

2404 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Road Development Trust Fund Bill

flood free system by 1988. Therefore, no State will be advantaged at the expense of another in the standard to which its share of the national highway system will be upgraded.
The honourable member for ShortIand condemned the Government also for discrimination in respect of local government. The Government recognises the importance of the local roads system to the movement of traffic, goods and passengers generally throughout the nation. We paid particular attention to ensure that local government would not be disadvantaged in the allocation of funds from this trust fund. In 1982-83 the estimated total allocation for local roads under the program is estimated to amount to $200m-an increase of about 24 per cent or $39m over the 1981-82 level. That substantial increase will more than offset the additional excise charges on local authorities, which are unlikely to exceed about $lOm in 1982-83. Benefits to local government will be even more marked in 1983-84 because it will receive $73m more, or a 46 per cent higher increase, than what was available in 1981-82. So allocations are also well ahead of recent trends in construction costs and will provide a real boost to local government authorities.
The honourable member for Shortland put forward the proposition that funds made available for national roads under the program would fall far short of the amount required to achieve the program's objectives, particularly in New South Wales. He must be basing his figuring on a standard that is far in excess of anything that is being considered at present. I do not know how he arrived at his figures or whether he underestimated the amount of funds to go to the national highways area. For instance, there will be a 73 per cent increase in the construction effort for national highways. The exaggerated cost estimates that he has used are based on a blind adherence to standards developed some 10 years ago, we think, and on traffic levels which are not likely to occur until well into the next century. It has been decided that the standards should be applied with the flexibility best suited to meet the foreseeable traffic need and local conditions. The figures that are used by my officers in the presentationthey are not my figures; they are figures that are worked out by experts in the Department after consultation with State officers-are the figures that appear in the documents that have been circulated. They are not rubbery.
Mrs Kelly-Ha! hal
Mr HUNT-He said they were rubbery.
Mr Morris-They are shonky.

Mr HUNT-They are not shonky and they are not rubbery.
Mr Morris-Here is the official telex from the Department of Main Roads-not your shonky figures.
Mr HUNT-They are not the figures that the Department has given us. The figures that we have used have arisen as a result of the Commonwealth-State officials' negotiations. The figures that I used in the documents that have been circulated are coming out on course with all of the States that have come in with applications and with indicative programs for the next six years. The early indications from the preliminary discussions on the programs suggest that the estimates are realistic; so the Government's expected achievements under the program are in fact quite reasonable. First of all, the Bicentennial Road Development Program is a road construction program rather than a road safety program. Nevertheless, as the honourable gentleman would understand, we wrote into the purposes of the legislation a provision to ensure that the objectives would give emphasis to the enhancement of safety on Australian roads. We will be asking the States to take into account as an integral part of the design of the proposed bicentennial road projects a number of principles which are aimed at enhancing road safety.
I wish to respond to a point made by the honourable member for Boothby (Mr Steele Hall) and the honourable member for Grey (Mr Wallis) in respect of the allocation of funds to South Australia. South Australia's 8.5 per cent share of the total road funding provided to the States, excluding the Northern Territory, under the ongoing roads grants program has remained constant since 1977-78. This allocation is in line with South Australia's share of roads needs that were assessed at the time of the former Bureau of Roads. I know that the honourable member for Boothby has not accepted those recommendations at all, but the approach of the Bureau of Roads involved the assessment of the total levels of economically warranted roads expenditure for given periods in each State. The assessments took account of a wide range of economic and social factors, including projected usage of roads, estimated reductions in vehicle operating costs, accidents and travel time, saving, construction, land acquisition costs and the different capacity of State and local governments to finance roads programs in each State. But it must be recognised that while recent studies have shown that there are deficiencies in the total national highway system in all States and the Northern Territory, particularly in the national roads area, the backlog is

Road Development Trust Fund Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2405

not evenly distributed. This is resulting in some skewing in the way in which the funds will be applied to national roads.
The honourable member for Boothby used a particular figure last night. He said that the amount of fuel that was consumed represented, I think, about 9.3 per cent of the total. On the basis of the latest data contained in the 1981 edition of the Australian Institute of Petroleum publication Oil and Australia, the South Australian marketing area consumed 7.9 per cent of Australia's main petroleum products. Under this program the South Australian share will be 7.2 per cent. If those figures are right there is not a great disparity between what the consumption of fuel will be and the allocation to South Australia. But for the information of honourable gentlemen I have met with the South Australian Minister for Transport in relation to this matter. We have agreed to an indicative program for South Australia which will undoubtedly revolutionise the roads program in South Australia over the next five to six years. The Stuart Highway, for instance, will be completed by 1986 as a result of the Bicentennial Road Development Program, which is giving a tremendous boost-
Mr Cohen-Before the Minister sits down, I remind him that he has not answered my question. How does he define tourist roads?
Mr HUNT-The honourable member for Robertson made a very valuable contribution to this debate in respect of tourism and tourist allocations. He sought information on how the Commonwealth will consider a road to be of national tourist importance. Before considering a road for declaration' as a developmental road because of its importance to national tourism, the Government takes into account the views of the various State and Commonwealth tourist bodies, including State tourist commissions and the Commonwealth Department of Industry and Commerce. It then looks to the State government to put forward a proposal for declaration. The Commonwealth thus gives every consideration to declaring proposed roads as being worthy of receiving such support. I know that there are some very important tourist requirements in the area represented by the honourable member for Robertson. If it has not already been done, I suggest that some action be taken to ensure that consideration is given to projects in that area for the life of this program by both the tourist commissions in the States and the Department of Industry and Commerce in Canberra.

Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport and Construction)-Mr Deputy Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest that the House has a general debate covering this Bill, the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Bill 1982, the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Bill 1982, the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Bill 1982 and the

Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (5.21)--Whilst the Opposition will not oppose the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Bill 1982 and associated Bills, which are largely administrative improvements on measures introduced last year, we nevertheless are concerned about the way in which the Government makes decisions affecting Canberra. Most of the measures are belated attempts to redress anomalies which resulted in considerable inconvenience and extra expenses for local business people. Unfortunately this inadequate method of developing Australian Capital Territory legislation appears to have become the norm for this Government. This 'shutting the door after the horse has bolted' approach has resulted in extra cost and administrative arrangements to many businesses. Coming on top of all the other antiCanberra decisions of this Government, this approach to Australian Capital Territory legislation merely adds to the uncertainty and disillusionment already existing in the private sector here.
It is obvious that in Canberra the private and public sectors are interdependent and that government decisions have an immediate impact on Canberra businesses which in some cases have been catastrophic. For example, in the past year Canberra's air fares have been raised dramatically-almost 60 per cent on the most travelled Canberra-Sydney leg. The result has inevitably been a massive slump in passenger numbers and a corresponding slump for local businesses and the tourist industry.
A few weeks ago at his retirement dinner, the local Trans Australia Airlines Manager, Mr Dick Thompson, commented on the effect of these massive fare increases. He said:
Thirty-six years is a long time to put into something only to see it being torn apart by circumstances outside one's control . . .
He blamed the fare increases and the 25 per cent drop in passenger traffic for the reduced flight frequencies. He pointed out that:
No other routes in the country suffered anything like that-even the Tasmanian routes-and they get a government subsidy. Nobody did anything for Canberra.
He added:

2408 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

A.C. T. Tax Legis/ation

All of this has been extremely frustrating despite the understanding I have experienced in the community, so I have decided to retire three years earlier than I originally intended.
That is just one area~the transport area. If we look at the housing and construction industry, the economic downturn in Canberra is all too evident. Building approvals are down, the Government housing program has only just been restarted after a long period of inactivity, and major capital works projects were cancelled by the razor gang last year. Even the new Parliament House project was threatened, and apparently was saved only by the vigorous efforts of local organisations in lobbying the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser).
To illustrate the effect of the Government's stop-go decisions on local business confidence, one only has to look at the message of the outgoing President of the Master Builders Association, Mr F. J. Marr. He said:
My presidency came at a very difficult time for our industry. We have seen, in those two years, the level of building activity fall further, movement of people from the Territory and a steady decline in the confidence of those remaining in the future of the city.
The MBA fought, and I believe successfully, to convince the Government that many of its Canberra policies were wrong.
Two bright lights in the darkness during my time as President were the start of work on the Defence Force Academy and the new Parliament House project.
Both projects will help Canberra's economy enormously in the years to come.
I heartily endorse that view. He continued:
However as is pointed out elsewhere in this report, those benefits won't be fully realised unless the Government has a commitment to the broad economic and social well-being of its own Territory, Canberra.
On a basic level the Government must begin spending on head works such as sewerage and storm water networks, and land servicing now that the pool of serviced land is slowly runningout.
This lack of Government commitment to the ongoing as well as the future development of Canberra is having a detrimental effect on business confidence and investment throughout the Territory.
Another of the razor gang decisions which has caused great concern is the decision to hand land development over to the private sector. There have been no developments on this decision. The current situation is that we are running out of land in the Australian Capital Territory. No decision has been taken by the Government, first, to determine how land is to be handed over to the private sector~if it is still to be~and, secondly, what is to happen in the interim. No money was provided in this Budget or indeed in the previous

Budget, for the servicing of land. We find ourselves in a situation where it is most likely that serviced land will run out in the Australian Capital Territory by the end of this year. This is just typical of the problems we have in Canberra because the Government is not thinking in advance of its commitment to this Territory.
The Canberra Association for Regional Development has worked hard to bring the problems to the attention of the Government. It has achieved some success in getting some business incentives introduced in the recent Budget. In its submission to the Prime Minister, CARD drew attention to the negative multiplier effect of adverse government decisions on the Canberra economy. CARD states the situation clearly:
In recent years there have been successive government decisions with adverse effects on the total economy of the Australian Capital Territory, which had been geared specifically to very large public works expenditure and the servicing of a public sector 'company town'.
There was, for example, the impact of the Review of Commonwealth Functions, followed by recent Budget restraints which, unlike other parts of Australia, reached down to Statelocal government expenditure as well as national projects.
With a few exceptions it so happened that the major projects deferred indefinitely and those put aside for later consideration were located in Canberra.
In the event there was no transfer of any matching resources to stimulate the private sector in Canberra, to offset (at least in part) the loss of job opportunities and work force due to the withdrawal of public expenditure. The effects of such decisions in the Australian Capital Territory are reflected in building industry investment statistics, (an acceptable indicator of business cycle ups and downs).
Of course there are graphs which dramatically illustrate the decline in the Canberra economy as a result of the short-sighted, ad hoc decisions made by this Government with no thought for their implication for business confidence. CARD lists these effects and states:
Some consequences of the abrupt changes in funding for the Australian Capital Territory are:
(a) A movement of unemployed from the Australian Capital Territory, thus 'exporting unemployment',
(b) An excess of 'outward' over 'inward' migration of members of the workforce and popUlation generally,
(c) An outflow of private investment funds and a reduction of an inflow from other centres,
(d) A definite trend towards stagnation and possibly a downturn of population levels, unless a recovery can be stimulated,
(e) A projected fall in the creation of employment opportunities in both the public and private sectors and predictions of future unemployment as school leavers are due to enter the workforce.
(f) A reduction in the ratio of the private sector in the workforce to 35% and possibly 30%.
(g) A reduction in the private sector mix in the ACT economy.

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2409

That statement was made by an independent body in the business community of the Australian Capital Territory. The Government has consistently talked about how, if there are cutbacks in the public sector, the private sector has to take up the slack. But it is obvious from this report that that is not happening. There must be a mixture of the public sector and the private sector. But the Government must take the leadership and present a long term plan to indicate where Canberra is going. After seven years of this Government we have had no long term commitment. We continue to run the Australian Capital Territory with a whole series of ad hoc policies. Instead, the Government should be looking after the interests of Canberra people and taking positive and constructive steps to restore the local economy, not just for today but for the next five or 10 years. That is what we are asking for-an end to ad hockery and the beginning of a long term commitment and plan for the future of this Territory.
Mr FRY (Fraser) (5.30)-1 want briefly to endorse the support of the Opposition for these Bills. They are not terribly important in themselves, but they do represent some concessions to business people, very small concessions indeed, in an environment which has been quite disastrous, as a result of government policies, for small business generally. Nevertheless we welcome them, such as they are. This debate gives us the opportunity to expand on the problems that government policy has created for small business. This has been outlined by my colleague the honourable member for Canberra (Mrs Kelly) in some detail.
I want to comment on a couple of particular aspects of these Bills, particularly in regard to the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Bill. I was interested in this Bill because I have made representations to the Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) calling on him to bring the registration tax in the Australian Capital Territory into line with the exemptions in New South Wales. It is interesting to note that all these initiatives arise from the Government's desire to fall into line with the progressive policies of the Labor Government of New South Wales. It is also noticeable that nobody in the Government has any interest at all in the Australian Capital Territory. No one on the Government's side has come into the House to speak to these Bills and the Minister is not present to hear our comments. This is a fair indication of the lack of interest and the anti-Australian Capital Territory or the anti-Canberra commitment of the members of the Government.
I am pleased that the Minister has agreed to bring these exemptions relating to the transfer of

registrations into line with New South Wales. Unfortunately the reality of the situation is that if people such as pensioners and other beneficiaries inherit a car as a result of a death in their family many of them will have to sell it because they just cannot afford the registration and third party charges that apply in the Australian Capital Territory. Of course, many of them cannot now afford the cost of petrol to run a car. They would not be able to take the advantage of somebody giving them a car.
It may be of interest to the House to compare the cost of registering a vehicle in the Australian Capital Territory. as against other parts of Australia. It may be a surprise to many honourable members to know that the Australian Capital Territory has the highest cost to register and insure a vehicle in the whole of Australia. It is particularly interesting to note that the cost in Canberra of registering and insuring-that is third party insurance-a Holden Kingswood is no less than $257. When we look at the other States we find that in Hobart, of all places, the cost is $143. That is almost half the cost in the Australian Capital Territory. This is mainly due to the cost of third party insurance, not the cost of registration.
It is interesting that the Minister who is responsible for the Australian Capital Territory has done nothing to introduce no claim or no fault third party insurance into the Australian Capital Territory although it has been very successful in his own State. Although there has been a recommendation from the local assembly that it should be introduced in the Australian Capital Territory the Minister has done nothing about it. This demonstrates again that he is more interested in his home base than he is in Canberra. Registration and insurance costs $257 in Canberra as against $143 in Hobart. Sydney is also quite high. with a cost of $242. The costs in Brisbane, Adelaide and Perth are much lower than in the Australian Capital Territory. Even Darwin has lower charges than the Australian Capital Territory. So we are way out in front in terms of the cost of registering and insuring a vehicle in the Australian Capital Territory and this is particularly hard on peopJe'on low incomes.
In regard to the payroll tax Bill. the increase in the exemption level brings the Australian Capital Territory up to the level applying in New South Wales. This is a welcome assistance to small business. It must be acknowledged that in some cases the level has been brought up to $120,000 per annum as the floor at which payroll tax begins to apply. Sometimes this acts as a disincentive to employ people. Employers want to keep under the floor so as not to attract any payroll tax at all.

2410 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

A.C. T. Tax Legislation

Nevertheless it is some consolation to small business people who fall into that category. As my colleague the honourable member for Canberra has said, small business in Canberra has had a very rough time indeed under this Government. Most of the decisions of the razor gang have been quite disastrous to the people of Canberra. I have some sympathy with the Minister for the Australian Capital Territory for being landed with those decisions. In some cases the Government has had second thoughts on those decisions, such as the closing down of the vehicle inspection stations and the sale of the retail fruit and vegetable markets. I only wish he would have second thoughts about the sale of the Belconnen Mall. Its sale is opposed by an overwhelming majority of people in Canberra. I believe this would ultimately be very detrimental to small traders operating in that area.
Today we received a report recommending deregulation of shopping hours in the Australian Capital Territory. Again this was completely in the face of an overwhelming bulk of evidence. A very large majority of people in the Australian Capital Territory, such as shopkeepers, workers and the public generally, is opposed to deregulation, yet the Government virtually pressured the Joint Committee on the Australian Capital Territory into bringing in a report which supported a decision which it had already made. Here again I hope the Minister will have second thoughts on the matter and try to take into account the real feelings of the people in the Australian Capital Territory. He should not just impose these conditions on the Australian Capital Territory as a result of some doctrinaire approach or doctrinaire application of the Government's broad philosophy.
Whilst small business has been made to sufer and has been given no assistance at all from the Government, we find that the Government has continued to give money in a very wasteful and irresponsible way to the Canberra Development Board which has a budget of $337,500 this year. This is the third year of its operation. It has now spent over $Im and has produced absolutely nothing. It cannot claim full benefit for one job in Canberra. I think it is an irresponsible decision of the Minister to continue funding the Canberra Development Board. I think that money would have been much better spent if given to the Canberra and Regional Development Authority or to the Canberra Tourist Authority which could have spent that money in promoting tourism, opening tourist offices in other cities and bringing some business to Canberra which would have been of

some definite benefit to small business here. Instead of that the Canberra Development Board has been running all over the world trying to sell ideas or propositions to people to come to Canberra. It has been a total failure. At one stage it claimed there were 130 proposals by industries interested in the Australian Capital Territory region. Later it said there were 30 significant proposals.
We have had a lot of airy fairy talk about Natsemi, Advanced Systems Pty Ltd, casinos, convention centres and Tivoli Gardens. None of them have come to anything. They are pie in the sky. The Minister should take a good look at this matter and stop wasting the public's money in propping up an organisation like the Canberra Development Board which has failed miserably. While the Canberra Development Board has been spending all this money trying to attract companies to Canberra, companies have been moving out of Canberra. Many building companies have had to move out and go to other centres. An electronics company called Electronics Research Australia Pty Ltd moved from Canberra to Queensland in the middle of last year because of lack of support. While this is going on the Board has been wasting money in a wild goose chase, running around the world trying to bring people here.
In conclusion, I think it is worth reminding the House that all these Bills refer to matters of the sort which normally would be dealt with by State governments. I understand that these Bills were not even referred to the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly before being introduced here. These are local concerns and should be considered entirely by local people. They should not be the subject of debate in this House. The Government should fulfill its obligations and give some form of self-government to the people of Canberra.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
Bill read a second time.

APPROPRIATION BILL (No.1) 1982-83
In Committee Consideration resumed from 19 October.
Schedule 2.
The CHAIRMAN-Before proceeding with the business I would like to say to the Committee that some confusion clearly exists among honourable members as to the method of allocating the call, particularly in respect of the Minister in charge of the Bill assuming his right to rise and respond to questions or detail arising in debate. Page 449 of the House of Representatives Practice states:
A Minister in charge of business in a committee of the whole may rise to speak as frequently as necessary and may speak without limitation of time and without closing the debate. He usually receives priority of all other Members whenever he wishes to speak. This enables the Minister to explain or comment on details of the legislation as they arise from time to time in the debate.
This provision is designed to add to the value of Committee deliberations, not to detract. The call therefore will, under ordinary circumstances, alternate side to side, ignoring the Minister's participation. The Chair will oblige the Minister to be as concise as possible in his interventions to guard against erosion of opportunity for members to speak within the time allocated for the debate, such allocation not being within the province of the Chair. The Deputy Leader of the Opposition has sought the indulgence of the Chair.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith)--I thank you, Mr Chairman, for telling me and my colleagues who are the Deputy Chairmen in advance that you intended to rule this way. I think this will create difficulties which the Opposition cannot be expected to tolerate for this very elementary reason: There is the management of Government Business to be considered. In that context the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen) always consults with me as Manager of Opposition Business as to what time will be available so that elected members of this Parliament can at least make submissions to a Minister. The time constraints are very severe indeed. In fact, the Opposition very seldom would want to agree with them and, in fact, is obliged to agree with them at times just to guarantee that the Government has some progress in its business.
I am very mindful of the fact that the Leader of the House is anxious to co-operate. But an extraordinary situation can develop. We are not dealing with a normal Bill when we are dealing with the Appropriation Bill. Mr Chairman, if you look at the Standing Orders you will note that in the process of debate on a Bill in Committee naturally one Minister can rise from time to time, as you

2412 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

say, and you would give him the call under the terms of this interpretation. I do not suppose that there would be much objection to that. But when we deal with the Estimates in Committee there is not just one Minister; there are a number of Ministers. In other words, there is an exceptional set of circumstances which, in my submssion, is not covered by the existing Standing Orders. I base my submission on this point: If time is rationed-it sometimes is by agreement, in which case Opposition speakers get the bulk of it-and if Ministers then get the call whenever they want it Opposition speakers will be denied their time to speak. There will be an extensive erosion of time for the Opposition.
The Leader of the House thought this arrangement could work. I do not think it can. That is not being disrespectful to the Leader. The question and answer session in the Estima~es debate which the Minister thought possible will now be impossible, Mr Chairman, under the ruling you have given. A debate on a section of the Estimates limited to 60 minutes would permit only three Opposition members to speak. Mr Chairman, you can see that if the Ministers take some 10 or 15 minutes to answer questions, as they might be expected to, at least two speakers would be denied the chance to speak.
In a spirit of co-operation I foreshadow-I understand that it is inappropriate to move any motion now-a motion that we immediately refer this matter to the Standing Orders Committee so that appropriate Standing Orders may be submitted to the chamber to guarantee the intelligent discussion of Estimates. Certainly the rationing of time should be agreed on if possible. But any time that Ministers want should be additional to the time agreed to. When a Minister exercises the right to answer a question the Chair should grant the next call on an alternate basis to the Opposition.
Mr Chairman, I think what I have suggested is a little different from what you have interpreted as being the situation now. I accept that what you say may well be the situation applying to consideration of a normal Bill. But an Estimates debate does not lend itself to normal procedure. Any number of Ministers and a large number of honourable members may want to participate. There is usually an agreement in advance about time. Such agreement does not come within the Standing Orders. The opportunity for members to speak within the agreed time will be eroded if Ministers feel that they have to intervene, thus making it a question and answer debate. I think this would militate against Opposition members

getting what we would regard as a fair share of time.
Without delaying the chamber any further I say that I appreciate what you have said Mr Chairman. However, the Opposition cannot accept it because we feel that it would lead to an erosion of our time. I think I have made our point very clear. My colleagues who are Deputy Chairmen have already agreed with what you have said, Mr Chairman. However, they have a slightly different interpretation with which I agree. They believe that if a Minister speaks the next call ought to go to the Opposition. We do not always intend to be in Opposition. But we want the call to be given on an alternate basis.
I say to the Leader of the House that it would appear that there is a strong urging from both sides of the chamber that in future we consider having the question and answer aspect of an Estimates debate in Committee covered under sessional orders rather than Standing Orders. We could then have the normal method of Estimates discussion and if Ministers wished to participate it would be understood that the next call would go to the Opposition. Perhaps we need a sessional order to deal with this. I foreshadow that we will move a motion to ask the Standing Orders Committee to deal with this matter on that basis.
Sir JAMES KILLEN (Moreton-Leader of the House)-With the indulgence of the Chair, I acknowledge the complete reasonableness of the approach of my friend, the Deputy Leader of the Opposition and honourable member for Kingsford-Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen). I acknowledge the validity of the case he has made. Over a long period I have seen the simple fact that at the Committee stage, be it the Committee stage of a Bill simpliciter or the Committee stage of an Appropriation Bill, some Ministers are more taciturn than others. Honourable members can put whatever interpretation they like on that. But if a Minister, robust in mind and spirit, has a determination to show those qualities by speaking to his estimates, that Minister can exclude private members from speaking on the estimates. I have too much respect for the role of the private member to do that and I am concerned about the diminution of the rights of the private member. If perchance a Minister is intent on speaking a great deal to his estimates, clearly he will prevent private members from offering their views.
The Deputy Leader of the Opposition has suggested that this matter be referred to the Standing Orders Committee and he has foreshadowed that that move will be made. I agree with that move completely. I assure him of

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2413

the Government's support. I suppose it is rather strange that after some 82 years of parliamentary experience this gap has never been attended to. I do not share my friend's assessment-this is the only murmur of disagreement between us-that it is not possible to introduce a question and answer format into the Committee stage. That may be another aspect of the issue which could be examined by the Standing Orders Committee. In summary, I say that I am grateful to the honourable gentleman for having brought the matter to the attention of the Committee. I assure him that we will attend to it with all possible dispatch.
Mr ARMITAGE (Chifley)-Mr Chairman, with your indulgence also I just make the point that I, of course, gave a ruling on this matter the other evening. With full deference and respect to you, I say that you have brought down a decision which I know was brought down with the most genuine intentions. As was mentioned by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition and honourable member for Kingsford-Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen) this matter has been talked out very fully indeed, I can assure you, Mr Chairman, by both the Deputy Leader of the Opposition and the three Deputy Chairmen of committees from the Opposition. We are unanimous in our viewpoint. That viewpoint was outlined by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition a few minutes ago. I do not intend to go further into that detail and to be repetitive. I want to make just one or two points.
Estimates committees are different from the Committee of the Whole which deals with the consideration of an ordinary Bill. The events that take place are different and I think the idea would be of very great merit. In private discussions with you, Mr Chairman, and Deputy Chairmen from both sides, we all agreed that there will be Estimates committee meetings long after any of us are in this place. This Parliament will be going for a long time. I think it is very important that we establish the relevant Standing Orders quite clearly. At this point of time there are no Standing Orders for Estimates committees. There is no written word or publication for Estimates committees. The ruling I gave last night was made purely on past practice. That ruling is in the memory of people and it is not writ. I think it is very important that what is writ be undertaken.
As was mentioned by the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen), Ministers-this could apply to honourable members from both sides of the chamber-can be a litle garrulous sometimes. This has occurred in the past. It may occur in future. It could bring many problems if this ruling obtains exactly as it is. I wanted to make those

points. It is an issue on which there is some confusion. I think it will be very greatly to the benefit of this Parliament if these matters are fully clarified. The words should be put into the Standing Orders as writ for the future handling of what are not called Estimates committes but Committees of the Whole for the consideration of Appropriation Bills.

Mr UREN (Reid) (5.55)-1 want to concentrate my remarks on the property section of the Department of Administrative Services. I believe that if the Government uses this Department and particularly the property section of it intelligently it could have an enormous influence over the development of our major cities. The sad thing about the matter is that there has been too much ad hockery in the past. Very little of the planning process has been involved in making decisions about where Commonwealth public servants are located. More often than not, top public servants have an enormous influence over where they would like to see their departments or authorities located. They have had enormous influence on Ministers and Governments in the past.
During the years from 1972 to 1975, under the Australian Labor Party Government, we tried to alter that situation. We looked at the situation and in fact, carried out a study in Melbourne in co-operation with the Victorian Government. We found that there were 18,000 too many Commonwealth public servants in the central business district of Melbourne. I refer not only to public servants but also to employees of statutory authorities. To name just a few, I refer to people who work at Trans Australia Airlines, Telecom Australia, Australia Post, the Australian Wool Corporation and the Commonwealth Bank. All these are statutory authorities and, in fact, can come under our direction if we want to use our powers in a more intelligent and efficient way. The Labor Party, at its recent federal conference, set out an urban improvement plan. In fact, we set down certain principles which we thought should be developed for metropolises as a whole. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard the Labor Party's policy, as set out in the recent federal conference.

Encourage state governments to focus urban development away from the central business districts of large cities to select sub-metropolitan locations on the public transport system in order to improve the equity of access to emloyment, social and cultural opportunities, to achieve an efficient spread of the transport load over the system and to stop wasteful expenditure on inner city freeways and inner city car parks.
Frankly, that should be done more. As I said earlier, it concerns me that the people who control the ad hockery of the Commonwealth Public Service locate public servants where it suits them and not in the interests of the development of a metropolis. In cities such as Sydney we should not be building in the central business district. As I explained earlier, in 1971 the McMahon Government wanted to build a building in the Woolloomooloo Basin to accommodate 15,000 Commonwealth public servants. We stopped that. We said that the Government should develop the first stage at Parramatta, where something like 5,000 employees would be located, and then at places such as Campbelltown and Penrith so that job opportunities would be created on the fringes of the capital city. A similar situation could occur in Melbourne. After discussion with the Hamer Government, we wanted to locate Commonwealth public servants and develop submetropolitan centres at areas such as Epping, Watsonia, Dandenong, Sunshine and Broadmeadows. I still feel that that is a rational thing to do.
Unfortunately, no work has been carried out by this Government to try to develop the metropolis in the way that we sought. For instance, the Minister for Administrative Services (Mr Newman), who is at the table, has been to Parramatta. He has put up a smokescreen by indicating his intentions to develop a centre at Parramatta, but we know that that is a lot of rubbish. The free market forces that control this Government have allowed the rental market to flourish and vacant office blocks in the central business districts of our capital cities have been taken up by the Minister's Department. I will cite figures on the cost of rental accommodation for public servants. In 1975-76 such accommodation cost $44,191,000; in 1981-82 it cost $96,576,000, or an increase of 119 per cent on the 1975 figure. If one looks at it in

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2415

real terms, it is an increase of 10 per cent. It is true that inflation over that period has increased the cost of rental accommodation, but there has been a real increase.
The Government should at least be encouraging a private sector building program. It could encourage private builders to build and lease back the accommodation, with an option for the lessee to buy in the long term. If we did that-the Minister is nodding approval-we would be able to build at locations which could be integrated with our urban public transport investment. Job opportunities could be created for people living on the fringes of a city, and many Commonwealth public servants who now have to travel 30 kilometres or 40 kilometres each way a day to and from the central business district could work near where they live. That is our policy. When we give people job opportunities we must give them cultur:tl, social and recreational facilities as well. It is one of the many failures of this Government that it has not done that.

Mr HUMPHREYS (Griffith) (8.5)-In speaking to the estimates of receipts and expenditures for 1982-83 for the Department of Administrative Services I firstly say to the Minister for Administrative Services (Mr Newman) that I have the greatest admiration for the public servants who work in his Department. The Minister Esimates that he will receive $92,219,042. I would like the Minister to point out to the House exactly what he intends to sell in order to receive that $92m plus. I would be very interested to know, as I am sure would all honourable members and members of the public who are listening tonight. I quote from an article in the Courier-Mail by Wallace Brown entitled 'Farce of the Razor Gang cuts'. He states:
Australians can only hope, fondly, that the latest major review of the public service announced by Prime Minister Fraser in the wake of the Costigan and Woodward Reports is more efficient than the last.

The Razor Gang cuts, orchestrated by Sir Phillip Lynch-
The right honourable member for Flinders-
and his colleagues in what was officially a Review of Commonwealth Functions, have turned out to be a farce at worst. and false economy at best.
As far as can be ascertained, and that has been difficult because of a great official reticence, the basic outcome at this stage has been two-fold: Either many decisions have, in fact, been abandoned. Or apparently quite often, what has been implemented has cost more than it saved.
It has been a story of things done, not done, and undone.
There is no doubt in my mind, and, I am sure there is no doubt in the minds of honourable members on both sides of the House, that the razor gang cuts have definitely turned out to be false economy. Many decisions have been abandoned, and it would seem that quite often the measures implemented have cost more than the amount the Government attempted to save. I hope that the Minister will be able to assure me about the $92m. The right honourable member for Flinders (Sir Phillip Lynch) stated in May last year that $560m would be saved and 17,000 jobs would go. I am hoping that the Minister will release a few of those details this evening. Obviously, the 90 per cent of decisions implemented have not saved the taxpayer the $504m that could reasonably have been expected if we are to believe the razor gang's recommendations.

If there have been savings I hope the Minister will be able to point out where there have been savings in his Department. The Government has seen that its decisions have not worked. It is ready to admit that the Review of Commonwealth Functions was not meant to be an exercise in cost cutting. The Review of Commonwealth Functions recommended that the Government withdraw from certain services and that these services be given over to private contractors. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) has been a keen observer of this ideology, stating that the exercises are not primarily for cost cutting purposes or for economies.
I will give some examples. The cleaning contract for Commonwealth buildings was turned over to private contractors on the advice of the razor gang. We now find that the cleaning bill has increased by more than $900,000 in the past year.

Mr NEWMAN (Bass~Minister for Administrative Services) (8.15)~There are a couple of matters that perhaps I can clarify immediately. I cannot answer all the questions that the honourable member for Griffith (Mr Humphreys) asked. I will attempt to answer those questions for which I have information immediately to hand. I will let the honourable member have the answers to the other questions separately. I refer first to the $92m that is estimated to be realised with the sale of government properties. The sorts of enterprises or installations, et cetera, that would be included in that list would include the Belconnen Mall, the Commonwealth hostels in Canberra~a variety of them~Mugga Quarry, the National Materials Handling Bureau and other properties. They are examples that might be of interest to the honourable member.

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2417

figures take into account the once only payment of severence pay to former employees who elect to accept their entitlements under the Public Service Board arrangements. I think the honourable member would find that these factors would account for the escalation in costs to which he referred. On the general question of moving what was a public enterprise to private enterprise I point out that, of course, those sorts of costs which I have just mentioned-particularly the once only costs-have to be set beside the medium and longer term effect of moving those enterprises into private hands. So it would be wrong for the honourable member to assume that the figures he used relate to long term trends in using private enterprise.
On the question of staffing in the Department of Administrative Services, I think the honourable member would be well aware that it is government policy to reduce, wherever it can, having regard to whether efficiency can be maintained, Public Service numbers. We make no apology for that. In relation to the Australian Federal Police it is true that, unlike the ceiling restrictions for government departments, the Australian Federal Police has not only been excluded from those ceilings but also last year had an increase of, from memory, 43 or 45 policemen-I might be wrong on those figures-the remainder being Public Service staff. That action sets the AFP aside from other establishments, whether they be departments or statutory authorities, and recognises the priority that we give to the tasks which the AFP has to meet. In fact, I hope to be in a position soon to make a major announcement on other initiatives for the AFP which again will give recognition to the importance that we place on the AFP and its current work. I think that picks up the major questions asked by the honourable member. If there are other questions I have not answered I

Mr LEO McLEAY (Grayndler) (8.30)-This evening I wish to address myself to a couple of matters relating to the Department of Adminis-
trative Services but prior to doing that I think it is

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2419

necessary to say that I find this method of dealing with the Estimates quite unsatisfactory. The previous system adopted by the House of Representatives in years gone by, of having Estimates committees whereby members of the Public Service could appear before parliamentarians and answer questions, was a whole lot more satisfactory than standing here and addressing oneself to a Minister who is not even bothering to listen. The Minister for Administrative Services (Mr Newman) gives one a look when one says that. That, quite frankly, points to the absurdity of this type of arrangement. One cannot get any sense out of the Minister. The Minister probably is not aware of the full gamut of the administration of his Department.
One is a whole lot better off if one can deal with the public servants who are involved in the day to day running of the Department. Indeed, the Senate Estimates committees have shown this year that one gets far more sense out of the Public Service than one gets out of Ministers of the Crown. I think the House-and, indeed, the Government-has made a grievous mistake in going into this form of discussion of the Estimates rather than continuing with the previous system. One might even think that the Government has done this because it is running away from scrutiny by the Parliament. We have seen evidence of that, of course, in the last day or so. A Minister of the Crown has attempted to deprive the Joint Committee of Public Accounts of right and proper access to documents held by his Department.- We are seeing in this exercise here tonight a government of secrecy. We are seeing a government being afraid to stand up before the Parliament and have itself quizzed on what is happening in the various departments. Honourable members are not being afforded the opportunity to perform their right and proper function of getting to what is behind the Budget Estimates. This procedure is most unsatisfactory and most secretive. It is unfortunate that the Government has attempted to hide its inefficiency and incompetence behind this charade that the Committee is going through tonight.
Be that as it may, I know that the Minister for Administrative Services, who is at the table, is very interested in law and order. In fact, he puts himself forward as the first among equals who would like to see law and order pursued to its nth degree. I find it rather interesting that a Minister who believes in this principle has presided over the demise of the Australian Federal Police. There are vast staff shortages in that area. Indeed, the Australian Federal Police, in a submission to the Public Accounts Committee, stated:

At present the unavailability of resources in AFP divisions is a significant factor when it is determining the effectiveness of any concerted action taken to suppress medical fraud or for that matter any form of criminal activity. There were long lead times in training and ever increasing demands on the services of skilled investigators and it has been said that offences of a fraudulent nature coming to the notice of police are merely the tip of a very large iceberg. Medical frauds would be no exception.
That is what the Australian Federal Police said in a submission to the Public Accounts Committee of this Parliament. The Government is not pursuing its responsibilities if it allows the manning of Australian Federal Police to fall below a level at which it can effectively and efficiently pursue those people who are ripping off the Australian taxation system, be they doctors engaged in medical fraud or people engaged in taxation conspiracies or bottom-of-the-harbour schemes. It would seem that the Government is saying one thing out of one side of its mouth about the need to prosecute people and investigate these types of conspiracies, and on the other hand is not increasing the resources of the Australian Federal Police so that officers may do their job. Indeed, in the edited version of chapter 10 of the report of the Royal Commission on the Activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union it is stated:
Police forces, including the Australian Federal Police, do not have sufficient personnel or the appropriate intellectual capability and training to undertake that difficult analysis of facts which is necessary to identify major criminal organisations.
The report stated further:
The forces are not properly equipped.
That was said by Mr Costigan in paragraph 10.24 of his report. The Woodward Royal Commission into the Australian Meat Industry pointed out:
when police were called in, their work was ineffective.
That was stated by Mr Justice Woodward in paragraph 1.8 of his report. He went on to say:
. . . the police officers assigned to most of these inquiries usually managed to impress the people they were interviewing with their ignorance of the matters under discussion.
He said that in paragraph 8.30 of his report. This points to the fact that the activities of the Australian Federal Police are considered unsatisfactory. This police force should be the best trained police force in this country; it should set an example to the State police forces; and indeed to my mind it should be seen as the training cadre for the other police forces. Yet, those expert investigators who are running royal commissions on behalf of this Government-Mr Costigan, Mr Justice Woodward-and the honourable member for Bradfield (Mr Connolly), who is running the Public Accounts Committee inquiry into medifraud,

2420 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

are not satisfied with the activities of the Australian Federal Police. In fact, the Chairman of the Public Accounts Committee, the honourable member for Bradfield, told us Ian Federal Police officers: 'Frankly, ou are finding yourselves in a crisis situation". The Committee had already been told that up to $200m was lost each year to the Federal revenue through malpractice by doctors and that the police were unable to do
anything about it.
What has been the Government's response to the situation? The Government's response has been to increase the size of the Australian Federal Police in the last year by a paltry 50, when it is obvious that that force needs many more people, much more training and a great deal more expertise. The Government is talking about bringing in a Bill to institute a national crimes commission, which it is quite fitting and proper to do in Australia, that commission will have to be staffed by skilled investigators. One would hope that such skilled investigators would be drawn from the Australian Federal Police. But it would seem,
when one reads the statements that have been made by Messrs Costigan and Woodward and by the honourable member for Bradfield, that the skill and training is not there. The Government has an obligation to ensure that the Australian Federal Police is the best equipped police force in Australia. The Government has an obligation to see that members of the Australian Federal Police are the best trained and the best educated police in Australia. It would seem that in the recent past this Minister and this Government have failed in that responsibility. It is only when we have a properly trained and competent police force that the government of the day can ensure that these types of law breaking are prohibited. It is only when we have a police force such as that that the members of that force can have confidence that they can properly prosecute the people with whom they are dealing. The type of people about whom we are talking who are engaged in the medifraud, in the meat substitution racket and in the taxation rackets are not little criminals who are robbing shops; they are the big league criminals. They are the people who are sheltered by high priced lawyers and Queen's Counsel. They are the people who are sheltered by the best accountants that money can buy. If the Australian Government is to prosecute these people successfully it needs to have working on its behalf the best trained officers that are available. It does not have those officers at present. It is time the Government did something about providing officers of that calibre. It is time the Government did the right and fair thing by the good, honest,

diligent officers in the Australian Federal Police and provided the training, the expertise and the staff needed to carry out the job that the Government will not allow them to do. I would like the Minister's comments on that matter.
Mr NEWMAN (Bass-Minister for Administrative Services) (8.40) - Yes, I would be delighted to comment on the speech made by the honourable member for Grayndler (Mr Leo McLeay). The honourable member has misread the reports that he quoted from or has deliberately set out to denigrate the Australian Federal Police or to mislead this Committee. Whilst I do not have the reports in front of me, I can quickly demonstrate why I believe that probably the latter is the best assessment of his speech-that is,
that he deliberately set out to mislead this House. Let us begin first of all with the Woodward Royal Commission of Inquiry into the Australian Meat Industry. The honourable member quoted some paragraphs from that report. I think we will find that Mr Justice Woodward was referring to the Commonwealth Police-that is, prior to 1979-and not to the Australian Federal Police. In fact, if the honourable member would like to refer to the Woodward Royal Commission report and examine it properly-perhaps he has done so and that is why he deliberately set out to mislead the House or perhaps he was fair dinkum in quoting from it-he will find that Mr Justice Woodward said that the law officers who assisted him and the joint task force of the Australian Federal Police and the Victoria Police materially assisted in producing the evidence which led to the successful prosecutions or recommendations for prosecutions that Mr Justice Woodward made.
Let me go over that point again. What Mr Justice Woodward was saying in his report, prior to 1979, was that the Commonwealth Police force was inadequate. We have admitted that. Not only have we admitted it; in late 1979 we set out to reform the Commonwealth Police. We took in other elements of our law enforcement agencies and produced the Australian Federal Police. Mr Justice Woodward recognised quite clearly the work and expertise of the AFP in the Royal Commission but, more than that, said that it was instrumental in the success of the Royal Commission.
I now deal with Mr Costigan. He has said, as everybody recognises, that all police forces in this country are stretched. The enormity of the crime that we face in this country is such that all police forces are having to make decisions as to the priorities of the level of crime to which they will commit their police officers. Again, the honourable member deliberately set out to mislead the

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2421

House because it is the officers of the Australian Federal Police who are assisting the Royal Commission on the Activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union. This has led to the success and the importance of the Commission's report. The Commissioner himself, Mr Costigan, has recognised to me in private conversations, and I think even in correspondence but I would have to check that, the value and expertise of the Australian Federal Police.
I wish to make a couple of points for the honourable member on the work of the Joint committee of Public Accounts. Again, he quoted selectively. If he went back to the report and really examined what the Commissioner said, he would find that the picture is totally different. I want to make it clear to the honourable member and to the House that the system of investigating fraud against the public health system is such that in the first instance it is the investigators of the Department of Health, assisted by their computer, who carry out the basic investigations. But when those investigations are carried out and it is found that there has been a conspiracy against the system or a major imposition against the Commonwealth, there are. resources within the Australian Federal Police which will immediately deal with that reference from the Department of Health.
Dr Theophanous-Why don't you read the public transcript? You have not read it.
Mr NEWMAN-I am saying this very clearly and precisely.
Opposition members interjecting-

It might be of benefit to the honourable member if I point out that it takes something like five years training and experience to produce a detective-the very officer that he is demanding we have more of. We just simply have not had the time for officers to encompass the necessary experience in the force. I point out, nevertheless, that if we exclude the protective services element of the Australian Federal Police-I hope my memory is correct-I think of the order of 16 per cent of the force is made up of detectives. Department of Science and Technology
