Dr THEOPHANOUS (Burke) (8.48)-The Department of Science and Technology estimates in the current Budget are woefully inadequate in two important respects: Firstly, in relation to the specific programs which already exist, the funding is grossly insufficient for Australia's needs in the area of research and development; and, secondly and more particularly, the philosophy of the Government in relation to this area of science and technology is mean and short-sighted. It shows a total failure to understand the importance of scientific and technological developments for present and future needs. As my colleague the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) has persistently pointed out, scientific and technological development is absolsutely crucial not simply for the development of our society but for its very survival. We are now commencing the revolutionary transformation of the means of production throughout the world. The extraordinarily rapid development of computers and microprocessors continues daily to replace many jobs, both in manufacturing industries and in general services. This revolutionary transformation creates serious employment difficulties. However, there is also a positive side to the matter.
Developments in relation to microprocessors create exciting new possibilities for the accumulation and storage of information and for making such information available more quickly and cheaply to more people. The Government has, of course, stubbornly refused to face up to the problems created by these and other technological developments. I refer to the increasing developments in the area of videotapes and discs. It has refused to look at the question of reductions in working hours so that the work which is available can be more evenly distributed. It refuses to create genuine opportunities for increased access to educational facilities for all persons, whether or not they are employed.
What is more serious, what in fact is unforgivable, is that the Government has not attempted to develop Australia's potential in the scientific and technological fields. The Government has failed

totally to ensure that Australians are employed in the research, developmental and production stages of these high technology industries and the information services associated with them. The Government has failed completely to encourage Australian creativity, the development of technological skills, and the development of information and research skills in the population. Thus research and development in our universities is going through an extreme crisis, with the morale of both lecturers and research students at its lowest point for many years. Promotional opportunities for researchers have been so restricted that many people have felt that research work, especially work for a higher degree, is a waste of time. Others who have not taken this view have had to work in intolerable circumstances and maintain their creativity in conditions which research scientists in other parts of the world do not have to put up with.
This is illustrated by the Government's Budget estimates. For example, research grants and fellowships-the Australian Research Grants Committee scheme-are up by 9 per cent from $18m to $19.6m; not even the rate of inflation. It is totally woeful. Yet the ARGC scheme represents very good value for money. The failure to increase this value in real money terms is scandalous. Furthermore, the amount appropriated for the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives projects was $32.68m in 1981-82. But only 37 per cent of that appropriation was spent. What was the Minister for Science and Technology (Mr Thomson) doing? Dreaming? I put it to him that his responsibilities in this area have not been met. Treasury would give no firm commitment earlier this year that the scheme would be funded for this and subsequent years. As a result the Industrial Research and Development Incentives Board felt unable to approve projects which were due to run for three four-year periods and applicants were dissuaded from going ahead with projects or even from applying. Until now we have been unable to obtain any confirmation of just how severe has been the effect of Treasury pressure. Given the needs of Australia in research and development, this is an absolute scandal.
The actual amount appropriated for 1982-83 is $32.8m, an increase of 0.3 per cent, or in actual terms a 10 per cent fall when inflation is taken into account. In the IRDI public interest projects only 40 per cent, or $2.4m of the amount actually appropriated, which was $6m, was spent in 1981-82. The amount appropriated this year is still less than last year's figure, at a time when we are facing an enormous challenge in the area of

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2423

technological development and scientific research. What is going on with this Government in relation to these activities? Let me take another area. The grant to the Industrial Design Council of Australia is to be abolished altogether. In Division 540, the 1982-83 appropriation is down 0.4 per cent on the 1981-82 figure but it is up 34 per cent on the 1981-82 expenditure. On past form this increase must be regarded as totally imaginary.
. The Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation, which spent every cent appropriated for it in 1981-82, has had an increase of only 12 per cent in 1982-83. This is an absurd level of increase, given the work and activities of the CSIRO. To follow up this point in relation to the CSIRO, there is no getting away from the total failure of this Government to have a coordinated approach to the whole area of research and development. Everyone knows the great work the CSIRO does, notwithstanding its limited resources and the failure of the Government properly to support it. Indeed, it is appropriate to say that creativity in Australia, both in the arts and the sciences, has been remarkable and continues to be remarkable, notwithstanding the efforts of the Government to stultify and frustrate it.
The tragedy is that even when Australians have created new products, new devices and new approaches in the production of goods and services, this Government has failed totally to take up those inventions and ideas and put them into practice. Instead, it has relied entirely on the notorious mythology of the wonders of the private sector. It has relied entirely on the view that, somehow, the private sector is imaginative and creative whereas the public sector is not. In reality, the reverse is true in relation to research and development. Most of the research takes place in the public sector. However, it is often the case that when products or inventions are put forward they are not taken up. This is because of cowardice and lack of imagination in many sectors of private industry. I should add that in some cases patents of the CSIRO have been taken over by foreign corporations, not to be produced in Australia but to be produced in other countries. So Australians have been paying for the research and development of products which are then produced elsewhere. This is a disgraceful situation which reflects the stupidity of the economic approach taken by this Government.
In order to solve these problems it is necessary for Australia to develop a co-ordinated program in the areas of high technology. We need to think

of a whole process, from research and development right through to the production process. This means that if the private sector is not prepared to enter into such projects then the Government must be prepared to put forward capital to ensure that these dev,elopments take place. This can be done either by direct establishment of corporations or bodies to take up venture'capital in these areas or, alternatively, by further encouragement of the private sector so that it will be prepared to go into those areas. The science and technology policy of the Australian Labor Party is concerned with this; these are its aims. These aims will be outlined by the honourable member for Lalor in a few weeks time and they will show up the stupidity and absurdity of the Government's approach, which is totally unsatisfactory.
Can we afford to allow our new ideas and inventions simply to be taken over by others? Can we afford to allow to continue this haphazard situation that exists at the moment, where there is no co-ordination of research, development and production? Certainly we cannot. Indeed, if one looks at the Government's policies one often gains the impression that it has no idea of the character of the changes that are taking place in our society. Alvin TofHer in his book Future Shock referred to the situation where the individual is unable to keep pace with technological and other changes which are taking place in society. This Government suffers from future shock.
The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Dr Jenkins)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr WHITE (McPherson) (8.58)-1 enter this debate on a very narrow subject but one which is causing me a considerable amount of concern. I refer to the decline in high technology development in this country. If we look at the electronics industry, and I have before me some figures for which I am indebted to the Electronics Industry Advisory Council, in 1970-71 approximately 35,000 people were employed in that industry but by 1980 the figure had dropped to approximately 20,000. That is a decline of 15,000 people over a base of 35,000, which is significant in anybody's terms. Over the same period, in 1970 Amalgamated Wireless (Australasia) Ltd employed 7,000 people, but by 1980 this figure had dropped to 4,200. I regret to say that the indications are that this decline in employment in these high technology areas is continuing, and there are many reasons for it.
Australia is a small country with a small domestic population, which in turn means a small domestic market. It is true, as other speakers have

2424 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

said, that neither the Government nor industry place enough emphasis as a matter of policy on research and development in this country. The fact is that it is much cheaper to import many of the parts that are needed. I believe there is also a general lack of education and understanding in our schools and colleges on the impact and importance of these high technology areas. For all those reasons I do not think that we can really afford to allow this decline to continue. It is not in Australia's interests in high technology or in any other field of manufacture to be totally dependent on overseas countries. We have all seen from time to time what happens when we become dependent on people and they choose to cut off the supply. If we do become dependent the time will come when we will want something and we will not be able to get it. From a defence point of view, apart from anything else, such a situation is
disastrous.
If we look at the imports of office machines and automatic data processing equipment, we find the same sorts of figures that I have just mentioned. In 1981 we imported roughly, on the figures that are available to me, some $466m worth of data processing equipment. This year, in the nine months to March, that amount has risen to nearly $600m. That is an enormous amount of capital to be going out of this country, a lot of which we hope, with some energy and determination, we could be manufacturing in this country. I think it is realistic to talk about the development of high technology industry in Australia in the same way as we talked about the development of a car industry in the post-World War II period. The time has come to encourage the high technology industries in the same way that we deliberately set out as a matter of policy in this country to encourage and establish a motor car manufacturing industry in 1946-47.
It is unrealistic to believe that we are going to compete with some of the well-established industries in other parts of the world, such as Japan and America, where they have long high volume runs. But there are specialised areas where we can, for example, design and produce components and manufacture data processing machines. Computer systems have been developed in Australia and manufactured in this country. They have been examined overseas and favourably received and there are certainly some select areas in which our expertise can be developed. It is up to us in this Parliament and elsewhere to give encouragement to the groundwork that has already been laid and to develop it further.
The Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation deserves congratulations,

as do two or three universities in Australia where such strides have been made. I would particularly like to congratulate the Minister who is responsible in this field, the Minister for Science and Technology (Mr Thomson) who is at the table, for the efforts he has put into this area in the last year or so. He has encouraged an awareness of high technology requirements in Australia. Some industries in Australia also deserve congratulations and encouragement. We would all like to see considerably more encCJUragement by those industries and certainly a lot more money spent on research and development in Australia. We need-it has been mentioned and I agree with it-a more comprehensive high technology policy for the whole of Australia. The Government probably has to undertake this responsibility to co-ordinate such a policy. I believe the Government will also have to look very seriously at the level of its research and development funding to industry and universities in this country.
I would like to expand a little on universities. I make this point in conclusion. We are not only talking about universities. I believe the time has come for the Government, as a matter of policy and as a program, to put computers in every school in this country.
Mr Dawkins-You have been reading my policy.
Mr WHITE-I am not unaware that statements have been made by members of the Australian Labor Party on this matter. I do not intend to dispute that here. I agree with them. I think this is something that we should be taking up. The provision of computer training facilities in schools and other institutions in Australia has become a matter of necessity. In the 1960s the Federal Government funded a program for science laboratories in schools and institutions throughout Australia. In the 1970s we undertook a similar program for libraries. I believe the time has now come for a similar program for computer training in our schools. There is a general lack of understanding in the community about the use of computers. Some of us are perhaps a little old to fully understand and grasp the concept of what they are capable of in the future, but we are surely not too old to encourage the youth of this country to be aware and to take up this particularly important field. The field in the computer industry lies with our young people.
A similar program has been introduced in schools in the United Kingdom in the last three or four years and I understand also in New Zealnd. It will be an expensive program. It would cost perhaps up to $15,000 at today's prices for a school to

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2425

put in sufficient terminals and computers to make it a worthwhile program. 1do not imagine that the Commonwealth Government would have to pick up the whole tab. The States and the schools can assist, as they have done in the last few years, by raising funds in many schools in Australia to put in these computers. 1 believe if we do not grasp this high technology nettle we are in danger in due course of reverting to a gigantic farm in this country. We have seen our standard of living slip over the years. One of the reasons is that if we do not become competitive in modern technology, we will have problems in the future. We cannot afford to ignore this field. We should be creating jobs in this country if we want to develop the technology for the future of Australia and if we want to encourage understanding and training in our schools and other institutions. I have already written to the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Minister for Education (Senator Peter Baume) on this matter. 1 hope the Government will take up this project, have a look at it and adopt it.
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (9.6)-1 want to make a contribution on behalf of the Opposition in this debate on the estimates for the Department of Science and Technology. In doing so I want to acknowledge formally the absence of my colleague the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) who is now in my home town of Perth.
Mr Thomson-We are very disappointed he is not here.

Mr DAWKINS-This is a 10 per cent fall at a time when the rest of the world is going forwards. It has to be acknowledged that 10 per cent in money terms represents something like at least a 20 per cent fall in real terms once inflation is taken into account. When considering Australia's performance in this regard we have to consider what is happening in the rest of the world. For instance, Canada has undertaken a doubling of its research and development expenditure for the period 1979 to 1985.
The President of France, President Mitterand, has committed himself and his Government to a doubling of his country's expenditure during his first seven-year term from 1981 to 1988. If we look at West Germany and at the United States of America we find that there have been quite substantial increases of 7 per cent and 9 per cent respectively in relation to their 1982 research and development budget allocations. What is particularly astonishing about Australia's preparedness to oversee a situation where the public expenditure on research and development is going backwards at a time when the expenditure on research and development in the rest of the world is going forwards at quite a rapid rate is that even the leaders of the rest of the developed countries have recognised the importance of this activity. Frank Press said this when writing of the recent summit conference at Versailles:

Something extraordinary happened at the recent summit conference at Versailles: science and technology were explicitly recognised as essential to the future welfare of the industrialised nations. Moreover, they are already on the agenda of the next heads of state conference in 1983 in the United States . . .
It is intriguing, to say the least, that the rest of the countries of the industrialised world have this matter on the top of their agenda in relation to international discussions about the future of the international economy. Those countries are individually taking action to expand their activities in this regard whereas this Government is apparently prepared to accept a 10 per cent cut, or a cut of 20 per cent in real terms, in the amount of public expenditure on general and scientific research. What has to be acknowledged is that Australia, perhaps more than many of these other countries, relies very much on its ability to develop its own latent capacities, the resources of our people. We will be able to develop those capacities only if we make some very definite and deliberate commitments to the development of scientific and general research. Regrettably, at a time when the rest of the world is doing this, Australia, of course, is going backwards. It is not just a matter of concentrating on research; it is a matter also of making a national investment in the education of our people and in the educational institutions of our country. Again we find that Australia is falling behind in so many regards. When we look at

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2427

the matter of participation in education, particularly at the senior secondary levels and also at the tertiary levels, we find that we are falling behind when other countries are going ahead in terms of the number of young people who are involved in full time education.
There are a few specific matters which I want to put to the Minister for Science and Technology. I hope that he will quite specifically respond to them. Will he explain what has happened to the Patents Amendment Bill which was introduced in this House in 1981 and discussed on 29 April 1981 together with the Designs Amendment Bill and the Trade Marks Amendment Bill? The other two Bills that I have mentioned have been passed by the Parliament whilst the Patents Amendment Bill has apparently been lost. Secondly, what has happended to the report of the Antarctic Research Policy Advisory Committee chaired by Professor David Caro which covered the period 1979 to 1981 ? Why is it that that report has apparently not been tabled when we understand that it has been in the hands of the Minister since the beginning of this year? We want to know why that report has not been tabled. We ask the Minister to respond to that point this evening.
Thirdly, we want to know from the Minister what positive steps the Government is taking to promote work in biotechnology. Canada, for instance, has pledged a- $500m investment over a 10-year period in this area. What is Australia doing? We have to have regard particularly to the fact that Australia's expertise in many of the areas associated with biotechnology is really quite well advanced. In relation to agriculture, some aspects of medical technology and some aspects of alternative energy sources we have to ask the Minister what he is doing to encourage that sort of activity in Australia. The last thing I want to refer to quite specifically is the urgent need for the promotion and encouragement of some additional research activities particularly in our universities and also in colleges of advanced education. What we have at the moment is a national program to support post-graduate research which supports students working towards masters degrees and doctorates of philosophy. But there is nothing to support graduates once they have completed those programs. A large number of Ph.D graduates are walking around this country with nothing terribly positive to do in relation to research activities. We on this side of the chamber, at least, believe that the Government could do a great deal by encouraging Ph.D graduates to continue their work in relation to research activities in universities and to some extent in CAEs as well.

The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Dr Jenkins)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr FREE (Macquarie) (9.16)-I rise this evening along with my colleagues on the Australian Labor Party side of the chamber to express disappointment at what this Budget sets out to achieve in the field of science and technology in 1982-83. Whilst noting the overall increase of 15 per cent in the 1982-83 appropriation over the expenditure in 1981-82, I point out that half of this apparent increase is, in fact, the result of underspending last year when $28.6m allocated was not spent. The increase in appropriation of only 7.8 per cent this year compared to the appropriation of last year is, of course, far less than the rate of inflation. It is an indication of the low priority that this Government gives to science and technology.
A further indication of the low priority given to the Department of Science and Technology by this Government is the cut in staffing. Salaries, I note, are down by some 14 per cent. That is, of course, a consequence of the mauling inflicted on this Department by the razor gang. This Department was a particular object of attention by the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions. The Department in fact rated three pages in the report of the razor gang. I remind the Committee that among the etTects of the treatment meted out to this Department by the razor gang were the following: Eleven advisory bodies were abolished, eight committees were amalgamated, four activities were terminated and seven activities were phased out or transferred. I put it to the Committee that this is false economy. It is an illustration that science and technology under this Government simply do not matter.
I draw the Committee's attention to the crucial role that the development of skills can play in revitalising the national economy I want to highlight the Budget's failure to encourage the development of skills and innovation. Between 1955 and 1982 the nations with the greatest advances in per capita gross national product may have lacked mineral resources but they put an emphasis on the development of the skills of their people. The nations which have relied on their mineral wealth and neglected skills have declined. Less than 5 per cent of Australia's export earnings can be attributed to research, design, innovation or invention. On the other hand, more than 60 per cent of the export earnings of countries like Japan, France and Sweden can be attributed to intellectual skills. Their skill-based earnings will grow. As long as this country sutTers from policies which fail to encourage research and innovation

Mr FREE-I thank the Minister and the Committee. This third table shows the numbers of patents granted in Australia over the years from 1975 to 1980. Whilst total applications grew slightly from 14,000 to 15,936, those with complete specifications lodged and those with complete specifications accepted declined over that period. Those with complete specifications accepted, for example, declined from 11,473 in 1975 to 7,327 in 1980. Our position certainly has not improved since that time and those figures suggest that, in fact, our position has declined.
There has been a lack of encouragement. By way of illustration I refer to the estimates figures for industrial research and development incentives grants. Expenditure in this area in 1980-81 was $50.7m. The expenditure in 1981-82 was down to $24.2m and the amount estimated for this financial year is $49.lm. Superficially, this Budget provides for an increase of $24.9m over last year. When one looks at the details of the Budget Papers one finds that in 1981-82, $49.2m was allocated but that only $24.2m of that amount was actually spent-less than half of the amount was actually spent. In fact, when one looks more closely at the project grants under this scheme as distinct from the commencement grants one discoves that, of the $32.7m appropriated last year, only $12m or 37 per cent was actually spent. We need to know the reasons for this underspending. We need to be in a position to judge whether the $49.1 m allocated for this year is another rubbery Budget figure. It appears to me that it is a very rubbery figure indeed, because the scheme is due to expire in 1986 and it will be subject to a review at some time before that date. In the absence of any guarantee or even reasonable expectation of continuity of support in what is, after all, essentially a dollar for dollar arrangement, it is hardly surprising that potential applicants are viewing the scheme with something rather less than wild enthusiasm.
I note that the Technology and Innovatron Program has been allocated $700,000, an allocation which is down in real terms on what it was last year. Research grants have received an increase of $2m, which will not even keep up with inflation. This is occurring in a year when the Australian Research Grants Committee has reported an increase from 292 to 372 in the number of good quality projects which have had to be rejected. I

ask the Committee: How many potential employment generating opportunities have been lost in those 372 rejected projects?
In commenting on these estimates I want to refer to the Minister's speech at the tabling of the science and technology statement on 25 February this year. It was a sad speech in many ways-a rather curious amalgam of good intentions and gloomy predictions. In February of this year the Minister said:
A nation without innovation succumbs to deterioration and decay.
We agree. He stated:
The development of our own capability in industrial innovation must remain a high priority.
We agree. He went on to state:
Our vitality as a nation, and our ability to respond to rapidly changing demands, depends on our success in this task.
We agree; they are all fine intentions indeed. But what about the reality? Elsewhere in the speech we find the reality. The Minister stated:
The ratio of private sector to government funding of research and development remains a matter of concern to the Government. In real terms, Rand D expenditure by private enterprise fell by nearly 50 per cent between 1973-74 and 1976-77. There was a small growth of 5 per cent in real terms between 1976-77 and 1978-79. However, in the same period, manpower effort devoted to Rand D by private enterprise fell by 7 per cent. Private sector Rand D spending as a percentage of gross domestic product remains very low compared to other Western countries.
Despite this poor performance all the Minister did then was to wring his hands. He said:
Industry must take the primary role in the innovation process.
Quite clearly industry is not and, equally clearly, it will get no lead from this Government. It is clear that the Government is not interested in this field. It is not doing enough. I want to pass particular comment on the content of this debate compared with that of last year's Estimates committee hearings on the estimates for the Department of Science and Technology. I am sure the Minister would agree with me that last year's Estimates committee proceedings for this Department were very good. I recall them very clearly. There was good discussion, there were good people from the Department and there was a free flow of information and questions. We had a very good evening on that occasion. This year the arrangements are far less satisfactory. We have had, I think, three people from the Labor Party side speak tonight. We had one speaker from the Government back bench who did not even use his full allocation of time. We had a thoughful contribution by the honourable member for McPherson

(Mr White). But really and truly, if that represents the combined wisdom of the Government parties back bench, I am very sorry for them.

Mr THOMSON (Leichhardt-Minister for Science and Technology) (9.26)-1 thank the Committee for this debate. I must say that it has been much more negative than positive. The whole portfolio expenditure for my Department of Science and Technology increased by 10.8 per cent this year, slightly more than the rate of inflation. In times of budgetary discipline I think this was a very good result. There were a number of very positive measures in this Budget-the decision to build the Australia Telescope, the decision to fund a feasibility study into Starlab, and greatly increased spending on Antarctica. While I am discussing Antarctica I can tell the honourable member for Fremantle (Mr Dawkins) that the Antarctica Research Policy Advisory Committee report was released today. I very much regret that it took so long to release it, but it was released today. There was an ARPAC meeting in Melbourne today.
Mr Dawkins-Will you table it?
Mr THOMSON-It will not be tabled. It was released and is public. There was a Press release. Honourable members will find that most of the recommendations of the ARPAC have already been taken up and many more will be taken up in the next Budget.
Mr Dawkins-Mr Deputy Chairman, I raise a point of order. Is the Minister saying that the report will be tabled?
Mr THOMSON-No, I said it was issued today-not tabled.
Mr Dawkiits-Will you table it?
Mr THOMSON-I do not have a copy here to table.
Mr DawkinsWill you get one?
Mr THOMSON-It was issued today with a wide distribution. The honourable member for Fremantle asked also about the Patents Amendment Bill. That Bill went through this chamber and went to the Senate. The major clauses in it were designed to correct a problem caused by rulings of some lower courts and tribunals. While the Bill was in the Senate a High Court of Australia ruling made those amendments unnecessary, and the major parts of the Bill were withdrawn. Some minor clauses were put in the Statute Law (Miscellaneous Amendments) Bill. 1 undertake to write to the honourable member for Lalor (Mr

Barry Jones) and give him the details on that matter.
I refer to biotechnology. The Government has taken a very positive stance on biotechnology. In November last year a major seminar was held in Sydney which was funded jointly by my Department, industry and the Bank of New South Wales. People from all over the world attended-leading experts, people from industry and people from academia-and it has had a very great spin-off. A number of other seminars have been held since. One was held in Brisbane earlier this year. One is about to be held in Sydney. I think one was held in Melbourne last year. As a result of these seminars a number of new industries have been developed by Australian innovators. People have taken risks in this very important field and a number of new companies have been formed.
I will not take up any more of the Committee's time. I thank the Committee for this debate. I can assure honourable members that 1 will continue to be concerned with the future of science and technology and that 1 will do my best to make sure that the financial priorities are met as far as possible.

Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (9.32)-1 do not intend tonight to speak on the substantive issues raised by the estimates. 1 intend to take that line because I, with many others in this place, share the extremely critical view of the way the Estimates committees have been arranged this year. It is, of course, easier for shadow Ministers to do this because we get plenty of opportunities to speak on the issues related to our shadow portfolio responsibilities. However, 1 hope that in what I say tonight I speak for at least most of the back benchers in this chamber. First of all, I want to protest quite clearly about what has been substituted for the Estimates committees which have operated for the past three years. Whatever the flaws and whatever the inadequacies of those committees-we would all recognise ways in which they could be improved-they could not be

Appropriation Bill (No. J)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2431

worse than the farce that has been perpetrated here this week in the way the Estimates have been dealt with. We have had to deal with 13 departments in five hours. Ministers have sometimes been present-I will give them that-but on the whole they have contributed very little, far less than Ministers do at Estimates committee hearings. There has scarcely ever been a quorum present in the chamber. In fact, I think it is true to say that while the Estimates have been debated, the numbers in the House have usually been less than what is required for a smaller single Estimates committee.
The farce that we have here is the result of the actions of the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen), actions which he said arose 'from a profound respect, admiration and love for this place'. I think this place could do with a little less of that respect, admiration and love if this week's superficial gallop through the Estimates is the result of that affection. Mr Deputy Chairman, I think you would very much agree with that. What this profound respect, admiration and love for this place has done is to deprive the House of one of the few small changes designed to keep it up with the information revolution of the twentieth century.
For the past three years we have had a set of small Estimates committees which at their best could probe the appropriations of the Government, which went a small way to breaching the insulation of the Executive from the Parliament by opening up Ministers and civil servants to sustained questioning. Indeed, it was the only forum avaiable in this Parliament for sustained and consecutive questioning of a Minister. Surely such respect, admiration and love that the Leader of the House has must be misplaced if it has the consequences of ending these Estimates committees.
When he defended the action that he has taken, the Minister did confess that his was a very old fashioned view. It is certainly a very old fashioned view. It is an antidilvian view because the Minister seems unable to realise that this Parliament can no longer be the cosy debating club for gentlemen that seems to be his ideal. He seems unable to comprehend the dramatic changes that have taken place in Australian society which impel change in the way we organise this Parliament. Societal change compels parliamentary change. If we do not do it, this Parliament will simply become increasingly ineffective and increasingly irrelevant. As my colleague the honourable member for Swan (Mr Beazley) and the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) have pointed out, there has been an enormous spread of functions of the Parliament in the twentieth century-in particular, a spread of

functions since the Second World War-which requires, if it is to be satisfactorily supervised, a subdivision of labour and hence a development of the committee system generally and in this case Estimates committees. In addition, those functions have been increasingly more dense because of the proliferation of information that this Parliament has to deal with. This forum, this kind of committee, gives no opportunity to probe in depth for that information.
Secondly, the Minister claims to be a great supporter of the authority of this House.
The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Dr Jenkins)- Order! I remind the honourable member for Bonython that we are dealing with the estimates of the Department of Social Security, the Department of Health and the Department of Veterans' Affairs. What is happening is that the other chamber is dealing with the social security estimates and health estimates in a much more effective way than we are. Mr MacKELLAR (Warringah) (9.43)-1 listened with interest to the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett). Like a lot of contributions from the honourable member, his speech was very interesting but totally irrelevant to the subject under debate here tonight. I wish to speak on an area of the Budget where I very firmly believe the Commonwealth is not doing enough. In fact, it is not doing anything. The area I am speaking of is that of preventive health and the lack of a national health promotion program. When I say that, I am in no way overlooking the responsibilities of the States, which do have a responsibility to undertake programs which educate the population about better health. The States, quite rightly, should undertake those programs, but I strongly believe there is still a need to provide a co-ordinated Australia-wide campaign for better health, supported by the Commonwealth, with advice and perhaps even a financial contribution from the States.
At present in Australia we have a general health status which is good by world standards but which could certainly be very much better. By world standards we rank high on measures of longevity. Our infant mortality rate is comparatively low but our population suffers from the diseases

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2433

of affluence, most of which are distinctly avoidable. The promotion of an awareness and an avoidance of lifestyle and behaviour which lead to some of these conditions and illnesses is to my mind ever more important. It is becoming clear that the high quality curative medical care provided by medical practitioners and hospitals in Australia is having less and less impact on improving our health status. In fact, if we look at the way our health services are run right throughout Australia we find that the curative side of things is of a very high standard indeed. The practitioners of medical and allied medical care in Australia, lose nothing by comparison with their overseas compatriots.
However, our present health care system is providing little more than a catchment net for the victims of ills caused by adverse environmental factors and personal behavioural risks. There is a growing recognition that many of today's health problems are related directly to the way we live our lives and are largely preventable by action at individual, family and community levels. It is for this reason that I believe the Commonwealth should be actively engaged in promoting better health. It is a sad commentary on modern society that as we become more successful at providing what are called the better things of life for our people we are in many ways and in many cases unwittingly providing them with more and more health problems.
Quite understandabiy, in the past the emphasis has been on treating illness. This is costly, and it is imperative that we all work together to change the word 'health' back to its original positive meaning with its connotations of well-being. I remember when I was first appointed Minister for Health ~omebody said to me that I really should have been called the Minister for ill-health, because that is really what we were looking after. What we were doing was treating the illnesses present in the community and doing very little to prevent their coming into existence. In saying that I remind the House that the Commonwealth took steps in 1980 to create a new awareness of better health when it launched a very successful pilot program called Help Yourself to Health, which used traditional marketing concepts to reach mass audiences. That program was further enhanced by the 'Life. Be in it.' campaign. I doubt whether many people in Australia would not have heard of the 'Life. Be in it.' campaign and the attitude and appearance of Norm.
Now, with no money being allocated to the Federal Department of Health for a health promotion program, I am concerned that hospital beds might be taken up by people whose illnesses

are largely due to personal behaviour and lifestyle patterns. The user pays principle for health care was introduced by the Commonwealth in order that the public would become more aware of the costs of the health services they use. This in turn would lead to a downturn in demand for expensive health services. Obviously the user pays principle is working. It has made States more responsible in the way they spend their health dollars and has gone a long way towards educating the public about the high cost of health services. A great deal more can be done, for in pursuing health promotion campaigns we are not only improving the nation's health but also reducing the sizeable burden of national expenditure on health. That is what health promotion is all about. It is about keeping people healthy and out of institutions.
Prevention should be our number one priority. I strongly believe that the Commonwealth must lead the way. By prevention I am talking not only about occupational health, an area where business and governments already spend a large amount of their time educating workers, but also about outside hazards such as smoking, alcohol abuse, preventable stress and the like. The link between smoking and lung cancer is well established, as is the effect of smoking on the severity of illnesses such as pneumonia and bronchitis. Yet so many Australians continue to prejudice their health by smoking. Similarly, too many people continue to disregard the accepted link between excessive alcohol consumption and diseases such as cirrhosis of the liver and alcoholism. They turn a blind eye too to the involvement of alcohol in many other deaths, such as those due to motor vehicle accidents, drownings and homicides. If we look at the influence of alcohol in so many areas of health, not only in the death syndrome but also in those debilitating illnesses and accidents which cause a great deal of disability to the individuals concerned, we will see that this particular aspect of our modern life has a massive contribution to make to the total health costs. High blood pressure is a known cause of heart attacks and strokes. People who are overweight, who get insufficient exercise and who are subject to stress are known to be more prone to developing high blood pressure and heart disease. Indeed, moderate diet and exercise are essential to good health.
I know that this is flogging a dead horse in many ways. So many of us are still failing to heed these simple health messages. That is why I say that money must be allocated to the Federal Health Department for a national health promotional program. The benefits of such a program are, I believe, extremely obvious. It need not cost much

2434 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

money for a well run program because such a program could in fact be self-supporting. Some months ago it was suggested to me that a health promotion program could be instituted along the lines of the Advance Australia campaign and that it would eventually be self-supporting and ongoing. Once an appropriate logo has been designed it would be sold to manufacturers for use on their products. It would show that a team of experts had endorsed that product as being useful for a healthy lifestyle. This is a suggestion which I believe has a lot of merit and should be considered in the near future, as quality of life is important to us all.
The maintenance of good health is essential to the quality of life. It is an element that needs support and promotion within our current social environment. We need to promote a better understanding of the close relationship between lifestyle and health and to encourage a community-wide philosophy of health advancement and personal responsibility for health. A healthy lifestyle does not require radical changes in lifestyle, but it does require positive action by the Commonwealth. I believe the Commonwealth must lead the way in providing a health promotion program that will educate members of the public not to abuse their health, thereby leaving those expensive health facilities to those who genuinely need them. So often we come up against the cost benefit argument, particularly from the Treasury: 'Can you prove that such a promotional campaign will lead to a diminution in costs in the future?' I believe that such a program, such a campaign, is a long term one whose benefits will obviously be seen in the long term. If and when it is successful all Australians will be able to say that they have a better understanding of the way in which they should lead their lives so that they can enjoy-I know this sounds a little trite-that most precious of all commodities, a healthy and happy life.
The DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Dr Jenkins)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (9.53)-1 want to address my remarks to the affairs of the Department of Social Security and the Department of Veterans' affairs. In many ways the Budget presented in these two areas is to the credit of the Australian Labor Party because every reform implemented in this Budget in respect of the Departments of Social Security and Veterans' Affairs has been advocated for a very long period against the most stringent opposition from the Government, some of that opposition extending virtually from the time when the Government came to office in

1975. I speak particularly of the increased maximum permissible earnings for pensioners in all categories. I speak also, in respect of the Department of Veterans' Affairs, of the repatriation benefits extended to those involved in peacekeep-
ing forces before November 1981; of the eligibility for Service pensions for all Allied ex-merchant mariners; and of the extension to World War I
veterans and to veterans of the wars in Malaya and Vietnam of the broad eligibility criterion for disability pension applicable to World War II veterans.
All those reforms had been advocated by the Labor Party for a very considerable time over opposition from the Government not only on the grounds of the nature of the economy at the time and the impossibility of finding funds to meet these needs but also on grounds of principle. In all these instances grounds of principle for not extending those benefits have been found by Government members. Fortunately those principles have been jettisoned, at least temporarily, for the purposes for which this Budget was established. Unfortunately, however, we cannot be confident that that will continue into the future. What has not occurred in the Budget is the establishment of a decent basis of life for pensioners in this country. This Budget has not reversed a slide in the value of the pension to those in receipt of it from about 24 per cent of average weekly earnings when we were in power to a little below 22 per cent now. That slide continues. Also we cannot look forward with any confidence to the value of the adjustments in the maximum permissible earnings before amounts are deducted from the pension being maintained in the years to come. Certainly no principle in that regard has been announced by the Government in the various statements on social security questions in this Budget.
What can be expected if this Government is returned is that the value of that concession will be permitted to decline in the way in which it has been permitted to decline from 1975 to this point. The purposes served by having this earning capacity granted to pensioners will be largely dissipated because they will have no confidence that it will be retained. Many of the sorts of problems pointed to by honourable members opposite in particular-not on this side of the chamber-of people not declaring their incomes in order to retain their pensions or to avoid paying tax will continue if that confidence is not developed. For example, under this Budget the maximum permissible earnings of a single mother with two children before she starts to lose money from her pension have effectively gone to $42 a week. That is no encouragement at all. Had those maximum

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2435

permissible earnings the value that they had in 1975, that single parent could earn upwards of $80 a week and could get into the areas of part-time work, whose value is funy declared to the Department of Social Security and the Australian Taxation Office, which could be reasonably pursued. This would be to the very great benefit of the Australian taxpayer in that a person gaining that sort of working experince would be placed in a position of being likely to be employable once more.
That has not been advanced in this Budget because this Budget has quite clearly been presented as a one-off Budget. It is a Budget whose benefits cannot be expected to extend beyond 12 months, as some honourable members opposite would argue, or beyond a few months, as we on this side would argue. There can certainly be among people in these categories no confidence of their capabilities in this regard being improved in the future. I think it is time we got away from these marginal arguments about improving in a piecemeal fashion the position of people on pensions in an categories and started a more comprehensive approach to an aspects of social security funding. We are moving in that direction-very slowly-in respect of retirement benefits, but not in other areas. If we started to move into contributory benefit schemes we would stop being involved in the archaic debates which we often have in this chamber as to whether there are 'deserving poor'. That is demeaning. We would move more effectively into discussing questions of equity and rights and into examining whether retirement benefits and other pension adequately supported the lives of people in receipt of them. Mr UREN-Mr Deputy Chairman, time is short and I want to confine my remarks to the housing aspect of the Social Security portfolio. Next to unemployment, housing is the most serious social problem in this country. Not since the Second World War has the housing industry been so confined or so damaged, its capacity so eroded and its activities so stagnant. Not since the 1940s has there been such tightness in the private rental market. Not since the 1930s Depression has Australia had such a high incidence of homelessness. Never before has public housing been so restricted. Never before have housing interest rates been so high. Never before has a national government shown so little regard for the social and economic importance of housing.
When we weigh the contribution of the 1982 Budget against the range and depth of housing

There is already a huge backlog in neglected housing needs. In addition we need to build about 160,000 new homes each year, at least until the end of the decade, to cope with the household formation resulting from natural population growth and immigration. The recent report of the Indicative Planning Council for the Housing Industry estimates that only 118,000 new houses will be commenced in 1983. That level of activity will mean a shortfall of 42,000 below the requirement of one year alone. Already, three years into the decade of the 1980s, we are 85,000 dwellings below requirement. If this trend is allowed to continue we will be 380,000 dwellings short by the end of the decade, and that figure relates only to emerging needs. We still have to catch up with the backlog of the Government's neglect over the past six years. Any sensible person would think that the Government, facing economically hard times, would do its utmost to stimulate a high level of housing activity.
Housing provides the widest and fastest flow-on effects throughout the economy. Every $100 spent on housing requires an input to the value of $240 in other sectors. Every $1 OOm spent on housing provides an additional 5,000 jobs directly and indirectly. In the 1982-83 Budget a total of $333m was advanced to the Northern Territory and the States for public housing. After repayment of principal, the net advance was $290m. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard three tables which show the real net Federal payments to the States for housing from 1974-75 to 1982-83.
Leave granted.

Mr UREN-I thank the House. The first table shows that the $290m advanced in 1982-83 compares with $975m advanced in 1974-75. In today's prices, the funds for housing in 1982-83 are $685m short of the 1974-75 spending. The cumulative real reduction in public housing funds over the last eight Budgets is a staggering $3,740m. If the funding had been held steady at the 1974-75 level, we would have been able to build an extra 75,000 public dwellings. We would not now be looking at 100,000 families on the waiting lists of State housing authorities. We would not now be looking at such massive unemployment in this country or at so much homelessness in our society. The second table shows the net Federal payments to the States for housing in 1982-83 after the States have repaid the principal and interest commitments for that year. We find that the six States have a total or a mere $1 04m. That third table shows that the $104m in 1982-83 compares with $652m in 1974-75. The States are now receiving less than one-sixth of the amount that they received eight years ago.

Let me make some further comparisons. The $104m spent on public housing in the States compares with $147m allocated in this Budget for the deposit assistance scheme and a further $415m estimated in revenue foregone through home loan interest rebates. About one-third of the population-those who are currently paying off home loans-are handed out $562m in a single year. That is over five times the amount of funds spent on public housing. What happened to the quarter of the population who rent their homes in the private rental market? The Government has allocated a mere $20m to be shared between these people and those who are having trouble keeping up their mortgage repayments. On the grounds of horizontal equity, this distribution of housing assistance is scandalous. On the grounds of vertical equity-a concern for the needy groups in our community-this Government's distribution is obscene. Rental vacancy rates are now the lowest on record-on average between one per cent and 2 per cent in the different capital cities. Mr UREN--I thank the House. The cutbacks in public housing and the reduced opportunities for home ownership are putting increasing pressures on the private rental market. Tenants are facing rental increases and many are facing eviction. We are heading back to the insecurity of the 1920s and 1930s. If comprehensive action is not taken now we will be faced with social chaos. We need a program of housing action co-ordinated at the national level. As the Catholic Commission for Justice and Peace noted recently:
There is no national housing policy upon which all governments agree.
There is no mechanism for achieving consensus about housing policy objectives. There is no Federal department of housing. This is the deplorable situation. No Federal Government department is responsible for housing. That is the low priority that housing has. That is why the Department of Social Security now controls the Commonwealth State Housing Agreement. As I pointed out earlier, since 1974-75 the Government has cut back some $3,740m. That represents one of the great transfers of wealth away from the great bulk of people to the wealthy sector. This is what this Government does. It worries about the very wealthy. There are so many people wanting a home that it is an impossible dream for about nine out of 10 people. That is why the situation will grow more difficult.
Mr ADERMANN (Fisher) (lO.26)-Because of the time constraints, I will confine my remarks to the estimates of one Department, the Department of Veterans' Affairs. Tonight the honourable member for Swan (Mr Beazley) raised the matter of a judicial inquiry. I know that the honourable member for Melbourne Ports (Mr Holding) has also raised the matter many times. I do not take the position of an antagonist in this matter because I believe that both honourable

members were very sincere in what they were saying. I wrestled with this problem for a long time. I spoke many times with members of the Vietnam Veterans' Action Association. I believe that we had a good relationship, that we trusted each other. But I have a point of view that I think ought to be put. I am happy to say that some good things have happened to the Vietnam veterans. The counselling service is working. I am glad to see that there has been an extension of medical treatment not only to veterans but also to their families in certain circumstances. I am glad to see that fast lane operating on urgent occasions. All those things are good.
The problem that I have with the judicial inquiry is simply this: If it is a question of compensation, let us pay whatever compensation is necessary with the minimum of delay and debate and the minimum of time going through tribunals. I can accept that. But the matter that has worried me very greatly is that many of these veterans have come to me and have said: 'I was in Vietnam. I believe I was exposed to agent orange. I feel perfectly well but, Minister, will you tell me what is likely to happen to me in the future? Am I likely to develop cancer? What is my future likely to be? I would like to father children. I have been reading all sorts of things. Will I be able to father children without worry?'
A judicial inquiry can answer some things but I believe that we have to pursue a scientific mortality study to try to find the medical and scientific answers for those people. It is more than a question of compensation and favourable treatment. I do not believe that we should rely on any American study or any other study to find out those medical and scientific answers. I believe that the course that is followed ought to be pursued very seriously and urge:1tly because those people must have their minds put at rest. Although I accept the sincerity of the point of view that has been put forward concerning a judicial inquiry, I believe that such an inquiry will not give us these answers. I say that kindly, recognising the strength of the arguments that have been put.
In speaking to the estimates of the Department of Veterans' Affairs I do want to say that this is a good Budget for veterans. I am pleased to see that the Government has extended Service pensions to a wider range of former allied mariners. The merchant mariners have waited a long time for the Service pension. I am glad to see that extension and the restitution of the 100 per cent rate of disability allowance to service men and women suffering from tuberculosis. They fought for that. They fought for that responsibly because they had to adapt their lifestyles to a certain situation.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2441

They will be pleased about that. I am pleased that the disability pension is now going to be totally disregarded in any income test for service pensions and fringe benefits. That is an acceptance of a very important principle. A disability pension is compensation, and I am glad to see that it is no longer taken into account in the income test. I am glad to see the extension of free repatriation hospital treatment to a further group of World War II veterans, that is, those who have a disability of 50 per cent or more. I hope we can work towards the goal of full, free repatriation treatment for all World War II veterans as quickly as possible. That treatment is now available to World War I veterans. We should be working towards that goal of extending full treatment as we are able, to World War II veterans. They are getting older, their disabilities are catching up with them, and their lives are not so easy. Surely that is a goal we ought to seek to attain. Perhaps from there we could extend it to later conflicts. What was done with World War I veterans is well on the way with World War II veterans. If we could go further in the future and extend that benefit to all people who have served their country so bravely and so well, I for one would be very pleased. Some of the other benefits, such as the upgrading of funeral benefits and those for the particularly seriously disabled, have been increased again, and I am pleased about that. If indexation is not brought in, that annual adjustment to take care of inflationary trends has to continue.
I should like to raise a couple of points in talking to these estimates. As has been previously mentioned by other speakers on both sides of the House, the "long delays in the appeals procedures are the cause of very much heartbreak. The appeals system, through the Repatriation Board, the Repatriation Commission and the Administrative Appeals Tribunal, and if necessary in certain circumstances the Administrative Appeals Tribunal and the Federal Court, is a very good one and a very fair one. However, if an appeal has to be taken to more than one level it does not take weeks or months; it takes years. I know that that causes considerable problems. It is far too long. I am not on my own in my concern; I know many members have similar cases and they have spoken to me about them. I refer to cases such as the particularly sad one I struck recently of veterans struggling to get disability accepted who died before they ever got even half-way through the system. Another veteran had been to the Board, to the Commission, the Tribunal not once but twice or three times because the matter was adjourned.

Finally, his case, which was very just, was accepted and he was given a totally and permanent incapacity pension. I rang his home to tell him the good news and I found that he had died the previous day. I cannot, I admit, give a full answer as to what can be done. I do know that, as a top priority, we have got to do something about reducing these very long delays because our veterans are getting older. The battle is long and the trauma is
great.
Another matter of concern is the manner in which pensions are paid. Veterans have asked for a long time to be put on a par with social security recipients. Why can they not get their pensions paid fortnightly in advance to their bank accounts? This is a hardy perennial, I know. The answer that has been given is that thjs would be very costly to institute. First up it would, but surely there is discrimination here. Surely at some time in the future, and I hope soon, we will front up to that cost and will be able to give the veterans that facility? The other answer that was
often given to me was that the computers would not handle it. In these estimates and in the estimates for last year I am pleased to see that computerisation has been very much improved in the Department. It is well advanced now, and I hope that with computerisation at least one reason has been put aside and we might be able to work towards getting this facility for our veterans.
Another matter that has been raised is the wonderful work that is done by welfare officers and those people who help their mates as advocates. That is voluntary work; it is wonderful work. They are angels of mercy, but they do it in an honorary capacity. When they appear as advocates they often have to travel some distance to the nearest city to appear for their mates and help them. Their mates appreciate the fact that there is somebody there whom they know and can trust. They feel that they cannot put their own cases. This is a pretty important facility. The problem is that many advocates say they can no longer do it because they have to bear the costs of travel and the cost of a meal while they are there and therefore they are dropping out. I hope that we can have a look at the principle of helping these advocates with some of the costs. They are doing a tremendous job, an honorary job, and a job that I would not like to see the Government have to pick up.
I should like to make one final point. I do not wish to be unduly critical of members of the tribunals. They do a good job, and the President, Mr Mahony, is doing tremendously good duty. The informality of those hearings is very important, but we will still hear from some veterans that

2442 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

appearing before some tribunals is an ordeal. They complain that it is like a court of law, an inquisition. They are regarded as being people who put up a facade to get something to which they are not entitled. They feel they have to prove their case, and the onus of proof clause is not working. I compliment the members of busy tribunals. I am not being critical in this. Their work is hard, tiring and onerous. At the end of a long session they get tired, but I want them to remember that veterans too are tired, aged, sick and frustrated from years of pleading their cause. I ask tribunal members to remember especially that the veteran often feels that he cannot adequately express his feelings and a court of law atmosphere makes it very difficult for him. There is stress and trauma, and I see this as a real ordeal for many veterans. If the tribunals would make sure that there was a human face there, this is a good system-it is the best in the world-I believe they would retain the confidence and the encouragement of the veteran.

Mr LEO McLEAY (Grayndler) (10.38)-The estimates before us for the Department of Social Security show an allocation of $70.04m for expenditure on child care services, an increase of $22.7m on last year's expenditure. As well, the States and the Northern Territory will receive a block grant of $33.09m as the Federal Government's contribution to pre-school education under the Children's Services Program. This is the same level of funding as provided for pre-schools last year. In the Budget Papers, in a tone of selfcongratulation, the Government declared its intentions for children's services in 1982-83 to be the maintenance of a healthy child care sector in Australia to which people of modest incomes and high social needs have access.
The substantial increase in the Budget allocation for the Children's Services Program is an

effective response by the Government to those people who have mistakenly questioned the Commonwealth's commitment to the provision of day care services for children. However, when the budgetary allocation for 1982-83 is put into perspective against allocations in recent years, it provides scant reassurance about the future of a healthy child care sector or adequate provision of day care services for children. The ministerial statements about children's services confirm my concern about the impact this Government's child care policies is having on the community. Child care is now another under-financed welfare service and it is not the integrated program providing child care and family support that it was intended to be. The expectation that child care would be a broadly based, directly funded program was encouraged by the introduction of the Child Care Act in 1972 by the McMahon Government. That Government recognised that Commonwealth financial assistance was necessary to provide a comprehensive Australia-wide child care service. That Government acknowledged that the full cost of reasonable quality care could not be paid for by most parents and proposed that fees in federally funded centres should be around the level currently being paid in the community. The then Minister for Labour and National Service, the right honourable member for Flinders (Sir Phillip Lynch), stated:
For those fees, however, children will enjoy in Government assisted centres, a quality of care superior to that otherwise available because of the nature of the facilities and the qualified staff provided.
The McMahon Government explicitly recognised that subsidies would have to be provided to enable families to make use of child care services. It was not just a question of access for people with modest incomes and high social needs. The Government expected a broad range of families to have access to the services provided. Building on that foundation in September 1974 the Federal Australian Labor Party Government committed itself to the provision of a universal children's services program. In a major policy statement the then Special Minister of State, the honourable member for Kingsford-Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen), said that all children in Australia would have access to services designed to take care of their physical, social and recreational needs. Central to that policy was the view that services should be flexible, community-based and integrated. No rigid distinction was to be made between educating children and caring for them, as is the case with this present Government's policy of providing a block grant to the States for pre-schools and direct funding to specified groups for child care.

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2443

It was after the election of the Liberal-Country Party Government in 1975 that Labor's goal, and indeed the McMahon Government's goal, of establishing and developing a network of child care services was frustrated. Fundamental changes were swiftly made to the Children's Services Program. During the 1975 election campaign the Liberal Party had promised to protect all welfare services against inflation. In February 1976 the allocation to children's services was reduced by $9m. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) announced also that his Government would not proceed with the Children's Services Commission although the Bill to establish it had been passed by the Parliament and indeed had received royal assent on 11 June 1975. The program was to be administered by the Office of Child Care within the administrative structures of the Department of Social Security-where it languishes even today. This was a radical change of emphasis for the Children's Services Program. The ALP Government's policy had committed it to the goal of providing flexible, locally-based services for children and their families. The concern of the right honourable member for Flinders for reasonable quality child care to be provided for Australian families, which he expressed when introducing the Child Care Bill in 1972, is now seen as a forlorn hope sabotaged by neglect during six years of the Fraser Government. The Liberal Country Party Government in 1976 categorised the provision of child care services as a welfare responsibility and began to restrict it to the needy.
A central feature of this Government's child care policy has been its stated intention to direct services to those children whom it classifies as being in need. Indeed, $6.86m has been specifically earmarked for this purpose in the estimates before us tonight. Under the ALP Government and the Liberal-Country Party Government in 1972 children in need were given priority of access to government-sponsored services. However, it was done without the stigma of being labelled as being needy, mainly because priority of access was given within the context of a policy of providing a comprehensive range of child care services for all children in this society. The underlying attitude of this Government seems to be that a normally functioning family will either not require child care services or will be able to purchase them in the market place. I believe that this attitude cannot be supported by the available evidence. There is considerable material available to the Government to show that virtually all families need and want access to some form of child care, that it is not merely a peculiarity of the needy.

Children currently receiving access priority under the Children's Services Program are categorised as: Children from low income families, children from single parent families, children from families with both parents working, migrant children, children with sick or incapacitated parents, Aboriginal children, handicapped children and children at risk of maltreatment. Common sense would seem to say that these categories must include the majority of children in Australia, especially when evidence shows that one-third of young children have both parents working in paid employment, that 10 per cent of young children are from single parent families, and that a large and ever-increasing number of children come from low income families. It seems obvious to conclude from this that the likelihood of a family
having at least one of the specified characteristics is high.
It is difficult to obtain firm figures about children's access to federally funded children's services. However, estimates obtained recently suggest that 53,456 children in a given week, with perhaps 39,392 children being regular attenders, is the pattern of access. These estimates refer to centre-based care and family day care. Included in these figures are primary school children as well as children from 0 to school age. This means that an estimated 1.6 per cent of children between the years of 0 and 12 use government-funded children's services with an estimated 1.2 per cent being regular attenders. What this Government means, therefore, by ensuring access to services for those people of modest income and high social need is that they will be represented among the few who have access to government-funded services.
The estimated level of access to governmentsponsored services of 1.6 per cent mocks the assertion of the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) that this Budget has provided a substantial increase in the provision of services for children. Indeed, it mocks his assertion that this is a measure of this Government's commitment to the provision of day care services for children. The Family and Children's Services Agency of New South Wales estimates that during the period from 1976 to 1981 there has been a 37 per cent reduction in real terms in child care expenditure. In recent years funding for new child care services appeared to cease. In 1981, because of the lack of funds for new services, the Office of Child Care rejected between 400 and 500 submissions for new services throughout Australia. This includes 120 submissions from New South Wales. Many of these rejected submissions had been classified as having high or very high priority in terms

2444 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

of this present Government's needs criteria. The $5m for new projects this financial year provided in the estimates will pay for the capital and recurrent costs of an estimated 25 new projects only. This is a mere pittance which will scarcely make an impression on the rate of access of children to Government-funded centres.
In Sydney, for example, approximately 100,000 children under the age of five live in the western Sydney region-more than the total population under five in the whole of South Australia. Almost a quarter of all New South Wales children and a third of those with working mothers live in this region which is the most disadvantaged in New South Wales in terms of pre-school, long day care, family day care and services for children aged 0 to two. It has been estimated that to raise the level of services in this region alone to that of the inner western region, which itself is not overly endowed with services, would cost $40m. The Government has provided only $5m for the whole of Australia.
But the Government, with its propensity for free enterprise, has provided a pilot scheme for commercial centres. The announcement in this Budget of $200,000 for a pilot scheme to test the feasibility of providing a subsidy for needy children using commercial centres is seen as further evidence of the Government's desire to reduce public sector spending and divest itself of responsibility for a range of health and welfarerelated services. The proposal is not designed to increase the number of children with access to subsidised services; rather it is aimed at transferring resources from the public to the private sector. These estimates do little to assure me that the Government has a strong and continuing commitment to a range of community-based services for children and their families. That particularly progressive policy was thrown out in 1976 when this Government began to dismantle peicemeal the Children's Services Program. The Government has forgotten also that it governs for all of society, not just a particular sectional interest within it. In 1982 the goal of providing reasonable quality care to Australian children and their families no longer exists, except as a vision in the minds of those who expect better things from an elected and responsible government.
Progress reported.
