Consideration resumed.
Mr McLEAN (Perth) (10.50)-The first thing I want to say regarding the estimates for the Department of Social Security is that the budgeted expenditures for 1982-83 in both the pensions and family support areas constitute a significant advancement for these recipient groups both in the new initiatives contained in the Budget and in the extension of existing services in a way which responds to many of the problems incurred by these groups. In the pensions area this Budget has responded positively to the major problems pensioners have raised with me in recent years.
Increasing the income levels determining pensioner entitlement for age and invalid pensioners by 50 per cent for single pensioners and 45 per cent for pensioner couples means two very important things. Firstly, it means that some 430,000 pensioners in Australia with private incomes above the existing free areas will receive substantially higher pensions in the near future. Secondly, it means the eradication, at least for the short term, of the problem whereby people approaching retiring age were being discouraged from making some provision for their retirement. As these limits had not been increased since 1972, pensioners with indexed private incomes such as superannuation would have found that a cost of living adjustment to his or her superannuation would have led to some reduction in the pension.
As a result, each adjustment would reduce, rather than protect, the real income of the pensioner or the pensioner couple. For the same reason, I welcome the increased income limits determining pensioner entitlement to health benefits. These measures, which are estimated to cost some $72m this year and $llOm in a full financial year, together with the initiatives to remove any tax liability from some 300,000 pensioners and low income earners this year, effectively meet the major problems which pensioners have been bringing to my attention in my electorate office in recent years.
I also want to support the proposed additional expenditure on behalf of families. The family income supplement, a new initiative involving an expenditure of some $30m in a full year, is an important and much needed support for low income

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2445

family units. The 50 per cent increase in the family allowance for the first and second child, following the same percentage increase for third and subsequent children in the last Budget, will also provide welcome support for the family unit in our society. These income support measures, together with the taxation relief provided for the typical family unit, make this latest Budget the most pro-family Budget in my memory. That is a social welfare priority and direction which has my full support.
I still maintain that in implementing a sensible national family policy we must move away from the ad hoc approach of the past so that decisions in this critical policy area-critical from both a social security and a taxation viewpoint-are administered from a sound data base involving all departments which have an impact on the welfare of the family unit and involving each level of government in each State. I will not elaborate further on this point because I have constantly raised this matter in this Parliament and did so again as recently as last week. But there is no doubt in my mind that the preparation of official family impact statements should be undertaken as soon as possible in order to ensure a co-ordinated and properly informed approach to family welfare matters.
In this Committee debate I would like to refer to a problem which was brought to my attention by a constituent. I refer to the administration of the class B widow's pension. I want to draw the attention of the Committee to the plight of a widow who, because her son found employment just two weeks prior to her 45th birthday, no longer qualifies for a widow's pension of any kind. She has now been forced into a position where, because of arthritis and a commitment to her aged dependants, she cannot find work and must submit to being certified as medically unfit for work every three months so that she can claim the sickness benefit. I find this situation unacceptable. Had the son intended to continue on to tertiary studies he would not have been aware of whether he had gained admittance to an institution of learning until December or even January and would not have commeQced his studies until February. In these circumstances his mother could have retained her entitlement to the pension for all time. In fact, had he commenced tertiary studies, left after one week and then started work-even in these circumstances-his mother would have retained her widow's pension.
For those women who remained at home to raise their family rather than enter the work force and who then lost their husband and/or provider, this is a frightening prospect, particularly in the

present unemployment situation. These women often do not have the skill or expertise to find employment at that age. They are not able to compete in the market-place for jobs and yet, by the way we administer this pension, we are basically abandoning them. We say that they are secure as long as they have children who are still studying, regardless of their age. But at precisely the age when their prospects of employment are low and their children leave school, we take away their family allowance. We also take away their widow's pension if they have not yet reached the age of 45 years. Instead, after being out of the work force for up to 20 years in order to raise a family, we ask them to find employment, collect the unemployment benefit or subject themselves to regular medical check-ups to prove that they are too sick to work.
I see this part of our system as flying in the face of the very values which have so recently been advocated by this House-the encouragement of the basic unit in our society, the family unit. How can we properly support those mothers who stay home and care for their families when we are prepared to abandon them at the very time when they most need our help-when they have lost husbands and their children are commencing to live their own lives with some relative degree of independence? I believe that every effort should be made to give some vestige of security to those who have chosen to make their career in the home. In this case a mother in unique circumstances failed by two weeks to qualify for the class B pension and has been denied the security and confidence she deserves as a result of undertaking her parental responsibilities. I believe there ought to be sufficient flexibility in the administration of these pensions to ensure that such people are properly protected. I ask the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) to examine this matter with a sense of urgency.
Solving this problem would not unduly affect the estimates, as the widow will receive some other form of income anyway-the unemployment benefit or the sickness benefit. My argument is simply that she deserves the security, confidence and recognition a widow's pension would provide. We should be able to provide an element of flexibility through an appropriate appeals process for special cases of this kind.
It appears to me that there is a degree of inconsistency in the way in which people in similar circumstances are treated by different classes of entitlement in the social security area. I urge the Minister to examine this matter and similar cases because they can be solved relatively easily and at

2446 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

(lppropriation Bill (No.1)

small cost. That would certainly assist many people.
Another example of this inconsistency was also brought to my attention earlier this year by another constituent. This Budget, incidentally, has rectified this inconsistency. My constituent was concerned at the way in which a widow is discriminated against when compared with the recipient of a supporting parent's benefit. The Social Services Act had provided that money paid throught maintenance to a deserted wife or divorcee for the support of a child should be taken into account in the assessment of entitlement to a widow's pension as the claimant's own income. It was intended that this provision should also apply when the supporting mother's benefit was introduced. But that legislation did not give effect to that intent.
Because of that anomaly-I admit that this has been rectified in the Budget-my constituent cancelled her divorce proceedings in order to benefit from the discrimination. But if this anomaly did not exist it would have been her preference to proceed with her divorce to clarify with some degree of finality her non-married status. The Government decided to amend the Social Services Act and has proceeded to do so in order that the treatment of maintenance for the children of supporting parent beneficiaries will be brought into line with that applying to widow pensioners.
This matter may not have been resolved in a way that would please supporting parents or supporting mothers. But I simply make the point that it does indicate that the Minister is prepared to remove these inconsistencies which cause a lot of concern to many people. Although I have reservations about the way in which that inconsistency was removed, I congratulate the Minister for moving in the direction of putting some degree of consistency into the social welfare system whereby people in similar circumstances are entitled to be subjected to the same criteria in receiving certain entitlements. I have brought a number of these matters to the attention of the Minister throughout the course of this year. I ask him to address his attention to them. Although they do not involve many people or a lot of money they cause a lot of concern, worry and hardship to the people directly affected.
Mr LES McMAHON (Sydney) (11.0)- Tonight I will speak on the estimates of the Department of Social Security. I wish to raise the issue of the Australian family, which is currently and has been for some years under attack. Since

1973 marriages and births have been on the decrease while divorces have rapidly increased. During the period from 1973 to 1979 the number of marriages entered into in Australia decreased by over 8,000; during the same period the number of divorces more than doubled. It is a fact that Australians are not marrying and having children as past generations have done. The greatest proportion of families in Australia today have only two children.
Whilst we are experiencing this dramatic decline in marriages and a rapid escalation in divorces, we are witnessing an increase in the number of children born outside marriage. In 1974 just over 9-!- per cent of children born in Australia were born outside of marriage. In 1979 the figure had increased to just over II -!- per cent, which represents an increase of 2,703 in six years. In 1982 it is not decreasing. Do not misconstrue what I am saying. I am not attempting to pass judgm~nt. There is only one who is capable of passing fair judgment and we will have to face that judgment one day ourselves. What I am concerned about is that many of these children will not experience a full family life. I realise the importance of a stable family environment in the development of any child.
But why the decline in marriages and births and an increase in divorces? Why should people marry, have children and stay married when it is so easy to get divorced and when, in many cases, divorce presents a far better economic proposition? Similarly, why should people marry and have children when, in many cases, it is also a far better economic proposition to have children outside of wedlock and pursue a single lifestyle and in some cases a de facto marriage lifestyle? Thousands of families are trying to exist on one wage which, in effect, is less than the combined social security benefits paid to many unmarried supporting parents and divorced supporting parents.

It is not a bright prospect for many people to marry and have children and struggle to survive on a single income. It is not a bright prospect for many women who see themselves becoming second class citizens totally dependent on their husband's income. many divorces. TAXATION (UNPAID COMPANY TAX) ASSESSMENT BILL (No.2) 1982
Bill presented by Mr Howard, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (11.10)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Some four weeks ago I introduced into the House a Bill by this title that set out to recover from former owners of companies that were stripped of pre-tax profits the amount of company tax that has been evaded as a consequence of such strips. The Bill that is now before the House replaces the earlier Bill. The revised Bill not only covers the same ground as the earlier Bill, but extends its scope to require promoters of these schemes also to effect some recoupment of the evaded tax. The legisation is thus now in a form that will result in both groups-vendors and promoters-who have benefited financially from the evasion of company tax being called on to make restitution. Subject to some exceptions that I will speak about later, the present Bill does not, in relation to vendorshareholders, vary from the earlier Bill and I think

I can draw the attention of the House to its main features in that respect by referring honourable members to my second reading speech on 23 September last.
Having done that, I turn now to describe the basis on which promoters and their associates will be liable to recoupment tax. The popular conception of who is a promoter of schemes to strip companies of untaxed profits does not help us all that much in framing the kind of tests which the legislation must express. The connection of promoters with the schemes they devised or marketed was one that varied substantially from promoter to promoter and is not capable of simple expression. Accordingly, the Bill contains a series of formal tests that describe which persons are to be treated as 'promoters' under the legislation or, in
the terms used in the Bill, as being members of the eligible promoters class.
Not only are promoters, as popularly understood, to be put into the eligible promoters class, but so too are those people, typically members of a promoter's family, who directly or indirectly had ownership rights in the companies and trusts through which promoters normally operated, and in that way received or stood to receive benefits. Included within the eligible promoters class will be those who purchased shares from vendorshareholders, were shareholders or directors of a purchasing company, were beneficiaries or trustees of a purchasing trust or were directors of a corporate trustee. Also included will be legal or beneficial owners through one or more further companies or trusts in a chain of ownership extending behind the purchasing company or trust.
Similar provision will be made where the promoter's formal connection was as agent for the purchaser or as broker, and where a person who was an associate of a purchaser from vendorshareholders gave financial assistance in the purchase of the shares. If by a loan that had the real character of a distribution of income or capital a person stripped moneys from a company or trust that is in the eligible promoters class, the person will also be included in the class. The Bill places Qn all the persons who are in the eligible promoters class in relation to a stripped company, a joint and several liability for recoupment tax in relation to that company. That means that each person in the class is fundamentally, but subject to rights of contribution, liable for the whole of the recoupment tax attaching to that group of persons. That feature, allied with the necessity to put persons into the eligible promoters class on the basis of their formal legal connections, for example as shareholder in a company, could lead

Taxation (Unpaid Company Tax) Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2449

to some people such as an office secretary being made liable when she does not either really participate in or benefit from the scheme otherwise than as an employee. Simply for that combination of reasons, there is provision in the Bill under which a person can achieve exclusion from the eligible promoters class by obtaining a Federal Court declaration excluding the person from the class.
And, as another aspect of the joint and several liability on persons in an eligible promoters class, a person who has in consequence been obliged to pay some or all of the recoupment tax attaching to that class may seek through the courts an appropriate contribution from other members of the class. The basic tests for determining whether 'promoters' are liable to recoupment tax will accord with those for vendor-shareholders, except that there will not be the test, applicable to vendors, that measures sale proceeds against the pre-sale net assets of the target company. The effect of the Bill will be that the 'promoters' group will generally be liable to recoupment tax equal to 20 per cent of vendors' liability, that is, 20 per cent of the total of unpaid primary company tax, any unpaid Division 7 tax and any late payment penalty accruing after vendors are notified of the company's liability.
However, in those pre-tax profits strip cases where vendors are not liable to recoupment tax, for example, where a tax provision was paid to a promoter who did not use it to pay the company tax, the relevant 'promoters' group will be liable for recoupment tax equal to the whole of such unpaid company tax. For those situations where the 'promoters' share of the evaded tax is 20 per cent, the Bill retains a full liability on the vendors, but to the extent that the 'promoters' group pays its 20 per cent share, the recoupment tax liability of the former owners of the company concerned will be correspondingly reduced. I mention that 20 per cent reflects the average amount of evaded tax that promoters took as fees for the service they provided to vendor-shareholders.
I turn now to some adjustments to the 'vendor' parts of the Bill and begin by speaking about some changes that have, in the light of representations, been made about aspects of it that could have given rise to elements of unfairness. A principal change on this category concerns service of notices of assessments for the stripped companies concerned. Under the original Bill, the service or re-issue of a company assessment notice that is a necessary preliminary to a recoupment tax assessment was to be one only of the former owners of the company. That person was the one who could object on the company's behalf. The Government

has now concluded that all former shareholders should be served with the company assessment notice, the only qualification being that the person's whereabouts is known or, in the case of a company, it is not dormant. Further than that, to ensure that the group that can object on the company's behalf is sufficiently representative, the representative group, each of whom is to get a copy of the company assessment, is to include up to five further people who were owners of the company further along a chain of ownership. The representative group, acting by majority, may under the Bill as altered then appoint one of their number to act on its behalf in any contest against the company assessment. The Government believes that this solution meets, within the bounds of practical administration, the point that has been raised.
Another change of broad significance is the offer of limited time to pay to former owners who decide not to contest their liability under the legislation and who make satisfactory arrangements with the Commissioner of Taxation for payment of the unpaid company tax on which their liability to pay recoupment tax would be based. The general background is, of course, that as I have indicated before, the Government hopes that former owners will simply arrange to pay the company tax and the Bill is structured so that any payment of company tax by them will not operate to diminish promoters' liability to the tax in respect of the stripped company concerned. The kind of arrangement for which the Bill makes provision is one where the former owners agree to pay the unpaid company tax in instalments over a period-normally of up to 12 months. They would, at the time of making an arrangement with the Commissioner, need to make an initial payment of part of the tax and, if the arrangement for subsequent payments was adhered to, they would not be liable for any late payment penalty; nor would they be liable to pay recoupment tax.
The provision in the Bill for this time to pay recognises that some persons who will be liable under the legislation may need to dispose of assets and takes into account such things as possible effects on employment that immediate payment of the tax could have. Yet another change in the original Bill will mean that if the underlying company tax liability is contested by some of the former owners of the company, but others wish immediately to pay their share of the evaded tax, they can do so without incurring any liability for late payment penalty that would arise from the delay brought about by the company assessment being put into dispute.

2450 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Taxation (l!npaid Company Tax) Bill

In another area again, we have now concluded that the situation does not warrant the Commissioner of Taxation being specifically authorised to name in his annual report to Parliament persons who do not pay the recoupment tax payable by them. Yet another significant area of note
concerns those situations where companies which have been sold had entered into tax avoidance schemes. The Government's intention has always been that the tax recouped under this Bill should be that tax that remains unpaid as a result of arrangements which have resulted in illegal evasion of tax.
In speaking about this area, let me immediately lay at rest a notion entertained by some that the legislation will recover tax that was escaped only because of the involvement of the companies in such schemes. It will not. The only tax to be recovered under the legislation is tax that was legally payable, and the paym-ent of which has been illegally evaded. This follows from the provisions in the legislation that make recoupment tax payable only where company tax legally payable remains unpaid, former owners having had the opportunity to contest the Commissioner's contention that company tax is payable. If the Commissioner, with full knowledge of the situation, has allowed a deduction created under a tax avoidance scheme, that would conclude the matter and there would now be no claim for tax based on challenge to that scheme.
The Bill as now before the House is set against the background of three broad situations in which companies have been sold and company tax legally payable remains unpaid. The first and most obvious one is a straight out asset strip with a clear intention of leaving the company unable to pay its tax liabilities. Included within that category are those situations where a company is sold after it has been assessed to tax by the Commissioner following his rejection of an earlier avoidance scheme that the company had entered into. A second category is the one where after the sale of the company the company has been put into what purports to be a tax avoidance scheme but it is one that, in substance, is part of an arrangement to render the company unable to pay its tax. The third situation is one where after the sale of the shares the company was also put into an avoidance scheme which could reasonably have been regarded at the time as being effective of its simple tax avoidance purpose.
In relation to each of the second and third categories I note that the Commissioner's action in contesting the legal efficacy of the schemes is not something that he is only now doing. He has had the widest range of avoidance schemes under

challenge and, as I have already indicated, a situation of any possible relevance to the Bill would only arise when, and to the extent that, when the courts do pass judgment on the schemes they find them to fail at law, so that tax is legally payable. The Bill now before the House puts the first and second categories within the scope of the recoupment tax. Each clearly has some element of fraud attaching to the arrangement at some point or another.
The third category is different, as it can fairly be put that, because the avoidance scheme could not be said to be part of the arrangement to leave the company unable to meet its tax liabilities, an element of fraud is not present. Accordingly, there is now specific provision in the Bill to the effect that if the target company was the subject of a post-sale attempted avoidance scheme which, objectively viewed, would have had reasonable prospects of succeeding in its purpose, and the scheme is not part of the stripping arrangement so that the arrangement carried no purpose of rendering the company unable to pay, there will be no recoupment tax liability. In line with that, the recoupment legislation wiII also not apply where there was a pre-sale avoidance scheme which, though ultimately shown to be unsuccessful, might reasonably have been expected to have worked, if at the time of the sale the Commissioner had not raised an assessment rejecting the claims made under the scheme.
Coming to technicalities I mention first one provision that treats for what it is, a sale and purchase of shares, an arrangement under which former owners mortgaged their shares in return for receipt of a loan, and then, as pre-arranged, defaulted in repayment so that the shares were forfeited or otherwise fell into the hands of the promoter. The loan proceeds were of course effectively the sale price of the shares and the legislation will apply accordingly.
Yet another strengthening change is one that concerns the tracing through of recoupment tax liability to former owners and will treat, as involving effective capital distributions from a trust, further variants of schemes to strip funds from trusts. An example is a 'blind trust' in which those who are intended to be beneficiaries are not named as such in the trust instrument but can become beneficiaries in pursuance of the exercise of powers of appointment vested in the trustee or someone else. Yet again, the Bill has been varied so that its basic test of disposal of shares carrying more than 90 per cent of the voting power of the company will be operative in those situations where shares are sold by a person who had formerly exercised control by reason of holding an

Taxation (Unpaid Company Tax) Bill

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2451

office such as that of governing director, and also where the purchaser controls the company by reason of assuming such an office. It has also been found necessary to correct, in relation to companies that have gone out of existence, the test that there had to be an arrangement that rendered the company unable to pay its tax.
That completes my explanation at this stage of the main features of the Bill. More detail will be contained in a revised explanatory memorandum no.w being completed. It will be apparent, I believe, that the Government, after careful consideration of the many points of view that have been put about this legislation, is sticking to it and its basic concepts, whilst making a number of necessary improvements. It will be clear that we have positively responded to reasonable criticisms of the measures. In the same vein, the Government will be willing to consider further amendments of a technical kind which further examination of this somewhat complex Bill shows to be necessary. In that light, I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Willis) adjourned.
TAXATION (UNPAID COMPANY TAX-VENDORS) BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Howard, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (11.28)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill, like the one that I have just spoken about, is a revision of an earlier-introduced Billthe Taxation (Unpaid Company Tax) Bill 1982. That is a Bill that the Government also will not be proceeding with. The present Bill, like the Bill it replaces, will formally impose recoupment tax on former owners of companies stripped of pre-tax profits and does so on the basis of the rules contained in the assessment Bill.
As I have explained, liability for the vendors recoupment tax will fall initially on the persons who sold their shares, but where it is impracticable or inappropriate to levy the tax at that primary level it will be possible to trace through to more remote links in the ownership chain and levy the tax at levels below the primary level. This Bill imposes on the taxable amount-which in the Taxation (Unpaid Company Tax) Assessment Bill is called the vendor's taxable amount-fixed for each former owner a recoupment tax equal to that taxable amount. It is by this means that evaded company tax is to be recovered. I commend the Bill to the House.

Debate (on motion by Mr Willis) adjourned.
TAXATION (UNPAID COMPANY TAX-'-PROMOTERS) BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Howard, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (11.30)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill is to be read with those additional provisions of the main legislation that set out the basis on which persons classed as 'promoters' will be liable to pay as promoters recoupment tax a share of the tax evaded in strips of pre-tax company profits. In speaking to the Taxation (Unpaid Company Tax) Assessment Bill 1982 I have outlined the basis on which this share of evaded tax is to be arrived at. The amount so determined is described in the legislation as a promoters taxable amount and this Bill will formally impose a tax of that amount. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Willis) adjourned.

(II) and (12) Audio cassettes are supplied by the National Library and all sound recordings are returned to the National Library's Oral History Collection.
Video recordings are kept for one month, with the exceptions of news broadcasts which are kept for one week. Tapes are then re-used.
Transcripts are filed by subject into the information files of the Current Information Service. A copy of each transcript is also sent to the National Library together with program notes which are used as a finding aid.
Transcripts are also listed in the Index to Current Information, the bulletin issued by the Alert Service.

Department of Health: Counselling Doctors
(Question No. 4583)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 5 May 1982:
(I) What is the role of counselling doctors attached to his Department.
(2) By what means maya doctor ascertain the correct Medical Benefits Schedule Item to charge for each procedure.
(3) Did his Department allow overpayment from June 1981 to February 1982 to Dr B. Wainer in relation to disputed claims relating to Item 797; if so, why.

2456 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Answers to Questions

(4) What guidelines govern the relationship between the Minister for Health, doctors with his Department and private medical practitioners.
(5) Ifpatient histories are to be seized, is it the practice for this to be done by police officers or by medical practitioners; if not the latter, why not.
(6) Why was the warrant to search Dr Wainer's clinic and home on 19 February 1982 sought in such wide terms.
(7) Were there documents seized in the raid that were not related to Dr Wainer's medical practice; if so, why.
Mr CarIton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The role of a counsellor is to counsel, to advise, to discuss, to clarify and interpret the Medical Benefits Scheme and the Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme. In specific circumstances, a warning may be given regarding possible future reference to a Committee of Inquiry.
(2) By reference to the Medical Benefits Schedule Book. This Book is provided to doctors as the primary means for ascertaining the correct item for a medical service. Each item has a description of the medical service to which it applies.
It also contains explanatory notes for interpretation of the schedule, and on the Medical Benefits Scheme generally. If a doctor is uncertain about the particular item number which applies to a medical service, he should contact the Commonwealth Director of Health in his State.
(3) During that period, benefits were paid in accordance with claims lodged. My Department is reluctant to disadvantage doctors or their patients during a period when the Department is identifying and quantifying any possible breaches of the medical benefits legislation.
(4) The relationship between the Minister and doctors within his Department is the normal Minister-public servant relationship governed by the Public Service Act and Regulations, and the General Orders relating thereto, and amplified in the Official conduct Guidelines for Commonwealth Public Servants issued by the Public Service Board.
The relationship between doctors within the Department and private medical practitioners is governed by those sections of the guidelines referred to above which have regard to the relationship between officers and members of the public, coupled with the relevant sections of the Health Insurance Act and the National Health Act, and other legislation pertaining to the activities of the Department. There are also specific guidelines on action to be taken by a Departmental medical counsellor who becomes aware of a possible breach of the law during a counselling interview, set out in a procedural directive circulated to State Offices of the Department on 9 June 1982.
(5) It is a responsibility of the Australian Federal Police (AFP) to investigate alleged offences against Commonwealth law. The gathering of evidence is part of that role. In each case where a search warrant is sought, the police must satisfy a Stipendiary Magistrate by evidence on oath that sufficient grounds exist to justify the issue of the warrant. In executing the warrant, and in the conduct of the subsequent investigation, procedures have been devised by the AFP in consultation with the Australian Medical Association and my Department to protect the privacy and confidentiality of the patient's medical history, and to prevent unauthorised access to records under police control.
(6) I am advised that the scope of the warrant was appropriate to the suspected offences and complied with guidelines set down by recent decisions of the Victorian Supreme Court. The terms of the warrant were drafted on the advice of the Office of the Deputy Crown Solicitor, Melbourne.

(7) I am advised that only documents relating to Dr Wainer's medical practice were seized.

Public Hospital in Broadmeadows Area, Victoria
(Question No, 4660)
Dr Theophanous asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
(I) Have both the Federal Government and the Victorian State Government acknowledged the need for a public hospital in the area of Broadmeadows.
(2) Has his attention been drawn to the long standing request from the members of the Broadmeadows Hospital Society to transfer the old Commonwealth Serum Laboratory's land in Camp Road, Broadmeadows, to the State of Victoria for the purposes of a community hospital in this region; if so, when can an answer to that request be expected.
(3) Has the Victorian Health Commission made a firm offer to the Department of Administrative Services for the

Camp Road land based on a figure provided by the ValuerGeneral; ifso, when can an answer to that offer be expected.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Matters relating to location and development of health services and facilities, including public hospitals, are the responsibility of the Victorian Government.
(2) and (3) The Department of Administrative Services has provided the following information:
'Negotiations for the sale of part of the former Commonwealth Serum Laboratory's land at Broadmeadows have been continuing between the Commonwealth Department of Administrative Services and the Victorian Health Commission for some time. The Commission did make an offer in December 1980, subject to certain conditions including the availability of funds. Subject to advice from the Commission as to availability of funds in 1982-83, negotiations for the sale of the property to the Commission will be resumed.'
United Nations: Restructuring
(Question No_ 4680)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to President Carter's report on restructuring the United Nnations system submitted to the United States Congress in the 1978 fiscal year (Public Law 95-105).
(2) If so, is he able to say whether the President called for (a) strengthening the UN, (b) a UN Charter Review Conference, if a substantial majority of member states believe it likely to be productive, (c) review and promotion of peaceful dispute settlement measures, (d) yearly Foreign Minister meetings on peace, (e) the International Court of Justice to give advisory opinions to national appellate courts on international laws, (f) wider compulsory jurisdiction for the ICJ, (g) national contingents trained in peace-keeping as a UN reserve force, supported by national airlift facilities and a fund of the order of then $USloom, (h) UN associate membership for very small states, (j) independent revenue sources for the international community and (k) UN agencies to be the major channels for international technical aid.
(3) To which of these principles does the Australian Government subscribe as they apply to Australia.
(4) What has been done to implement each of those principles Australia has accepted.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) (a)-(j) Yes.
(k) This is not a wholly accurate rendition of what was contained in the earlier report. The report called for a strengthening of the role of the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP) as the major channel for voluntary contributions for the technical assistance programs of the UN system.
(3) and (4) The Foreign Affairs Committee of the United States House of Representatives held hearings on this report, but there was no specific follow-up in the United Nations. The recommendations of the report appear to have been left simply for the record, implicitly to be pursued only if and when the climate becomes favourable.
Australia is in broad sympathy with a number of the principles enunciated in the report and spoke accordingly under

2458 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Answers to Questions

relevant individual agenda items in the Sixth (legal) Committee of the UN General Assembly. It is to be regretted that
to date a broad international consensus on reform and restructure of the UN system is lacking, but, as the report notes, until such a consensus develops, it is unlikely that any of those principles will be implemented.

(b) See (a) above.
(c) According to advice given to my Department by specialist professional bodies, there are no clinical indications for such a wide range of pathology tests, particularly on a routine basis.
(d) See (c) above.

National Acoustic Laboratories: Hearing Tests
(Question No. 4724)
Mr Cadman asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 17 August 1982:
(I) How many hearing tests were conducted by the National Acoustic Laboratory, Parramatta, N.S.W., in each of the last 3 years.
(2) What was the total number of staff employed at the Laboratory in each of the same years.
(3) What brands, types and models of aid to each age group have been fitted in each year.
(4) How many persons are on waiting lists for aids in each age group and how long has each been waiting.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1)-
1979-80-5,387 1980-81-5,033 1981-82-3,448.
NOTE: Testings reduced from 1909 in 1980-81 to 498 in 1981-82 in respect of clients to age 21. This resulted from a policy decision late in 1980 that such clients would be screened or treatment-related tested by other agencies before being accepted by National Acoustic Laboratories (NAL).
(2)-
1979-80-12 1980-81-12 1981-82-12.
(3) NAL conducts hearing tests and fits hearing aids to the following groups of clients: persons up to age 21 years, aged and invalid pensioners having health benefits entitlements and their dependants, certain Repatriation and Service pensioners, war widows, Social Security rehabilitees, armed services personnel and recipients of supporting parent's allowance or sickness benefits. At Parramatta hearing centre these groups are fitted from the same range of aids. In the last three years the range has been-

Body-worn aids Behind-the-ear aids
In-the-ear aids

1979-80
Calaids C and G Calaids HL, HH, HM,
P, PE, PH, and RE Calaid E

PE, PH, and RE CalaidE

1981-82
Calaids C and G Calaids HL, HH, PM,
PH, RE and Phonak Calaid E

(4) Clients of the Parramatta hearing centre do not have to
wait for hearing aids. However, eligible pensioners who made their initial applications for hearing tests on 31 August 1982 should have waiting times of about six months before their first appointments. At that date, there were 393 eligible pensioners waiting for their first appointments. Applications for urgent initial appointments are considered when accompanied by evidence that other incapacities make urgent fitting imperative or that clients could suffer severe hardship if aid fitting is delayed.

Clients to age 21 years who are potential hearing aid cases are not required to wait for their first appointments. Conductive clinics are held for others in this grouping (usually those over age 4 years who are unlikely to benefit from a hearing aid fitting).
First appointments are not given to Veterans' Affairs clients at Parramatta hearing centre.

Independent Audit: Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet
(Question No. 4769)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
None: As indicated in answer to Question No. 4795, no grants or payments in the nature of grants, were made by my Department in the 1981-82 financial year to non-government organisations.
Independent Audit: Department of Primary Industry
(Question No. 4776)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The conditions for funds administered by the Department of Primary Industry and granted to non-government organisations require that unless the grantee organisation's accounting records are required by statute to be audited by the Commonwealth Auditor-General or the Auditor-General of a State, the grantee must furnish a statement of the receipts and payments on the project during the financial year to which the grant relates. The statement must be accompanied by an audit certificate given by a qualified auditor who is not a member, employee, or public officer of the grantee organisation.

Independent Audit: Department of Social

.

Security

(Question No. 4780)

Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice,
on 18 August 1982:

What independent audit is required by the Minister's Department to ensure the proper expenditure of funds granted to non-government organisations by the Minister or the Minister's Department.

Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honour-
able member's question:

With reference to the provisions of Finance Directions issued under the authority of the Audit Act 1901, grants administered by the Department of Social Security whether or
not covered by specific legislation are made subject to the condition that evidence is to be provided to show that the pur-

poses for which the grant was made are being met. Organisations are required to produce statements of expenditure on those purposes, supported by audit certificates. Audit certificates are required to be given by a qualified public accountant who is not an officer of the organisation.
Organisations receiving general support grants, as distinct from grants for specific purposes, are required to supply copies of their audited financial statements covering the periods for which the grants were made.

Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2) No grants, or payments in the nature of grants, were made by my Department in the 1981-82 financial year to non-government organisations.
Service Guards of Honour
(Question No. 4821)
Mr Leo McLeay asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:

Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Guards of Honour are accorded HM The Queen and members of the Royal Family on both formal and informal occasions.
Guards of Honour are provided for distinguished personages to whom such an honour is appropriate (as indicated by the Department of Prime Minister and Cabinet) and for other entitled persons for formal visits and ceremonies of State. These other entitled persons include:
a. A Foreign Sovereign;
b. The Head of State of a foreign country;
c. An ambassador presenting credentials;
d. State Governors and Administrator of the Northern Territory; and
e. Australian and foreign senior Service officers.

Fluoride: Renal Effects
(Question No. 4858)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 18 August 1982:
(I) Is he able to say whether A. L. Maduska and others

(Am. J. Obstet, Gynecol., 130,84, (980) have evidence that maternal fluoride levels are occasionally above 50 micromol/L with the usual exposure to methoxyflurane and with high fetal to maternal flouride ratio.
(2) Do adults vary in susceptibility to high-output renal failure with high blood fluoride.

Answers to Questions

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2461

(3) Have studies been done to suggest renal damage in the newborn from exogenous flouride.
(4) Is he able to say whether the decision to ban flouride supplements in pregnancy in New York was due solely to a belief that these supplements were ineffective: if so, what other examples of such bans of medication solely on the grounds of ineffectiveness are known: if not, on what evidence were the other grounds for the ban based.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) In their study on pregnant ewes (Am. J. Obslel. Gynecol. 136, 84, 1980), Maduska and others did report serum inorganic fluoride levels above 50 micromol per litre, compared to a mean maternal baseline of 13.3 micromol. This rise in serum level followed the administration intravenously of a large quantity of sodium fluoride-20 ml of a solution of 100 parts per million were rapidly introduced. In the study, the ewes were not exposed to methoxyflurane. The anaesthetic administered was halothane, a stable fluroine compound. The study was designed to measure the effect of sodium fluoride-not fluoride released from methoxyflurane---on maternal and fetal serum flouride levels.
(2) Yes. The presence of existing kidney disease and its degree of severity, obesity and the PH of the urine are among the factors influencing individual susceptibility to this classification of renal failure.
(3) No studies have been reported in the scientific literature concerning renal damage in the newborn from exogenous fluoride.
(4) The 'ban' imposed by the United States Food and Drug Administration in 1966 in New York and other States was a 'ban' on the advertising and promotion of sodium fluoride supplements in respect of claims for efficacy. It was not a 'ban' on the use of these supplements. In the United States, 'bans' on advertisements, which claimed an effect not proven, have been imposed for many years. The 'bans' apply to many other readily available substances, including other dietary supplements, analgesics and mouth-rinses.
Aircraft Noise: Brisbane Airport
(Question No. 4874)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Aviation, upon notic~, on 19 August 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the National Acoustic Laboratories and Commonwealth Department of Health Report 'Aircraft Noise in Australia: A survey of community reaction' which recommended that the new NEF index value of 20 be used to indicate areas having 'an excessive amount of aircraft noise' on maps showing aircraft noise exposure around airports.

(2) If so, when will maps showing aircraft noise exposure based on this index value around (a) Brisbane airport and (b) Brisbane's new international airport be available.
(3) What publicity will be given to their release.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable members's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.

They would also be included in public displays, as appropriate, of the airport redevelopment, and at the public information centre on the new airport site.

The comprehensive detail sought by the honourable member is not available. Details of satellites tracked from or otherwise utilised by ground stations in Australia operated by Defence or in cooperation with Defence are as follows. No information is provided in respect of classified satellite programs.

Pensioner Health Benefit Concession Cards
(Question No. 4926)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 24 August 1982:
(I) Is a computer letter sent to all pensioners who are no longer entitled to retain their health benefit card or concession card.
(2) Does the letter advise pensioners to check with the State health authority to see if they can still receive free hospital services; if so, as Queensland has a free hospital system, should this part of the letter be censored for Queensland pensioners as it may cause them alarm.

Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Yes. A letter is automatically produced by computer and forwarded to pensioners who are no longer entitled to retain their health benefit card or concession card requesting the cards be returned.
(2) Yes. The text of the letter does advise the pensioner to check with the State Health Authority.
It is considered desirable for the pensioner to do so, so that he is fully aware of the situation applicable to him. The arrangements for the provision of full medical and hospital care to holders of Commonwealth health benefits cards are different from the arrangements existing in State hospitals, particularly where the pensioner has been receiving treatment by a private medical practitioner.

Non-aligned Moyement Summit Meeting: Australian Observers
(Question No, 4960)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 25 August 1982:
(I) Having regard to Australia's increasing involvement in the Middle East, and the size of the membership of the NonAligned Movement (NAM), what consideration did the Government give to sending a (a) Government and (b) Parliamentary, representative to the 7th NAM summit meeting to be held in Baghdad, 6-10 September 1982.
(2) Will any official Australian observers be present; if so (a) who are they, (b) what is their status and (c) to whom will they report.
(3) If no Australian observers will be present; why not.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Government gave no consideration to sending a Government representative to the 7th Non-Aligned Conference of Heads of State. The question of attendance by a representative of the Parliament would of course be a matter for Parliament itself. In any event, the meeting has been postponed and is likely to be transferred to another venue.
(2) and (3) Australia is not a member of the Non-Aligned Movement. It is not the normal practice of the Non-Aligned Movement to extend invitations to aligned countries to attend NAM meetings, whether as Observers or Guests.

(8) There are two components to the statistical reporting system at the national level:
(a) Workload Statistics~The workload statistics provide information on the operations of the centres by measuring the major activities conducted by the staff. This includes identifying the type of counselling (i.e., counselling new clients, telephone counselling, casual contacts) and other non-client related activities (e.g. staff development) conducted in the centres. The indicators in this system are the number of contacts and time spent on each activity.
(b) Client-Specific Data Base~This data base provides basic statistical information about the clients, the problems they are experiencing, patterns of utilisation of the centres and counselling outcomes.
As the veteran is considered to be the prime client and the system is veteran oriented, data collected on this system pertain to veterans.
The information is divided into~
(i) Veterans' details~including sex, marital status, number of children and Repatriation details;
(ii) service details~including the Branch of Service and details of tours of duty in Vietnam;
(iii) contact details~including with whom the contact was made (i.e., veteran, spouse) and the problems involved;
(iv) type of counselling~individual, group or family; and
(v) referral information~including the agency to which referred (i.e., Local Government, non-Government, voluntary) and the reason for referral (e.g., legal, drug/alcohol, employment).
(9) Statistics on the V.V.C.S., as on most other operations, are collected on a four-weekly cost period basis. This enables consistent comparisons to be made between different periods.
Statistics are also collected in terms of client contacts and tables giving these are set out below. Definitions of the terms used in these tables are as follows:
Client: for unique identification purposes the prime client is the veteran, although contacts may be with veteran and spouse/others. References to 'client' refer to the veteran or any family or other associate.
New Client Contact: the first contact at the Centre by a client, after which contact the client is registered (i.e. at the time of contact no registered client file number is held at the Centre).
Past Client Contact: a second or subsequent contact at the Centre by a registered client (i.e. a client file is held at the Centre).
Casual Client Contact: (i) an initial contact by a client after which contact the client is not registered. This could include contacts made on country visits.
(ii) a contact by a client, either registered or unregistered, which is not part of a specific counselling plan (i.e. in the case of a registered client a contact which is not part of a Counsellor's course of treatment and no record is made of the contact on the file) e.g. attendance at a self-help group.

2466 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Answers to Questions

Outside Contact: a contact with a registered client outside the Centre, e.g. home visit, hospital visit.
If a contact is made outside the Centre with an unregistered client this is included as a casual client contact.
Telephone Contact: a contact by telephone with either a registered or unregistered client. Telephone contacts are included only if they involve the provision of a professional

service by the Counsellor. Appointment making and discussions about clients are not included. [n the case of a registered client the client's file mayor may not be noted.
The number of clients registered with each Centre for each period is given by the figure in the column "New Clients".

(10) As at 17 August 1982: New South Wales 55; Victoria 13; Queensland 27; South Australia 16; Western Australia 12; Tasmania 28; Northern Territory I.

They would be assisted through training, discussion, etc., to cope with veterans' needs.

(II) The following measures to cater for country clients either exist or are proposed:
(i) establishment of regional offices where distances from the capital city and concentration of clients warrant it. Townsville and Canberra have been suggested as sites.
(ii) acceptance by the existing Centres of reverse charge telephone calls from country clients-this already operates.
(iii) visits to country centres by V.V.C.S. Counsellors.
(iv) development of a network of counsellors and services in country areas to which veterans could be referred.

(12) No records are kept in a form which would permit this question, in the way in which it has been asked, to be answered precisely. [ draw attention to my statement to the Senate on 25 August 1982 in which [ said: 'Broadly speaking the most common problem, occurring in 26 per cent of cases, ';s family problems embracing both marital and parent/child relationships. Next, at 25 per cent are what might be termed behavioural problems, covering such things as violent behaviour, withdrawal, irritability or moodiness, insomnia, bad dreams and flashbacks, and depression. Finally, 22 per cent of cases have reported problems of a social nature such as housing and accommodation, employment and finance.'
(13) All advertising of this nature is arranged through the Australian Government Advertising Service which has provided the following information:

(14) No.
(15) The V.V.C.S. is subject to continuing review on a dayto-day basis by its Director through such mechanisms as the statistical reporting system described in (8) above, the normal financial and staff reporting systems and by contact and visits by the Director to the various Centres. The Director in turn reports through the normal Departmental channels to me and

$

$

1360.00
through a Commissioner of the Repatriation Commission to the National Advisory Committee of the Vietnam Veterans Counselling Service, of which the Commissioner is Chairman, on major issues of a developmental or policy nature. The National Advisory Committee can make recommendations to me.
In the longer term, probably at the completion of a full year of operation of all the existing Centres, it is intended to carry out an evaluation of the Service embracing such aspects as statistics, professional aspects, client response and satisfaction, administration and finance.
(16) None. The records of the V. V.C.S. are confidential and are not available to the Department without the agreement of the client.
(17) The Department has information on a geographic basis for Vietnam veterans in receipt of pensions. This covers about 6,900 veterans. For the total Vietnam veteran population, the Department has used estimates provided by the Australian Bureau of Statistics, based on a sample survey carried out at the end of 1979. Some estimates were below the levels normally released by the Bureau and they were provided to the Department on the basis that they would not be released outside the Department.
(18) No, with the possible exception of Dr R. T. Anderson, Psychiatrist-in-Charge of the Elizabeth Street Clinic in Melbourne, who is a member of the National Advisory Committee. Clients of the V.V.C.S. can be referred as necessary to the full range of medical and counselling services available in the community either directly by the Counselling Service or through the Department's facilities.
Injuries and Accidents in Federal Government Departments
(Question No. 5002)
Dr Jenkins asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 7 September 1982:
(1) How many injuries and accidents occurred in each Federal Government Department for each year since 1977 to the date of preparation of the answer to this question.
(2) What is the cost of these injuries and accidents.
(3) As a result of these work related injuries and accidents:
(a) how many man weeks were lost,
(b) what amounts were paid in workers' compensation, and
(c) what were the effects on each Department's productively as a result of time lost by employees in each of the last 5 years.
Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1), (2) and (3) (b) Federal Government injury statistics are shown in Table 1 (Consolidation and for each department/authority (Tables 2 to 6) including number of cases and cost covering the years 1976-77 to 1980-8\.
(3) (a) Average man/weeks for both total and partial incapacity cases are shown in Table 7 for 1979-80 and 1980-8 I-that is, since such records have been kept.

2470 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Answers to Questions

(3) (c) The information is not available.
Note: The sources of the statistical tables provided are the Annual Reports of the Commissioner for Employees' Compensation for the relevant years. Numbers of cases and costs for 1981-82 will be available in the Commissioner's Report when tabled.

Note I Item I relates to the number of cases involving injury, accident or disease in respect of which a determination of initial acceptance of liability has been made since the commencement of the financial year and in respect of which at least one payment has been made in that time.
Item 2 relates to the number of cases involving injury, accident or disease in respect of which a determination of initial acceptance of liability was made before the commencement of the financial year and in respect of which at least one payment has been made during the financial year.
Note 2 Although sub-section 122 (I) of the Act refers only to injuries, it should be noted that the above figures, for each financial year, include cases of compensable diseases. This follows from the operation of sub-section 29 (2) of the Act whereby a disease which is compensable under the Act is, for the purposes of the application of the benefit provisions of the Act, deemed to be a personal injury to the employee. The figures also include those cases where the employee was involved in an accident arising out of and in the course of his employment and the accident resulted in the loss of, or damage to, an artificial aid or appliance, but did not cause personal injury (section 28).

Social Welfare: Mobility Allowance
(Question No. 5010)
Dr Jenkins asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 7 September 1982:
(I) What are the eligibility criteria the Minister is using to define 'a severely handicapped person' as qualification for the new Mobility Allowance.
(2) Why is the implementation of the scheme being delayed until April 1983.
Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The detailed conditions of eligibility for the new Mobili~ Allowance are currently under consideration by my department. When these are finalised I will make a further statement on the matter.

(2) Considerable work is involved in setting up the Mobility Allowance assessment and payment mechanisms and procedures. It is anticipated that April 1983 will be the earliest practical target date for implementing the scheme.
Disabled Persons: Attendant Care Scheme
(Question No. 5011)
Dr Jenkins asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 7 September 1982:
Has the Government made provision in 1982-83 for funding an Attendant Care Scheme for disabled persons to enable them to live in private accommodation of their choice, if so:
(a) what is the amount of the grant,
(b) how will the grant be administered,
(c) when will the scheme be implemented,
(d) how many disabled people will be assisted by the scheme,and
(e) have intended recipients been consulted regarding the operation of the scheme.
Mr Wilson-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
Provision has been made in 1982-83, for the conduct of a pilot attendant care scheme designed to examine the feasibility of alternative modes of providing attendant care assistance.
(a) A total of $100,000 has been provided to conduct the pilot during 1982-83.
(b) These funds will be administered through the Department of Social Security.
(c) The pilot is expected to commence in March 1983.
(d) My department is currently developing proposals for the conduct of the pilot for my consideration. The number of disabled persons to be included in the pilot will be considered in this context.
(e) Potential participants in the pilot have not yet been identified.
Stockpile of Explosive Power
(Question No. 5022)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 8 September 1982:
(I) Is he able to give estimates of the total explosive power used in (a) military action in Indo-China in the period of involvement of United States of America and Australian Forces, (b) World War land (c) World War II.
(2) Is he able to say what is the estimated explosive power now stockpiled by the permanent members of the (a) United Nations Security Council, (b) Northern Atlantic Treaty Organisationand (c) Warsaw Pact.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) It is not possible to estimate with any degree of certainty the explosive power used in the conflicts listed in the first part of the question; nor is it possible to estimate the explosive power of stockpiles of conventional and nuclear weapons at present held by the countries mentioned. A report on nuclear weapons, which was submitted by the Secretary General of the United Nations to the Thirty-fifth Session of the United Nations General Assembly on 12 September 1980 (UN Document A/35/392), noted that while the exact

2478 REPRESENTATIVES 21 October 1982

Answers to Questions

number of nuclear warheads in the world at that time was unknown, the total was estimated to be in excess of 40,000, with a strength probably equivalent of some 13,000 million tons of TNT.

Chemical Defence Experiments
(Question No. 5028)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 8 September 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the allegation by Dr R. Watson, Director, Chemical Defence Experimental Establishment, Porton Down, United Kingdom, that Australia's main contribution to the 1965 Technical Co-operation Program agreement was for the testing of equipment developed in Britain' and the United States of America, as reported in the book A Higher Form of Killing, R. Harris and J. Paxman, published by Chatto and Windus, 1982, page 175.
(2) Can he confirm or deny this allegation.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes, but I am uncertain whether these are Dr Watson's words or interpretations made by the authors of the book concerned, based upon their interview with him. The Technical Co-operation Program comprehends exchanges of results of research and development in many subjects in defence science and in maintaining the knowledge of the state-of-art.
The Government would be negligent if it did not take the necessary steps to develop its defences against the threat posed by chemical agents; accordingly exchanges and crosstesting of protective clothing and detection equipment have taken place between Australia and other countries. However I am advised no large-scale field testing of chemical warfare equipment or agents has taken place in Australia since World War II.
(2) The allegation is untrue.

Wheat Sales to The USSR
(Question No. 5039)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 9 September 1982:
(I) What have been Australia's wheat sales to the USSR in each year since 1977.
(2) Is there a potential for sale of canned fruit to the USSR; if so, why has this sale not proceeded.
(3) If it is government policy which has caused this sale not to proceed, is the Government intending to compensate the canned fruit industry.
Mr Nixon-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Shipments of Australian wheat in each year since 1976-77 under sales negotiated with USSR are as follows:

Year

Tonnes

(December-November) 1976-77 1977-78 1978-79 1979-80 1980-81 1981-82 (to 18 September 1982)

944,068 446,701 1,253,267 3,270,790 1,735,580 2,047,405

(2) Australia has never exported canned fruit to the USSR and all information gathered by Government sources strongly suggests that future sales prospects are very limited. The value of canned and bottled fruit imports by the USSR has ranged from around $3.1 m t'o $4.7 m per annum over the period 1976 to 1981, and virtually all these imports have come from Bulgaria and Hungary who would have the capacity to substantially expand sales to the USSR if the demand arose in future. Moreover, the USSR's current situation of 'hard' currency shortage would be likely to preclude at this stage the possibility of the USSR seeking to augment supplies from traditional Socialist country sources with purchases from developed Western countries.
(3) The Government's policy towards the USSR provides no impediment to the sale of canned fruit on normal commercial terms.

Visits of Nuclear Powered Warships
(Question No. 5052)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 14 September 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the 1976 report on environmental considerations of visits of nuclear powered warships to Australia which recommends the establishment of a Naval Nuclear Ship Safety Organisation in each port to be visited by nuclear powered warships.
(2) Have these organisations been established; if so (a) in which ports and (b) in what years were they established.
(3) What steps were taken by the Commonwealth to ensure that such organisations were established.
(4) Do these organisations conform in detail to the structure and functioning laid out in annex C, paragraphs 43-58, and appendix 2 of the annex of the report; if not, where and in what ways do they vary from the guidelines.
(5) What assistance has been sought by, and offered to, State governments in establishing these organisations and

Answers to Questions

21 October 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 2479

from which Commonwealth Departments and for what purposes has this assistance come.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes. Port safety organisations were set up in Melbourne, Hobart and Fremantle in 1976, Darwin in 1978 and Brisbane in 1982.
(3) Consultations took place between Commonwealth and State officials and the organisations were set up with the full co-operation of the State Governments concerned.
(4) Yes.
(5) Assistance has mainly consisted of the provision of technical advice, manpower for radiation monitoring programs, and radiation monitoring equipment. In addition the Commonwealth has carried out analysis of environmental samples as part of the routine environmental monitoring program. Commonwealth authorities involved have been Defence, Home Affairs and Environment, Health and the Australian Atomic Energy Commission.

Concord Repatriation Hospital: Operating Theatres
(Question No. 5206)
Mr Maher asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 23 September 1982:
(I) When were the operating theatres at Concord Repatriation Hospital last refurbished and renovated.

(2) What action is taken to minimise the risk of contamination and cross infection due to lack of separate 'clean' and 'dirty' traffic ways in and out of each theatre.
(3) When will the theatres be modernised and refurbished.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Repatriation General Hospital, Concord, has seven operating theatres located in two separate areas of the hospital. Three theatres are located on the seventh floor of the multi-storey building and were constructed in the I 940s. Four theatres are located in the administration building and were built in 1971.
A continuing repairs and maintenance program is undertaken in both areas to ensure the standards appropriate for modern surgical procedures are maintained. The maintenance of sterile conditions is emphasised and a regular replacement program for air-conditioning filters and waste lines leading from the theatres is undertaken. Examples of recent major repairs and maintenance work on the operating theatres include-
1977-78-$15,000 to repair ceiling and walls of the theatre in the administrative building.
1979-80-$16,000 to repair and replace floor coverings in the theatres in the multi-storey building.
1982-83-$35,000 to paint and carry out minor repairs in the theatres in the administrative building.
(2) In the administrative building theatres 'dirty' or used instruments are enclosed on trolleys in protective coverings at the end of each surgical procedure and disposed of by way of a lifej'dumb waiter' connected to the dirty area of the Theatre Sterilising unit. This disposal procedure ensures that dirty material leaves the operating theatres by a route separate from that taken by the patient. All 'dirty' linen is sealed in protective bags prior to leaving the operating room. Disposal of used materials in this manner minimises the risk of cross infection.
In the other theatres, all used equipment and instrumentation are cleaned in a wash steriliser unit before leaving the clean-up area located between the operating rooms. After cleaning, instruments are transferred to the central sterile unit for re-sterilisation.
Regular microbiological tests of the air, water and equipment are conducted in both operating suites, the results of which are monitored by the hospital's infection control committee. The hospital has not experienced any significant instances of cross-infection or contamination in either operating suite.
(3) Plans to upgrade and relocate the operating theatre services at the hospital are presently under review by the Department of Veterans' Affairs with a view to bringing forward a proposal for Government consideration.

Answering of Questions on Notice: Minister for Education
(Question No. 4946)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 25 August 1982:
(I) What are the details of the Minister's arrangements for the answering of questions on notice, specifying what officers (a) on the Minister's personal staff and (b) within the Minister's Department, are responsible for (i) examining the question asked and (ii) preparing the answers.
(2) How many questions on notice, from senators and members, has .the Minister been asked from 25 November 1980 to date.
(3) What is the average delay incurred between the time of questions being placed on notice and the provision of the answer.
(4) For each question referred to in part (2), how many have taken (a) 1-3 months, (b) 3-6 months, (c) 6-9 months, (d) 9-12 months and (e) over 12 months, to be answered.

(5) How many of these questions are currently still on

notice, and what is the reason for delay in the case of each

question which has been on the Notice Paper of either House

for 12 months or more.

.

Mr Fife-The Minister for Education has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(I) In the Parliamentary Liaison and Public Relations Branch of my Department a Clerk Class 8 working directly to the Assistant Secretary (Level I) of the Branch examines questions on notice and directs them for the preparation of draft replies to the appropriate functional areas in the Depart-
ment and/or other agencies within the portfolio. The draft replies prepared are cleared by senior officers in the areas and agencies concerned. Before final clearance to my Office, the officer responsible also arranges for the draft reply to be examined by the Assistant Secretary, Parliamentary Liaison and Public Relations Branch and by either the Deputy Secretary or Secretary of the Department.

Once received in my Office the proposed reply is examined by my Senior Private Secretary before submission to me fot
final approval. My Assistant Private Secretary facilitates the formalities oflodgement of the reply.
